take it back, and ask for—

That other brands deceive them

But oft my thoughts again repeat,
That BATHOS’ Powder is the best,
And I know it can’t be beat.”

“THE BULLETIN.”
(Published at Sydney, N.S. \V.)
Tlie Unique Weekly—Australia’s Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper.
“The Bulletin,”
Premier Australian Literary Journal,
Is the only paper which has a consistent and dis-
tinctive Australian National Policy.
This Bulletin favours—
'A Republican Form of Government.
One Person, one Vote.
Comnlete Secularisation and Freedom of State
Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
Australia for the Australians.—The cheap
Chinaman, the cheap nigger, and the
cheap European pauper to be absolutely
excluded,
A State Bank, the issue of bank-notes to be a
State monopoly.
The direct election of Ministers by Parlia-
ment, instead of Party Government or
rather Government by Contradiction.
A new Parliamentary System one House to
be elected by constituencies as at present;
the other to be chosen by the whole
country voting as one Constituency.
A Universal System of Compulsory Life
Insurance.
The entire Abolition of the Private Owner-
ship of Land.
The Referendum.
The Abolition of Titles of so-called “nobility. ’
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
m The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter that is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started 13 years ago with no capital but brains,
and has become the representative Australian
journal, because it possesses a vitality lacked
by the countless newspaper-ventures in which
Australian capitalists have unavailingly spent
large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after th
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. I hey are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
Form printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever stm.
The Bulletin is the only well-established A us-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress: it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege— the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the cla’ins of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, ae-ainst the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected witli any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushtnan of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the Maoriland
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based.
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any postal official—that
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and moat
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

Tonsorial Diagnosis.—A Story for
Surgeons only.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
I hate the smell of iodoform, and that s
why I used rather to avoid my friend Barker
Browne. Poor fellow, he died yesterday had
fairly wasted away. .
The doctor told me 8.8. died of an affection of
the tonsils.
* * •
I took my usual shave this morning at my
usual barber’s. Confound it! The place reeked
with iodoform.
“ I see your friend Mr. Barker Browne is dead
—lather—“ poor feller !” —scrape —“ I shaved him
when he was bad” —tickle —‘‘and I shaved the
corpse this morning shaving-brush all oyer my
nostrils. “Doctor said he was consumptive ’
blob !—“but I didn’t believe him”— taking in the
slack skin of my neck —“a man with the roof of
his mouth gone ” —gash ! —“ centreboard of his
nose absent ” —squirt!—“ hovy do you like this
razor, sir? I only broke it in this morning ”
dry rub—“.no, sir ; he couldn’t kid me ” —perfume
—“ thank you, sir; good morning, sir.”
I shave myself now.
CLOSE SHAVE.
“The Wombat” writes, taking Henry
Lawson severely to task for some of his bitter
utterances in “ Popular Australian Mistakes,
rebuking him for his ignorance respecting
“ mirages,” “ deserts,” “ mountains,” &c., and
giving geographical facts that tumble up the poet-
joker j—•
Tae manager is not called the “super ” now, but he
was in the old days ; and H.L. is w rong in saying he is
called the “ overseer ; ” he”is usually called “ the boss,
and rarely referred to as the overseer. A swag is gener-
ally called “ bluey ; ” then comes “swag,” and lastly
“ Matilda,” but the latter term is seldom used out
west. During 17 years’ experience out back, I have
never yet, to my recollection, once heard one
man ask another to “ have some tucker, mate
the generally - favoured mode of invitation is
“ Better come and have a feed, mate,” or “ Will you have
a drink of tea, mate?” Swags are carried all ways, and
any half-dozen travellers met promiscuously would pro-
bably be found each to be carrying his swag differently.
The "horse-collar” is the most uncomfortable, and, con-
sequently, the least-used style ; the “strap over one shoul-
der” style is most used. For general ease and comfort, I
recommend the knapsack style. The term, “nose-bag” is
not used out-back, but is confined to the farming and
settled districts ; out west the bags are “ tucker-bags,
or “ ration-bags ” —generally the former. The shearing-
shed (as H.L. truthfully says) it the most degrading hell
on the face of the earth. Of “ the poetical bushman and
the Australian blackfellow ” I may have something to
say on some future date. If “Jim doesn't tell pathetic
yarns in bad doggerel jn the shearers’ hut," why is H.L.
eternally making other men do what he says “Jim
doesn’t do?
Another of those unfinished yarns about
the royal family in which the soul of the loyal
Bull delighteth is going round the English papers.
Some time ago, it seems, Princess Maud of Wales
visited a French watering - place incognito—
which means that she was alias Smith or some-
thing like that. For a while everything went
well, but the princess and her attendant gorgon
began to notice that a young man was evidently
“ mashed ”on the former lady. At all events he
raised his hat to her on two occasions, and on the
second occasion the gorgon with much difficulty
restrained herself from shrieking for the police.
Finally, however, the young man filled up the
measure of his iniquity by leaving a basket of fresh
spring flowers for the nice-looking girl who was
alias Smith. Then the gorgon sent for him and
fixed her spectacles on him, and shook her mittens
at hiqi threateningly. “ Sir,” said she, in a deep
and awful voice, “ your intrusive and ill-bred be-
haviour compels me to betray our incognito. The
lady whom you have taken the liberty of address-
ing is the Princess Maud of Wales.” Then the
story ends abruptly like all the other stories of
outrages upon the royal family. That is the un-
satisfactory feature about them —you never know
what becomes of the wilted victim. The presump-
tion is that he crawls away and dies miserably, or
that his reason gets off its throne with the shock
and he is now a helpless lunatic in an asylum ; or
he may have taken an attic somewhere, and started
business as a hermit vith white flowing hair
and toe-nails of immense length, and have his vic-
tuals thrown in to him by his landlady because he
has taken a vow never again to be seen of men.
It, is all a matter of conjecture, for the story never
finishes definitely.
Colemane & Sons’ Eucalypte Ex. is the strongest.
Editor Bulletin, —Be Bushranger Power.
About seven years ago, a most respectable old
party, judging by the black clothes, cravat, and
top-hat, set off by a Parkesian wealth of con-
trasting white hair and beard, came to Melb.
Hospital, suffering from a painful bladder-
affection. Before being relieved he begged for
chloroform, and during its administration at
first was quiet, till he felt King Ohio,
getting the upper hand, when a fierce battle
took ipi ace. Wild scenes of the past were acted
over again for our benefit (in chloroformo veritas,
now being considered not quite a lie—happy
thought! why not chloroform De Jong ?) till those
around began to wonder who the respectable old
party could be. Next day it was found that he
was Power. In the wards he complained of the
crowd and the confinement, and begged for the
privilege of erecting a tent in the grounds where
he could live or die in peace. To the writer he
confided that the real cause of his being run to
earth was the affection under treatment, which
had harassed him for many years, and this some-
what bears out your correspondent, for the
popular remedy for such maladies is gin, and
often much gin. Shortly before the old highway-
man’s disappearance he called again about a
troublesome molar tooth, which he carried away
carefully in his pocket, to the disappointment of
the writer, who had reckoned upon adding it to
the historical relics of this tradition-lacking
country. If Power went out under the Murray
waters as he went off under chloroform he died
hard.—A Geopkk. 27/11/’93.

PRICK Is.
Tom Perrot.
[ FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Something less than 15 years ago, the old
Rob Roy, with her consort, the'Susan, was on
the Murrumbidgee, a day or two’s distance from
Swan Hill, bound for the lower river, laden with
piles.
some little time previously, a Swede hail gob
ids foot jammed between the tow-rope and the bit.
He bellowed so lustily that the boat was sent
astern and his foot was released before too much
injury was done. On that occasion, Perrot sung
out: “ Stow yer bellowing, you baby"; you
couldn’t make more fuss if I was shutting my
fists.” The Swede sulkily muttered —“ It’ll be
your turn next, and see how you’ll like it.”
On this occasion, the mooring lines had been let
go, the plank taken in, the signal go-ahead rung
out. Suddenly Perrot, standing in a loose coil of
the tow-line, was drawn rapidly three times round
the bit, once round the stem-head, and into the
river. A gallant rescue of his mangled body was
effected. All about the fore-deck, and for a score
of feet along the piles, blood lay coagulating ;
thickened here and there with bits of splintered
bone and quivering flesh, pieces of which even
were afterwards found in the bargeman’s “ tucker-
Can.”
Perrot, with legs literally torn from the hip-
sockets, with only the sinews connecting with the
trunk, was laid in the Rob Roy’s cabin. When
he heard a proposition made to take him back
with the boat, single-handed, to Swan Hill, he
said, in a surprisingly fir-m voice—“No lads;
stand by, all of you. I want you all to see the
last of me. It won’t be long. Skipper, don’t
move the boat; there’s nothing can be done for
me. But I ain’t agoing to sing out.”
Holding a mate’s hand in each of his own, Tom
Peraot “slipped his lines,” grit to the last.
* * *
A butcher-boy who was once going from
Wilcannia by the Pilot, “ home to see his mother,”
also got foul of the tow-rope, and had his foot
taken clean off. He was got ashore, and when he
saw the foot, boot and all, drifting about in the
slack water, demanded that it should be restored
to him. “Cos it’s my property,” he said.
B. R. W.
The How-we-beat-the-favourite
Affliction.
( FOR THE BULLETIN. )
It started at first in the brains of one Gordon
.*.(He blew them out after and somewhat atoned);
Each ass is his echo—encoring—encored on—
A plague on posterity, never condoned.
All ages, from infancy up to virility,
Record how the ring-men were yelling, “Dead
heat! ’*
The tremulous voicing of gummy senility
Out-gibbers—“ And that’s how the fav’rite was
beat I”
Attend at a concert, if you are aesthetic,
To soften your spirits, hr bolster them up ;
It’s twenty to one you will get that emetio
Worn-to-rags lay of the Loamshire Hunt Cup.
Soprano sings first of the “Scenes that are
brightest; ”
By bassoyoulre rooked on the waves of the deep;
The tenor’s voice trills, in sweet notes of the
lightest;
Contralto attunes you to languorous sleep.
Bat, next, you’re aroused by a din of hoarse
cheering,
A wide, scarlet nostril flits close to your knees;
Some donkey is on to the platform careering,
And, hopeless, you see that he’s got the disease.
And then you hear all the old fatuous drivel —
How he was left sailing in front of them all;
Disgusted, you pick up your girl, and locked level
Discarding all eunning, you race from the hall.
It’a,the same at all socials and picnics and parties,
Dissenting, or Roman, or Orangeman’s Pup—
You’re told by the Atkinses, Dugalds, and
Flahertys,
“Be calm: and they think you may just win
the Cup.”
One sentiment only in which you can revel,
On searching the dirge from its finish to start,
Is the short fervent prayer that is muttered by
Neville,
It wakens an echo in every heart.
We’ve beared it recited aboard of the Cuzco ;
The boys of “ the Never ” can’t let it go by ;
From the loftiest summit of Mount Kosciusko
Its lines have been yelled to the suffering sky.
It’s poisoned the life of the old sage of Ha warden’
He’s sharpening his axe, and he’s pickling his
rod,
For he’s going to brain or to castigate Gordon—
He doesn’t know yet Gordon’s under the sod.
I’m weak andTm limp; I am weary and ailing;
I’ll never enjoy the same vigour again ;
But—“ Giles, on the Greyling, came down at the
paling ” —
I fear, by my soul! that I’m going insane.
“ Keep back on The Heifer; lie by on The
Mullock;
Back you, sir, on Fig-leaves ; sit still on The
Hag;
Slide down on The Rainbow ; turn round on The
Bullock ;
So, steady there—easy—and up went the rag.’’
May The Clown, at the stud, prove completely
* defective,
And drift, at the last, to the shafts of a cart;
May Iseult, with pain, feel the “ damns ” and
invective
Of thousands of sufferers gnaw at her heart.
May the lampers annoy them, the glanders attack
them,
The heaves and bronchitis e’er torture their
breath,
May greasy heels lame them, and thoroughpin
rack them.
And spavins and bota bring them down to their
death.
North Melbourne.
N. M. O’DONNELL.
According to Yic. railway regulations,
the members of Speight’s household were entitled
to “ Occasional passes to persons constantly form-
ing part of an olncer’s family, not exceeding six
in each year.” “Ours would not work out to
that number,” said Speight in the witness-box.
As a matter of fact, Speight’s large family did
very little free travelling in the whole course of
his nine years’ reign, and his daughters used to
bemoan the fact that “Pa ” wouldn’t get passes
for them.

SATURDAY, DECEMBER 23, 1893.
The N.S.W. Deadlock.
A few days ago the cumbersome machine of
Party Government broke down badly in New
South Wales, and that province is now
without a Parliament, and practically with-
out a Government. Also it is without a
people so far as concerns the purposes of the
Electoral Act; for the old constituencies
have been abolished, and the new ones are
not yet in working oi’der, therefore these is
no power which can create a new Legislature
or a new Government. This curious dead-
lock arose, ostensibly, out of the culpable
conduct of the Attorney-General and Minister
for Justice in accepting briefs against the
State while at the same time they drew large
salaries for defending State interests—in
other words, they tried to serve two masters,
and to be paid by plaintiff and defendant at
one and the same time. The matter was not
one which affected the Cabinet as a whole,
and there was no earthly reason why the
Government should have fallen owing to the
personal greed of two of its members. But
Premier Bibbs, whose leading vices are a
great and comprehensive gratitude, and a
fixed resolution to stand by his colleagues
whether they are right or wrong, but
principally when they are wrong, because
then there is a greater prospect of that free
fight in which his soul delights, chose to
make it a Government question, and as a
consequence the Ministry was defeated by
21 votes. Thereupon Parliament was unex-
pectedly prorogued, and the badly-defeated
Cabinet remains, for the time at least, in
possession.
» • *
Since that memorable defeat every paper
in Now South Wales has been vociferating
on the subject either from the Government
or the Opposition standpoint. The Foreign-
trade press has systematically cursed the
Ministry with a great and bitter curse, and
has dragged innumerable blue-moulded pre-
cedents from their congenial dust, and
hunted up innumerable worm-eaten authori-
ties to throw at the heads of the Bibbs
Cabinet, But, in reality, neither party’s
view of the case is a matter of any special
moment. In the first place, the Opposition,
as a whole, was absolutely and utterly
indifferent to the moral aspect of the
transaction. The majority of 69 which
voted against the Government consisted
of about 50 members who always vote
against the Government, and about 19
others who turned the scale, presumably
from motives of genuine patriotism. Ihe
Foreign-trade leader, who expects to inherit
the Premiership when the moral stench of
the whole business drives the Dibbs Ministry
from office, did not personally move in the
matter, because to him it was merely an
interesting point of legal etiquette; he was
so utterly unable to realise that anybody
could seriously object to the attitude of the
two Ministers that he let his opportunity
pass, and but for the action of a private
member the transaction would presently
have dropped out of sight. His present
attitude of horror is merely a matter of
party politics. And the action of the Cabinet
in demanding, almost in so many words,
that the Assembly should whitewash the
utrly transaction and declare it to be al-
together commendable, was also strictly in
keeping with the traditions of party
politics. The leading axiom of the party
system is that one section of the House
shall declare everything done by the Govern-
ment to be utterly horrible and bad, whether
it is bad or not, and that the other section
shall declare everything which the Govern-
ment does to be absolutely spotless and
good, whether it is good or not. Under
these circumstances the Cabinet was bound
to defend Messrs. Barton and O’Connor,
and the Opposition was hound to attack
the n, and whether both parties were sincere,
or whether one of them was sincere and, if
so, which one it was, or whether both of
them were purely mercenary and hypocritical,
are merely matter's of conjecture.
* # •
Also, the unwonted resolution of the Dibbs
Cabinet to remain in office after its defeat
matters very little, apart from the fact that
sundry dusty precedents have been badly
damaged thereby. As it was impossible for
a new Ministry to be re-elected till the new
const tueucies were in working order, the
Cabinet would probably have had to remain
in office in any case till the general election.
Moreover, the great principle of party, so
far as the Ministry is concerned, is that it
shall remain in office to the last gasp
whether the country wants it or not, and the
conduct of the Bibbs Government, in hanging
on to the bitter end, is only in keeping with
the oldest traditions of Government by
Contradiction. If the Cabinet had resigned
so long as there was one solid object left
to hold on to, it would have been false to
every axiom of politics ; the only difference
between it and any other Cabinet was that
the suspension of the Electoral Act gave it
an opportunity of holding on longer than
usual. In any and every case the one
enduring principle is to hold on, and the
Bibbs Ministry was true to the spirit, if not
to the letter, of the unwritten law. By-
and-bye, if Party Government continues to
develope on its present lines, defeated
Ministers will cease to resign at all, and will
have to be formally dismissed and then
dragged out of their offices by violence, or
ejected with the fire hose, or turned out by
means of stink-pots.
• # #
The real trouble is not that Dibbs remains
in, for, so far as men, apart from measures,
are concerned, the country cares very little
whether Dibbs remains in or not. Nor is it
that G. H. Reid remains out, for the people
of New South Wales will cheerfully let that
ephemeral humourist remain out for all
eternity. It is not even the alleged injury
done to the “constitution” that is in ques-
tion, for nobody really knows what the con-
stitution is, or how it has been injured. But
through this abject squabble almost the
whole work of the session baa been fooled
away and utterly wasted. This also,
by the way, is strictly in keeping with
party tradition. For another of the prin-
ciples of Party Government is that the
Legislature exists solely in order that the
Government may keep in office if it can, and
that the Opposition may get into office if
possible, and therefore the great business of
each party is to keep the other party from
doing anything so as to bring it into public
discredit. And the country must endorse
any and every act of meanness, folly,
treachery, or dishonesty done by the party
in power under penalty of bringing the
public business to a standstill ; it cannot
object to a single act done by a Protectionist
Government, unless it is prepared to go the
entire animal in the direction of Foreign-
trade, or to a single member of the Govern-
ment without reducing everything to sudden
chaos. Therefore, because the country was
not prepared to declare that the proceedings
of Messrs. Barton and O’Connor were alto-
gether praiseworthy, half-a-dozen urgent
measures and about 50 bills of less import-
ance have gone to wreck, and the whole
business of the country has come to a dead
stop. The nation at large has either got to
cordially approve of the hungry avarice of
two uninteresting lawyers, or else sacrifice
its entire public policy, and start the work
of the last twelve months afresh.
# # •
The party system has been a dismal
mockery since its first inception. Under its
operation the Legislature has practically no
control over the appointment of the subor-
dinate Ministers, and very little over that of
the Premier. It cannot reject one member
of the Cabinet, no matter how foolish or
degraded he may be, with- ut rejecting th
whole lot. It cannot object to or reverse a
single important public act of any one office-
holder without turning the whole Cabinet
out of office. lb cannot expel a single
Minister or reverse any act of a single
Minister without abjuring the whole policy
of the Ministry, and handing itself over
bound hand and foot to another gang of
political adventurers who are equally irre-
sponsible. It is always putting up with all
manner of insults, sins, and shortcomings
lest something worse should happen to it.
The whole system is a mass of utter irre-
levance and incoherence. There is no con-
nection between the Mining on Private
Property Bill and the yearning of Messrs.
Barton and O’Connor after private
fees earned at the expense of the public
interests, nevertheless that much-ueeded
measure, which would have afforded em-
ployment to many hungry and workless
citizens, has to be sacrificed in order thab
the country may be able to formally com-
plain of the private hog-selfish ness of these
two lawyers. It was only by deliberately
sacrificing it and a dozen other bills of more
or less importance that the Legislature could
express its disapproval of their conduct.
And it would be quite as logical, and a good
deal cheaper, and at least equally to the
point, if the authorised method of objecting
was by setting fire to the Cathedral, or by
the leader of the Opposition running amok
down George-street, Sydney.
The Desolation which is to Come.
The following Woman’s Suffrage report, ex-
tracted from Melb. Age, is cordially recom-
mended as cheerful reading for the general
public :
Melbourne, Thursday.—A demonstration was held to-
night at the Congregational Church in favour of the
extension of the franchise to women. There was a very
large attendance of women and girls, and no less than
15 resolutions were carried, providing for reforms of a
most sweeping and drastic character. The suppression
of smoking altogether was one of the modest demands,
while the removal of the penny-in-the-slqt cigarette
machines from railway-stations and the abolition of the
liquor-traffic were asked for. Protests were also entered
against theatrical performances and Sunday trains.
The above is an instructive picture, or rather
the beginning of a picture, of the world that is
to be if ever the truly pious and stupid woman
becomes a political majority in the land. Also
it is a picture of what will be if ever the truly
pious man becomes supreme, only the male of
the human species is less devout than the female,
and the chance of a regime of male snufflebusters
is consequently more remote. It is a lurid
illustration of the desolation that is to come—
the desolation of a world of Cold Tea, the
dead level of monotony that will be achieved
when every joyous sin is abolished, and only the
virtues and a few oppressively dreary iniquities
are left. It foretells the coming of an age of
slow and long-dragged-out psalm-tunes ; a day
when all the human race will keep time in one
solemn goose-step on the road to a church where
black-coated and black-hatted men will tell
them solemn things in solemn monotones about
an exceedingly unpleasant hereafter. Then he
who desires to smoke will rise up in the middle
of the night and smoke secretly in a lonesome
place contrary to Act of Parliament, and who-
soever desires to chew tobacco and expectorate
will go out fearfully into the exact centre of
some great wilderness and there expectorate in
disguise and under a false name. Then the
land will be possessed by the evil spirit of
Ginger Beer and it will become a nightmare.
There will be no ale in these times, and no
oysters, because they lead to stout and late
hours, and every man will be officially smelt
twice a day by a duly-constituted authority on
the look-out for signs of concealed liquor.
Further, there will be no more billiards or
cards or dice or horse-racing or any other form
of gambling or dissipation whatever, and no
man shall plug any other man on the eye or
elsewhere, for the gate-money or otherwise,
and the world in general will be painfully good
in these days of the desolation which is to come.
« • •
Evidently there is a period of monotony
coming in the earth’s chronicle. A great deal
of the mystery has died out of life, to begin with.
The hand of Satan no longer reaches up above
the waters of the black Atlantic to drag down
passing ships ; the gods are mostly dead, and
the powers of evil are mostly dying. That
strange region where the mariner used to get to
the outer rim of the world, and fall over in an
eternal fall which lasted until he was wrecked
against tne constellation of Hercules or there-
abouts, has been anxiously looked for and has
proved to be non-existent. So has that alleged
locality in the far southern seas where the sun
goes out for ever, and the waters slope down-
hill, and lost ships sail onandon down agliddery
steep till the crews die of old age in the dark-
ness, and the vessels themselves become coffins
sliding eternally on the inclined roof of an up-
ended ocean that leads to nothing recognisable.
The sea-serpent of good old Bishop Pontoppi-
dan is worn out. The world has lost faith in
that ancient geographer who used to account
for unknown lands by the brief inscription
on the map: “This country is all full of
devils.” The mystic region on the east of
the Caspian Sea which used to be marked:
“The Ends of the Earth” has lost its mysticism,
The world has been mostly explored to a
greater or less degree, and it has proved itself
to be pretty much alike, the principal differ-
ence being that some parts of it are rather more
alike than others.
# # #
A good many favourite varieties of romance
also show signs of old age and senile decay. War
has become too much a matter of turning a
handle and then watching the butchery that
ensues. A vast number of picturesque though
dirty races have been, or are steadily being,
civilised off the surface of the globe, and many
picturesque though objectionable animals are
fast being extirpated. Evidently the time
must come when this earth will lie a profoundly
serious region of men and cows and very little
else. And when exploration is dead because
there is nothing left to explore; and many
varieties of adventure are exploded because the
opportunity for them is gone ; and war ceases
to be romantic because it becomes pure, un-
varnished murder, and because the warrior
simply hangs to an automatic butchery pump
until he is killed; and when, through
the medium of the pious Congregational
female of the future, drink and tobacco
have been abolished, and fiction has been
suppressed because it is demoralising, and
gambling has been utterly wiped out in all
its varieties, and the “age of consent” has
been raised to 70 or thereabouts, and there is
no more drama or comedy, and sport has died
a natural death because no one is allowed to
bet on the game, and no one is permitted to use
a strong expression even when he steps on a
tack in the dark, and there is no more ballet or
nigger minstrelsy, and love has been put down
because it is a fleshly emotion, and all the
world is in its solitary bed by ten—then this
earth will have got down to the cow-level for
good. A great horror of Cold Tea will lay hold
of it, and there will be nothing to live for
except the changeless, unchangeable monotony,
in wliich not even one cheerful sin will remain
to lighten the oppressive goodness of mankind.
• * *
The upheaval of the good Congregational
female is significant in more ways than one.
Her objections, are all directed against the sins
that man indulges in. The proposed abolition
of the theatre is partly based on the fact that,
owing to her ownexcessivegooduess, shehaslittle
real personal knowledge of itand its ways; partly
on the assumption that her husband is pursued
there by the gold-washed female who is partly
real and mostly legendary; and mostly on
Mademoiselle D’Argo’s legs. The enthusiasm
which is raised in the soul of man by these
solid and bounceable extremities is to her in-
comprehensible and therefore sinful. She does
not really desire to suppress the theatre in toto,
but she does want to suppress D’Argo’s two
powerful supports, and Bartho’s rythmical
dance, and the ballet in general, and to ex-
tinguish everything theatrical except “ East
Lyue”and afew weeping productions of the same
order—and possibly Charles Ryley’s beautiful
calves and the lovely smile of Mr. Roberts at
Sydney Royal. Similarly, she desires to abolish
tobacco because she doesn’t smoke, and liquor
because she doesn’t drink, whereas, in the
great drama of life, her male relatives are con-
tinually going out between the acts to see a
man. She proposes to abolish gambling, too,
because, as a rule, she doesn’t gamble, and if
she does she never pays when she loses ; and to
utterly prohibit the peimy-iu-the-slot machine
which is loaded up with cigarettes—though
nothing is said about the one which supplies
damp, muggy packages of stickjaw and similar
abominations. The good Congregational female,
in short, is a whale on the sins of man in all
their varieties.
• * •
But the crusade stops there. There is
nothing about the sin of an undue consumption
of confectionery, nor about abolishing overmuch
loquacity over the back fence ; nor about the
iniquity of “shopping,” which generally con-
sists in making a weary atom of humanity
unroll two tons of goods to no purpose, and
then leaving him in the lurch. There is no
mention made of that kind of debauchery which
is based, not on alcohol, but on the earl with
the unnaturally long legs in the London
Journal. Ami in all the crusades against im-
morality there is never a mention of the
necessity for raising the age of consent as
regards the unduly elongated nobleman in the
Journal, though half the womanhood of Aus-
tralia and Great Britain elopes mentally and in
a vision, with that pictorial hero every time he
is published. The necessity for raising the age
of consent in the matter of the blastiferous
peer in the cheap serial, and preventing girls of
tender years falling victims to his fascinations
regularly once a month, has not received the
attention it deserves. Further, there is nothing
in the programme about the abolition of the
tuneless piano drunkenness, or about the en-
forcement of cleanliness by Act of Parliament.
As the average civilised man has a bath about
twice as often as the average civilised woman
this latter point might have come in for a good
deal of serious notice.
# # •
The programme of the Congregational female
is practically the abolition of sin, and this
world will be a solemn place when sin is abol-
ished. History consists almost entirely of it;
so do biography, fiction, drama, religion, ad-
venture, and nearly everything else which
makes up the story of mankind. When it is
abolished the world will be a dull and arid
waste, and the glacial epoch of Cold Tea will
be a great affliction, and an incubus on the soul
of five continents, and it will be about time for
man to start out for another planet. There are
many theories about the final death of humanity,
including death by frost when the sun grows
cold and becomes a mere cinder in the blackened
sky, death by water when the oceans eat away
the continents and a shoreless sea rolls all
round the globe, death by universal thirst when
the seas sink into the inner caverns of the earth
and leave its surface desert—by suffocation
when the atmosphere is lost in space—by fire
and concussion when the solar system knocks
up against the star Algiatha and goes to
pieces—and by promiscuous upheaval when the
ice-weight at the poles sends the earth off its
balance. And, in addition to all these recog-
nised theories, there is still another fate in
prospect —death from universal dreariness and
lemonade and eold-tea, and from the melancholy
dark-brownness of all things, and from being
preached into insensibilty by wild Congrega-
tional women. This latter is evidently what
the world is going to die of. The other disasters
may all come along in turn, but they won’t
arrive in time to do any damage.
Working the Crimson Thread.
It is true that we are at the day of social
issues, but we have not passed the time when
questions of national import can be slighted
witli impunity. Therefore, it is well for Deino-
oui 'y, whilst setting itself squarely in front oi
social problems, to keep an open eye on the
wiles of politicians in matters of a. national
kind. There are abundant reasons for this.


We know full well, from experience, that there
are men in positions of trust in Australia who
are ready and even eager to barter the interests
of their country for the smiles of Imperialism,
and this should cause us to view with suspicion
any attempt made by our politicians to en-
tangle us in engagements, whether commercial
or political, with provinces of the empire beyond
the seas. This is peculiarly applicable to the
recent palavers with Canada in which the
‘ ‘ crimson thread” and the “silken bonds”
have been curiously entwined with matters of
business, which, if brought to an agreement,
would result in the maximum of gain and the
minimum of expense to Canada; but the mini-
mum of gain and the maximum of expense to
Australia. In some ways, at least, Canada has
her head screwed on fairly well. She sees very
clearly that, apart from all sentiment, there is
money in the Crimson Thread. It is a good
spec, for her, since by it she may, at the ex-
pense of Australia, transfer the trade between
this continent and America, from San Fran-
cisco to Vancouver, and, in addition to obtain-
ing control of the traffic now flowing through
the States, she may reasonably hope that a line
of steamers sufficiently endowed with Aus-
tralian subventions will be able to carry goods
at such rates as to vastly augment it. Thus, so
far, from a Canadian standpoint, the crimson
thread is a real good thing. It has enabled the
Canadians to get hold of £lO,OOO a year of our
money to put them in a position to undersell
the Americans in our markets and to in-
crease their importance at the expense of
the States, and now, working the thing
for all it is worth, they have partly
succeeded in inducing us to spend a vast
sum of money to invest them with a monopoly
of the telegraph business between .Europe and
Australia-—a monopoly, undoubtedly, because
its munificent endowments from the revenues
of Australia would not only allow it to defy
competition across the Pacific, but enable it to
render the Port Darwin line unpayable.
* • *
Apart from the commercial and political
advantages of having control of the communi-
cation between Australia and Great Britain,
the business between the Atlantic and Pacific,
carried over the Canadian wires, would render
these increasingly productive; just as the
carriage across the Canadian wastes of the
traffic brought to Vancouver by the steamers
we pay for will cause that once white elephant
of the Dominion—the Canadian-Pacific railway
—to grow and flourish like a green bay-tree, a
circumstance, no doubt, entirely satisfactory to
the Canadian Dominion. But where, beyond
paying for it, does Australia come in ? That is
a question asked in vain by Australians, when
bound by their politicians to pay £120,000 per
annum to keep a British squadron to protect
British merchant men, and to annually con-
tribute some thousands of pounds to support,
in the Imperial Institute, a toadying set of
schemers in plotting against our liberties.
Counting up all these tributes, with others
that might be mentioned, and it will be
tolerably apparent that the silken bonds form
bonds to our people in more senses than one,
and, this being the case, it is just about time
that the blue-nosed contrivers, and their co-
adjutors in Westminster, should be carefully
advised that wx appreciate their sentiments on
the silken bonds question at their full money-
value, and that there are many in Australia
who believe that the palavering and slobbering
over “The Empire” are influenced by self-
interest more than fraternity. To those of that
opinion it will also occur that the main-
tenance of Canada, apart from the United
States, is not a subject for hilarious con-
gratulation, but much rather the reverse.
* # #
If Canada were to complete the work
of Federation on the North American
continent by joining the great Union that
borders her on the south along a frontier
of over 3000 miles, she would be doing
infinitely more for the race we belong to than
by professing regard for distant and trans-
marine countries. The peace of the world, or
the peace of a whole continent, is of much more
importance in this stage of our history than the
aggrandisement of Great Britain or the ability
of the Empire to dictate to other Powers, and
if Canada were to join with the States the prin-
cipal means whereby Great Britain—-and, as
things are, Australia also —could be embroiled
with the United States would be removed,
i’his is what the Canadian-Pacific men, the
Ottawa Government people, the army and navy
men, the lovers of titles and haters of Demo-
cracy crowd, do not care to see. But they are
quite satisfied for Great Britain to pull their
chestnuts out of the fire on one side in case of
differences with the United States, and for
English-speaking people in all parts of the
world to be liable to be drawn into a war
with the American Union over some question of
fisheries or territorial rights, and they are not
at all disposed to abandon their present style of
Government as long as its continuance guaran-
tees them the expenditure on their coast and
their highways of a stream of Australian and
Imperial subventions. That being the state of
Canada on the European business, is it not time
for Australia, when the Empire palavering is
again on the board, to follow the Canadians in
playing for her own hand ? Then, if we do so,
the Blue-noses will learn, for the first time in
their history, that Australia has far more to
gain by close relations with the great and
wealthy American States than by any connec-
tion with the weak and snow-clad Dominion,
which revels in the tinsel of a mock-king
at Ottawa, and gushes over its “brethren”
when they are thousands of miles away whilst
snarling at its big brothers just over the border.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Government by “Stoush.”
Government by Contradiction we have always
with us, and government by minority is a
familiar thing, while government by bluff and
government by “ euchre ” are more than occa-
sional incidents in political history. And, as if
hese were not burden enough there has lately
been super-added in New South Wales a form of
authority more hideous than all the others, and
which may be labelled government by “ stoush.”
It had its origin in the braggart visit of a lot of
big-footed and heavy-fisted footballers who
went to “ assist ” Mr. M'Millan at one of his
meetings held near Darliughurst somewhere
about two years ago. The demonstration suc-
ceeded, and Mr. M‘Millan has never had a
good hearing in Sydney since, and has been
compelled to go away to a suburban con-
stituency because, as he alleges, he will
not be listened to in East Sydney. But
his meetings have not had a monopoly of
the “ stoush ” system. The Active Service
Brigade, and the pugilist “ push,” and the
Mosquito Club, and gentleman-larrikins of the
football persuasion have carried their assault
and battery campaign into every recent politi-
cal meeting, and the question whether a motion
or amendment will be carried depends now
upon the possession by either party of a con-
trolling force of fists. The police are not
counted on either side. They are present
simply to see what goes on: the work of
“chucking out” opponents and suppressing
hostile disorder is left to the bullies of the
respective parties. And the climax was reached
when, at the recent meeting of the Foreign-
trade party in Sydney Protestant Hall, Parkes
(chairman), and Messrs, Reid, Wise, Want,
and a host of other upholders of lawnorder,
looked on at the edifying sight of a notorious
pugilist with an objector’s head in chancery in
the front row of the gallery. At whose request
the bruiser punched heads on that occasion is
immaterial to the present question. He may
have. knocked his victims over for the mere
love of exercise, but the disgraceful fact
remains that the promoters of the meeting, by
permitting his continued ministrations, identi-
fied themselves with the policy of stoush. It
is weakly urged as an excuse that opposition
was anticipated, and that it was necessary to
meet violence with violence. The excuse
admits assault and battery to be a means of
political advocacy, and assumes that it is right
to use illegal violence to attain a political
object, which is bad law and worse reason-
ing. The fact is that the whole evil
has arisen out of the practice of allow-
ing bullies to do the work of the
police. An agreement to prevent a speaker
from being heard is a legal offence, and an
assault is no less a crime, because it is made
during the course of a public meeting. Years
ago the case of Lee v. Macpherson showed
that an assault in the Assembly was just as
clearly punishable in the police-court as an
assault committed in the street, and the police
have only to use their ordinary powers of arrest
in order to put a final stop to the evil that has
disgraced Sydney recently, and which threatens
to put every candidate at the next general
election at the mercy of the pugs, and
“ pushes.”
The Fraudulent Trustee Industry.
Last week a Sydney solicitor named Broad
received a sentence of five years’ imprisonment
—an altogether astonishing consummation in
the case of a “ Gentleman, One.” His offence
was an utterly heartless misappropriation of a
woman’s whole life’s savings, amounting to some
£2600, which sum had been entrusted to him
on the strength of his presumed respect-
ability as a solicitor and a gentleman
by Act of Parliament. Broad’s case is an
amazing exception to the hitherto almost in-
variable rule that a solicitor may steal anything
on the face of the earth with no worse conse-
quences than temporary or permanent suspen-
sion—which only means the necessity of starting
business again with the assistance of a qualified
dummy whose name appears on the official
door-plate. Also, as a further innovation,
Broad is required to refund £5OO of his steal,
while the ordinary suspended solicitor refunds
nothing at all. This new departure, how-
ever, is not to be regarded as a
precedent, and the next fraudulent solicitor, or
the next dozen fraudulent solicitors, will pro-
bably be suspended for a brief period and
allowed to retain the boodle in the good old
way. One of the most astonishing things on
record is the world’s simple faith in the common
or garden solicitor. The Australian legal prac-
titioner has bolted with the trust funds to
nearly every port on earth, and he has lain low
on nearly every Pacific isle waiting for the
storm to blow over, and he has been
suspended in every Supreme Court in the
seven Australasias, and the result, so far as his
clients are concerned, has been generally the
same—the money has never been recovered.
And to his stealings must be added those of the
banker, financier, and high-minded private
trustee, who have bolted with the widow’s
mite and the orphan’s humble crust by every
Valparaiso coal ship, and every tramp steamer,
and every South Sea trader that infests the
Pacific. As a general rule, the man who leaves
the fortune of his widow and orphans in the
care of any private trustee might as hopefully
leave them in the guardianship of the crocodile
at the Zoo. The Maoriland Government, recog-
nising this fact, has created a Public Trust
Office with the State as security for the honesty
of its guardianship. In Australia, owing to the
absence of such an institution, joint stock com-
panies are beginning to compete with that
private swindler, described—when he is found
out—as a “ Gentleman, One.” Melbourne has
now several of these institutions, and Sydney
has two the Permanent Trustee Co. of
New South Wales, and the Perpetual
Trustees. These companies act as executors,
trustees, administrators, agents for absentees,
etc., and perform all the other duties of the
private trustee. And, unlike the private
trustee, they can’t take the well-worn road to
the Pacific slope ; they never die or become
disabled by illness ; and if they ever defraud
the widow, their shareholders must make good
the loss out of capital. And seeing that the
solicitor, in very many cases, has no capital
except a brazen front, a reliable thirst, and a
yearning after the gold-washed female who
leadeth unto destruction, this advantage alone
is of inestimable value.
On behalf of Victorian charities interested in
the nullifying of Dr. Beaney’s “Canterbury
Bequest,” a queer application was made to
Chief Justice Madden last week. Poor, vain-
glorious Beaney bequeathed, as everybody
knows, £lO,OOO to his native city of Canter-
bury to found an institute that should bear his
name. Melbourne hospitals and other bodies
(being entitled to share “residue ” of Beaney’s
estate) disputed the legality of this Canterbury
gift, but Justice A’Beckett decided in favour
of the bequest. An appeal to the Full Court had
the usual legal result of upsetting the decision
previously given, and Beaney’s £lO,OOO was
ordered to remain in Victoria to swell the
residue aforesaid. Then, of course, the city of
Canterbury, considering itself put upon by Vic-
torian Judges, determined to take the matter to
the Privy Council. At present the case is
slowly making its way to that august tribunal,
but meanwhile Chief Justice Madden was
besought to strike out the reasons and explana-
tions which Justice A’Beckett had appended
in support of his judgment after the Full Court
had overruled it. This application was refused,
so the Privy Council will presently see why
Mr. A’Beckett differed from the other Judges.
One would have thought that there could be no
honourable cause for seeking to strike out the
A’Beckett opinions, inasmuch as they could
make no difference if they were bad opinions in
law. But the appealing counsel displayed
much anxiety to carry his point all the same.
Presumably, then, there is a danger of the
Privy Council overruling the Full Court which
overruled Justice A’Beckett, and saying some-
thing nasty on the matter. We shall see.
The San Francisco Examiner proposes to re-
organise the bi-cameral system of government
on an entirely new plan. Hitherto the system,
in most English-speaking countries, has been
to have one progressive Chamber and one
Chamber of Stagnation—one House whose mem-
bers try, in a feeble sort of way, to do some-
thing, and another which always prevents
them doing it. The scheme has been a dismal
failure. Therefore, it is suggested that there
shall be instead a sober Chamber and a drunk
one; or else only one Chamber, which will
periodically resolve itself into drunk com-
mittee, and while there either confirm or cancel
the things it did when in its sober condition.
The theory is that when the House is in
intoxicated session it will suffer from occasional
attacks of truthfulness—that the member who
is engineering a political railway through his
own estate will then announce, in the thick
utterance of honesty, that he doesn’t care a darn
for the country, or anything but his own private
swindle; that the man who is trying to negotiate
a land resumption spec, will rise and move
that the Government should buy his uninhabit-
able swamp at six times its value ; and that the
member who doesn’t give himself away will be
given away by some other member while he is
insensible under the seat. In short, the theory
is that when the House resolves itself into
drunk committee it will wreck all the little
schemes which it organised when sober, and
that ten members will speak at once, each busy
furnishing valuable information about the other
nine which will effectually block their re-
election and his own, and make Parliament all
the better for their absence. The scheme, if it
doesn’t end in murder, has some obvious
advantages. The one thing wanted to make it
complete is for the House to have a threefold
identity, and, in its third capacity, to resolve
itself periodically into bilious committee, in
which state it will finally review everything
which has passed the other two stages. If a
Legislature isn’t honest when it has a vast
amount of green fruit lying undigested on its
chest, then it never will be honest on this side
of the land that is paved with flames.
Attorney-General Barton, in answer to a
question in N.S.W. Assembly, said that the
convict Glasson, on the report of the visiting
surgeon, was exempted from wearing the usual
half-cwt, of fetters. But he added that gaol
regulation No. 36 provides that condemned
prisoners should be ironed, and “ no adequate
reason has been shown why the existing
practice should be discontinued.” Such an
answer is thoroughly in keeping with the hoary
traditions of Botany Bay. The object in iron-
ing a condemned prisoner is not to prevent his
escape, for he is no more likely to escape than
any other of the several dozen or several
hundred unfettered convicts Avho inhabit the
same gaol. And it is not to prevent him
dodging the gallows by suicide, for a leg-
ironed man can hang himself to the window
bars as well as one who is not ironed, and if a
prisoner really means suicide even half-a-ton of
rusty chains would not stop him. Moreover,
there is no logical reason why he shouldn’t
commit suicide if he wants to. Society only
desires to get rid of him, and if he saws his
head partly off in his cell it gets rid of him as
effectually as it could any other way. The
chains are only a formality—a remnant of red-
tape brutalism—a rusty incubus, left over from
the old convict days, and the Attorney-General
who can’t see why this ancient cruelty should
be abandoned is too obtuse for his century.
New South Wales badly wants a few statesmen
whose mental horizon is not bounded by the
narrow limits of gaol regulation No. 36.
That unspeakable tendency of New South
Wales Judges to be a law unto themselves
grows stronger every day. An instance is
reported from Young (N.S. W.). At that town
a woman, some little time ago, accused two
men of stealing from her the sum of £2 19s. Id.
The evidence for the prosecution was miserably
weak, and consisted largely in the fact that
the plaintiff swore to a B.N.S.W. £l-note
which was found in the possession of one of the
prisoners, as being her property, but as there
was no mark on the note to distinguish it from
any other note, and as all £l-notes issued by
the same bank are very much alike, this was
an interesting commentary on the great art of
identification rather than evidence. Then
the prisoners proved that they had drawn
certain amounts in wages, which were
sufficient to account for the money in their
possession, and the jury acquitted them.
Whereupon Judge Stephen remarked that he
approved of the verdict, and ordered that the
money found on the prisoners should he con-
fiscated and handed over to the prosecutrix. If
this order was lawful, it would seem that a
Judge can overrule the verdict of a jury—
which verdict is final—and compel a man to
hand over his own property to a prosecutor
from whom a jury has declared such property
was not taken by him.
The passing of the death sentence on Mrs.
Knorr, the Melbourne baby-murderess, was
attended by all the stereotyped gruesomeness.
The half-dying woman was brought to the court
to be exhibited to the crowd. She was “ stood”
up in the dock moaning piteously, and as she
presently collapsed she was supported on either
side by a warder. Then the weeping Judge
inserted himself in the specially-designed black
cap which makes the law, on such occasions,
look like an inky Punch ; and, in a voice
broken with emotion, and with the usual official
tear running down his visage, he delivered an
address to his practically insensible auditor,
and finished up with the customary sentence
and a slab of pious exhortation. Then the
prisoner, who had fainted, was carried away,
and the ceremony terminated. To all intents
and purposes the Judge might as well have read
a tract to a corpse, but, as usual, he had a lot
of undigested eloquence on his chest, and the
broken woman in the dock furnished an oppor-
tunity for getting rid of it. The eloquent Judge
and the weeping Judge are two varieties of
Judge that should be promptly removed from
the Bench. The eloquent Judge is an awful
specimen of bad taste, and the weeping one is a
mere affectation. The hangman doesn’t weep,
neither does the prosecuting counsel, nor the
warder who removes the condemned prisoner,
nor the arresting constable, nor the jury, nor
anybody else connected with the business ; and,
as tenderness is not confined solely to judges, it
can only be presumed that the weeping of these
latter is merely a matter of tradition. Also, it
is a decidedly objectionable tradition ; many a
prisoner has caught cold—which was no part of
his sentence—through being convicted before a
damp Judge, and has had his constitution
seriously undermined thereby; and as this is an
altogether unauthorised addition to the penalty
of the law, the moist variety of Bench is dis-
tinctly illegal, and the sentence passed by a
tearful court should be overset on appeal.
London Referee illustrates that vapoury thing
so sickeningly reiterated—-the “ British spirit
of fair play,” in these gentle observations con-
cerning the recent battue in Matabeleland
Imagine, finally, the coolness needed to meet the shock
of a deadly rush of black fiends, one-tenth of whose
numbers, if they succeeded in traversing the narrow zone
of fire, would be sufficient to massacre every white man
opposed to them. It is epic ! Nothing finer than this
Matabele campaign has been done under the English flag.
Probably not. The annals of “ the English
flag” are red with such episodes. A “zone of
fire ” that is certain death to the rudely-armed
Africans at half-a-mile distance, while the
white operator has only to turn a handle and
smoke a cheroot, is a pretty solid guarantee for
coolness. The writer is a tolerable coward,
but he would hurl defiance at the biggest whale
that ever smashed a boat or swallowed a Jonah
so long as he (the writer) was at safe Maxim
range from the water’s edge. “Epic!” So is
eating piecrust.

One of Julius Vogel’s sons is in the em-
ploy of the Chartered Company of South Africa,
and is out with Rhodes’ little army against the
Matabele. He has not done much in the fighting
line, his contribution to the success of the war
being mainly in the shape of booming letters to
his father, who very considerately places them at
the disposal of the press. Good old advertiser
Jew-lius. Where there is borrowed money, and
land-grants, and concessions that require to be
floated, and gold-mines, and the three-ball shadow
is over the land, there will the Vogels be gathered
together.
The Countess of Rothes, a Scotch peeress,
who died the other day at the age of 83, hands
down a grand old mortgage to her descendants.
When the seventh Earl of Rothes died in 1681,
Charles 11., his companion of many drunks and
unprintable-festivities, ordered for him about the
grandest funeral ever seen in Great Britain. But
he never paid for it, and the bill came on the
family, whose estate was mortgaged almost to the
last square inch of dirt to raise the amount.
Since then twelve holders of the title have lived
on husks and other cheap refreshments in the
effort to settle up for that old-time burial, and
yet the family mortgage hasn’t been entirely lifted
even to this day.
The hand of the Lord, which a couple of
years back pressed heavily upon Shirley Baker,
the Premier of Tonga, has now let up a bit, and
news from Auckland states that the young King
has dismissed his Ministry and formed a new one.
The new Premier is Sabeki, a devoted adherent of
Baker, and a much abler man than the deposed
Tukuaho, who was a mere dullhead—a creature
of the missionary interest. Every other appoint-
ment has been given to Baker’s friends, and, as
whites and natives alike want him back, the
probability is that he will return, though nob
without a strenuous protest from the High Com-
missioner of the Western Pacific, who will be
backed up by the almost all-powerful Wesleyan
mission. But with all Baker’s faults, there is no
doubt that, as Premier, ho will be the strong man
of the Pacific Islands. Insensate vanity, born of
too much power, once proved his downfall, bub ha
is nob likely to err again in that direction.
Premier Kingston (S.A.) has decided to
“retrench” Sammy Deering, who has held in
London the rosy billet of Assistant Agent-General
for S.A., since 74. For 40 years, Deering has
lived and flourished on officialism, his last S.A.
appearances being as Lands Secretary and Crown
Law Secretary. Before that he was aide-de-camp
to two Governors—Dominick Daly and James
Fergusson, and several clerks in several depart-
ments. lie first struck South Australia in ’53,
and waded in to occupy Government billets almost
immediately. His present screw is .£750, but the
“perks” run to as much again. In ’9l-2, Deer-
ing acted as Agent-General while the late Arthur
Blyth was ill, and it was quite a shock to him,
after Blyth’s death, to find that little John Cox
Bray wanted to air his balloon-like form in
London as A.G., which billet was the goal of Sam
Deering’s ambition.

The first production—the first at Sydney
Lyceum, that is—of “Princess Ida,” on Saturday,
revealed that epigrammatic sylph in a more
radiant aspect than .of old. Miss Nellie Stewart
made a much more artistic and melodious Princess
than Leonora Braham did six operatic years
agone. Miss Stewart was in bright voice for the
music that came her way—the Invocation to
Minerva was quite grand-opera iah ; likewise, she
deserved much praise and many flowers for her
share of the pessimistic but pretty quartette,
“The World is but a Broken Toy,” arid the
natural, unforced feeling she put into “I Built
Upon a Rock.” Also, the large audience
applauded Miss Stewart for the well-delivered
stump-speech in her first entrance. Elsie
Cameron, whose re-appearance is welcome as
flowers in May, played and sang Lady Blanche in
a calm, absorbed sort of way that made that
ambitious schemer a very real character; and
Florence Young, as Psyche, the girl with the
slightly “ blue ” song about Darwinian man, and
gentle May Pollard, as Melissa, did all they could
in thoir little parts.
* * *
The dependable Henry Bracy, as Hilarion,
was in fresh, articulate voice, and played the love-
lorn youth without a trace of the stage-manager.
His “ Appeal,” in the last act, was very wheed-
ingly done. Lauri was King Gama, got-np like
Richard Crookback, and talking like Albert
Norman when he wears a shiny iiat and swears
that a time will come. His Gama makes one
think that Lauri only keeps out of the sort of play
where the hero strikes a subtle attitude and dis-
sembles before his minions, not for want of natural
aptitude for that sort of thing, but because there’s
no money in it. Ryley, as Florian, made his
greatest hit when he tried to put his gown on
over his feet, but his futile struggles with
that garment rather spoilt the lively “Every
Maid” trio. Rosevear was in Woodfield’s old part
of “Cyril.” The difference was brought home to
you most keenly in the tipsy “Kiss Song.”
Woodfieid’s stage-intoxication insinuated that he
had got foggy on gold-top fizz in Venetian glasses
—Rosevear suggested rather a common colonial
beer obfuscation discovered in a pewter pot.
Howard Vernon did the best anybody could do
with Hildebrand; and Grundy, as the principal
warrior, dressed in sardine-tins, will improve with
practice. Also, the chorus made as little of a hot
night as the locusts do. “ Princess Ida ” ends the
conrio opera season in Sydney for the present, and
on Boxing Night the pantomime, “Beauty and
the Beast,” commences to glitter with Nellie
Stewart as Beauty, and Bartho heading the
bounding brigade.
** * *
Maggie Moore quitted Sydney Royal a few
days ago and departed for Melbourne, there to be
principal boy in a pantomime. The balance of
the company filled in the last ten days or so of the
season with “Queen's Evidence”—an eminently
respectable drama which no person could seriously
object to. Still there is a lack of novelty about
“ Queen’s Evidence ” these times. The joy faded
out of it some years ago, and now it seems reminis-
cent of nothing in particular save the modern
realistic novel—the one which opens with a dull,
stout woman in a brown dress sitting down beside
a table on a wet day, while a lame girl with hair
of no particular colour looks out of the window.
Still, regarded as a stop-gap, the drama served as
well as any other to wind up tiie season, and then
the company went its way like the ten lepers
alluded to in Holy Writ. The Royal is closed this
week while, the new lessees, Messrs. Gourlay and
Walton, are struggling with vast preparations for
the forthcoming pantomime. “Babes in the
Wood ” opens on Saturday (23rd) amid a blaze of
Oriental gorgeousness.
** « *
Her Majesty s (Sydney) is also closed
while the stupendous pantomime of “Jack the
Giant-killer ”is in preparation. It is generally
understood that this effort will makean astonished
earth sit up, and will break all past records. The
Criterion, after a long period of desolation, re-
opens on Boxing-Night with Thornton and
Arnold’s company in “ Charley’s Aunt.” Various
small shows of more or less note also propose to
take advantage of the season, and sundry houses
which have been almost forgotten of late will
make a fitful re-opening, like the unexpected
appearance of a ghost from the dank, oppressive
tomb.
*» * *
Melb. shows have accomplished nothing
novel during the past fortnight, and just now the
theatres are mostly closed for Boxing-Night pre-
parations, whilst their patrons track the fatted
goose to its lair. Thirteen farewell performances
of the clever Italian Company terminate to-night
(Thursday) with “Faust.” At 11 p.m., or there-
abouts, Giraud will sink through the floor of the
Princesses’ in the grip of our old friend Buzzi,
after which Del Torre will join the angels as
per stage directions, and the curtain will descend
upon a notable operatic season. “Pagliacci ” and
“ Oavalleria ” made a fairly successful double-bill,
the former being played by the original cast, ex-
cepting only the natty little figure of Signorina
Mettler in place of Del Torre. For acting pur-
poses the blonde lady was quite equal to her pre-
decessor, and an occasional paring down of
the music, to bring it within her compass
made little difference in the general effect.
“Cavalleria” was knocked about a good
deal on the nights when Giraud couldn't under-
take the tenor part in both operas. Sometimes
Fnisca gave a shrill, serio-oomio rendering of the
giddy soldier. On one occasion only plump,
cherubic Charles Saunders, the concert singer,
.appeared as a Turiddu who had to be shaken
violently by the prima-donna to make him seem
anxious to avoid her importunities. Saunders
proved unequal to the heartless conduct required
of him, which, in any case, would have been quite
out of keeping with his adipose tissue and happy
smile. Also, nervousness crippled his light tenor
voice, albeit he sang many bits like a genuine
artist. The tall, nnelastio baritone, with the
slender legs, was an indifferent substitute for
Caruson, as the teamster, and so, one way and
another, “Cavelleria ’’lost caste in its latter days.
On Saturday, the pastoral narrative of “L’Arnico
Fritz ” was warbled with much narcotic success.
*« « »
The Princess's pantomime is “ Little Red
Riding Hood ” —not that the title has much bear-
ing on the subject—and for this grand annual out-
break the Firm has secured the usual “host of
attractions.” Courtneidge, Jennie Lee and
Violet Varley are engaged to lead the riot. The
name of Greville appears. Mesdames Ethel
Haydon, Rosie Nott and Maie Saqui have con-
sented to take part in the fell deed. D’Argo and
Mary Weir come in as pearls of the ballet, and a
foreign gentleman who gambols on the slack wire
is especially recommended to notice by his huge
salary. This gifted stranger is said to be the
highest paid artist —next to Divine Sara —who
has ever visited Australia on business.
*» * *

Royal, in a broad, untrammelled panto, which als°
includes “Jack Horner.” The Royal company
reads quite strong enough to bring grist to the old
mill. Tretty Bella t’ennan, Martin Hagan, and
a number of “variety” people are down on the
bill, together with Miss Isabel Webster and
Leumane, who will certainly give a good musical

“ Charley’s Aunt” flourishes until Friday
night at the Bijou, and then journeys along to
Sydney. On Boxing Night the Seraphina Opera
Co., under David Cope’s management, will re-open
this theatre and struggle to keep its gates ajar.
** * *
Dan Barry, with Ms dramatic tribe from
the interior, will occupy the poor old Alexandra
on Saturday (23rd). Their pioneer show is to be
“Ransom,” a play illustrating bygone bushrang-
ing days, whilst the advertised prices are strictly
adapted to modern requirements.
** * *
The Beaumonts, who live, move, and have
their being in a glass-case with water in it (but
are infinitely more diverting than goldfish or
aqnarflim exhibits), form a chief attraction at
Melb. Alhambra. Also, Slade Murray is a recent
arrival here, and Xmas prospects are pretty
bright. The Gaiety rubs along as usual, and

Night.
** * #
Melb. Exhibition, a building notoriously
addicted to Xmas “ revels ” and profuse celebra-
tion of the season in its sacred or festive aspects,
intends to be very busy this year. W. J. Turner
has arranged a series of large shilling entertain-
ments, ranging from oratorio and views of
Palestine down to funny clowns and skirt-
daneing. As for the vocal artists attached to this
undertaking, they are grave or gay with equal
impartiality. It is not unusual for a gentleman
to sing “Nazareth’’one day, and impersonate the
Devil 24 hours later.

Dear Bulletin, “ Immomeena,” tho
operatic production of two Adelaide (H.A.) men,
has been most favourably criticised —in Adelaide.
Which is why the comments are worthless. This
so-called comic opera is only tit for suburban pro-
duction by (say) a University Musical Society. I
don’t think, outside of Adelaide, it would run for
two nights; probably there’d be an impromptu
massacre at the opening chorus. Curtain rises on
a meeting of some 15 station-hands, in scarlet
shirts, corduroys, and top-boots, with the girls
attached to the station. On the opening-
night, these queerly-dressed “bushies” were
entrusted with stockwhips to handle, but got
them so tangled up that they had to be taken
away. To hear “Coo-ee!” sung and repeated
echo-wise, gave an Australian the hump. The
dialogue is vapid and even vulgar. The grazier tells
the girls to go in for “ unsweetened gin!” A
song of “Australia,” under flying banner d la
“ Pirate King,” and with a “Rouse ye, Gawler ”(!)
chorus, maddens a native to want to burn Aus-
tralia in effigy. Plot? Ahem :—Alderman Mayne
reaches the station in search of an erring son,
being accompanied by a scientist in search
of a bMyip and other curios. A spieler
namesake is foisted on the alderman’s cre-
dulity for a while, but, by and bye, the
real prodigal, Trooper Fergus, is hugged to his
dad's bosom and enabled to marry Kitty Crosby.
The love-passages are as sheepish as some in real
life. The music is reminiscent—Sullivanesque;
in some places, diluted “ Mikado; ” redeemed in
other places with melodious duets and a first-class
gavotte. But the dialogue ! Great Congreve 1
Tiie scientist rushes on and off, speaking patter
songs, chasing the bunyip, taking long pulls out
of a black bottle, and generally stupefying the
audience, till the bunyip is captured. And this
is the climax of the button-bursting comic opera—
the hauling on to the stage, by means of a
hawker, a rope, a cord, a string—of a small pug-
dog. A wheeze as old as original sin.—Yours,
&c. C.P.H.

A correspondent forwards a long and
abusive letter on many subjects, and winds up by
objecting to The Bulletin’s favourable notice of
% recent production at a certain Sydney theatre,
which, he says, could only be owing to the paper's
representative being “squared.” He also asserts
that dramatic critics usually are “ squared.” The
Bulletin can only say, in reply, that it believes
the average Australian dramatic critic to be
strictly honest —so far as it knows, he never gets a
chance to be otherwise. The writer lias attended
all manner of shows for over six years, and in
all that time ho only hud one opportunity to
forget tho dignity of his position, and that was
when the father of a fat girl took him aside, and,
in a smothered voice, offered him 10s. He also, as
a further attempt at corruption, made the fat girl
take off her boots and stockings and exhibit two of
tiie largest feet in Christendom. Apart from this
solitary incident he has always been strictly disin-
terested because he had to, and ho presumes that
Hither critics have met with a similar experience.
The press doesn’t get a decent chance to be cor-
rupt these times. The beastly integrity of
theatrical managers is against it.
At Povarty Point.
The penny Argus lately spoke of “ Helen Terry and
Mrs. Langtree.” We’ll be hearing next of J. C. Wilkinson
and George Copping.
Prank Thornton’s proper name is Tubbs. 110 has a
brother in the office of a well-known Hebrew financier in
Melbourne.
Maude Hobson, who plays the name-part in “ A Gaiety
Girl,” at the Prince of W ales, London, is alleged to be a
native of St Kilda (Melb.) She first appeared at the
Gaiety in the time of the “Happy Family’’—Nellie
Farren, Kate Vaughan, Edward Terry, and Teddy Royce
—as an “ extra lady.” Twelve months later, she married
Captain W. B. Hayley, of the 11th Hussars, and after-
wards lived over the years in the Sandwich Islands, where
her husband was Vice-Chamherlain to King Kalakaua,
and she a lady-in-waiting to the Queen. She is decidedly
handsome, but the critics say ugly things about her
singing.
According to English files, Messrs. Lestocq and Lincoln
have constructed an Australian drama, entitled “The
Bush King.” It was to have been produced at the Surrey
early in November. Lestocq’s partner is Mr. Willie
Lincoln, son of the senior partner in Lincoln, Stuart &
Co., the big Melbourne cheap-clothing firm. He is a
mild-looking youth, and has a harmless ambition to be a
dramatist. Before leaving Melbourne with his port-
manteau full of MSS., he got about three of his pieces
produced at girls’ schools and amateur charity shows
without any fatal results.
Gilbert and Sullivan’s newest prima donna is named
Ann Mackintosh.
The mild and persuasive Snazelle is in South Africa. At
latest advices a Johannesburg reporter had failed to boom
him as he thought he ought to be boomed, so next night
he delivered a brief address stating that the pressman
was “ a brute and an ass.” Snazelle has an unpretentious
British way about him which is very charming.
The Rev. De Watt Talmage is coming to Australia on a
lecturing tour.
A travelling company was playing “The Merchant of
Venice ” recently in a Maonland country town. The
show went well enough until it readied that place where
Portia tells Antonio to bare his bosom for the knife.
Then a voice from tiie gallery interposed in a few brief
words: “Garni E cant. Don’t yer see his shirt
buttons down the back ? ” The curtain went down after

Bland Holt advertised his big drama, “ A Million of
Money,” round Maoriland by dropping purses full of
bogus sovereigns about the street. He generally dropped
the purse near to a respectable-looking man of pious
exterior. Then the respectable - looking man would
promptly put his foot on it, stoop down and meditatively
scratch the calf of his leg, and at last gently pick up the
plunder and glide down a bye-street. Then there would
be a sound of unprintable language down that bye-street,
anil Bland would go on his way in peace.

Penley was fairly “ had ” over the sale of tiie Australian
rights of “Charley’s Aunt.” He had vowed that he
would not Sell on any terms to Frank Thornton, who, on
his part, was equally determined to get the piece. Time
after time Frank nnavailingly tried to negotiate. At last,
it was arranged between Thornton and Charles Arnold'
that the latter should buy, if possible, and that Thornton
should then have tho play and join with Arnold and
“ Hans the Boatman ” in an Australian tour. The scheme
was successful; Penley parted with the Australian rights,
and his hated rival got tho play after all.

Amateur theatrical clubs complain without reason that
W. and M. charge them a fee of £3 or £5 for the right to
give one performance of any of the firm’s operas. The
real truth is that W. and M. would prefer amateur per-
formances to be restricted to operas in which they have
no interest. They don’t care for selling their sanction
for a “fiver ” in most cases.

Richard Stewart, senr., commenced life as a tailor, then
became a ship’s carpenter, ran away from his ship in
Port Jackson, and took to tiie stage. His wife is the
mother of the Australian stage. She arrived in Hobart
Town in 1842 as Mrs. Stirling. In 1844, in Sydney, she
married her second husband, an excellent violinist
named Guerin, who was the father of Mrs. Harwood and
Miss Nellie Stewart.

tile piece was too slow because it had no dancing in it.
Tiie co. had no ballet, so the obliging Duncan donned a

and the manager reported that the' audience all agreed
that Macallan) was the best actor in the co.

The hardest thing for an actor to do is to eat naturally
on the stage. Nobody looks really graceful when he is
sating—in fact, it is a question whether every human
being ought not to have his meals in absolute solitude—
and when he knows that several hundred people are
watching him the result is dreadful. Not one aelor in a
thousand can make any impression on his food under
these circumstances ; the result is about the same as if he
opened and closed a book on a piece of meat. Moreover
he feels bound to converse with hit month full to show
that he is quite at his ease. Titheradge has this complaint
badly, and at the stage-banquet he talks with his mouth
fuller than any other actor of The Bulletin’s acquaint-
ance.

An “Australian Reciter.”
In deference to repeated solicitations, The Bulletin
proposes to issue a volume of Readings and Recitations,
consisting entirely of prose and verse which originally
appeared in this paper. The Editor will feel obliged for
any suggestions as to selections deemed by readers suit-
able for incorporation in a work of the kind, and it will
facilitate matters if, whenever possible, each adviser gives
the date of the issue in which the item appeared. Special
consideration should be given, in selecting specimens, to
their elocutionary possibilities.
