Mr. T. C. Kerry, the New Guinea ex-
plorer, has just returned from a successful cruise
in the South Seas, and left by the Tasmania for
Auckland on Wednesday last. Mr. Kerry has
been engaged in exploring work in New Guinea
for the last 20 years, and intends shortly to pub-
lish an illustrated book of his travels in those little-
known parts.

Madame Belle Cole is coming to Australia. A Melbourne
paper sketches the prospective visitor as follows
“Belle is an immeasurably finer artiste than Antoinette
Sterling, although she may not have the natural voice of
that wild, prancing singer and she-buffalo from the
plains. She is, like most contraltos, preposterously
obese. When she walks on to the platform she wobbles
all over. And, what is more peculiar, one of her favourite
songs is a selection from one of the Italian operas, com-
mencing ‘O Fatima!’ If anyone else sang a song con-
taining the word ‘ fat ’ in her presence you would call it
grossly personal. But, though she is a strong woman’s
suffragist, Belle Cole is to be relied upon not to make
herself ridiculous as Sterling did, careering round, shriek-
ing and getting delirious.”

Lord Henry Somerset, who dabbles in musical com-
position whilst ins wife tears around Great Britain on the
teetotal lay, has just written a successful “Song of
Sleep.” It is a simple, drowsy melody, and the words
were supplied all out of his own noble head. Altogether
the song is a respectable achievement for a lord.

The Wild Cat Column.
There is still some vague and shadowy talk of
criminally prosecuting the Freehold Investment Co.
(Melb.) directors. Of all the “boom” specs, this was
legally the most impudent, and the only person who has
ever appeared shocked by the suggestion of crime in the
matter is Henry Gyles Turner, a liquidator with singularly
liberal ideas on financial management. Just now the
name of James Balfour is before the Court in connection
with a compromise offered by that holy gentleman
towards squaring an action for the recovery of certain
dividends “alleged to have been wrongfully declared ”
by the Freehold Investment directorate. The old
“ Balfour, Elliott & Co." scandal is also erupting again in
a somewhat angry manner, so there is still some hope
that the history of various gigantic advances made to
that unsatisfactory firm (Elliott is Balfour’s son-in-law)
may be given to an ignorant world.
• • • • •
The story of one of these advances, from the
standpoint of one or two aggravated investors, was
poured, the other day, into the capacious ear of the
Court, Messrs. Balfour, M. H. Davies, J. M. Davies, and
Moodie, according to this tale, were shareholders in the
firm of Balfour, Elliott & Co., and were also large share-
holders in the Freehold Co., of which some of them were
directors. In their capacity as Balfour, Elliott and Co.
they, it is alleged, were interested in some land at Elstern-
wick, said land having been bought for ±‘25,000. Then,
when it boomed to a paper value of, perhaps, £70,000, the
Freehold Co., which was partly them, lent to themselves,
as Balfour, Elliott and Co., some £59,000 on the security
of this same land, and they were thus enabled to declare
a dividend in their capacity as Balfour, Elliott and Co.—
of ±6500 each. The loan was never repaid, and the
interest, at 9 per cent., remains outstanding to
tiiis day. Nevertheless, the interest due and unpaid
by themselves in one capacity was allowed by
them in their other capacity to appear in the
accounts of the Freehold Co. as profits realised,
and on the strength of it, among other things, the Free-
hold Co. paid a dividend in which the same financiers had
a share. Therefore, they had one dividend because they
had borrowed a large sum of money, and another partly
because they owed the interest on the said amount. Now
the “ security ” is valued at something like £7000; the
Freehold Co. is gone bung with total liabilities of
±1,500,000; most of the financiers involved are under-
stood to be practically ruined ; and the one, Balfour, who
still keeps his honoured head above water, offers ±5OOO,
payable by ten yearly instalments, without interest, in
satisfaction of all claims ; and there is much ground and
lofty cuss-language among depositors and others.
The above transaction is merely quoted as one
of the financial methods which led to the strange
doubling-up of the once great Freehold Investment Co.
The transactions on which it is proposed to base a criminal
prosecution are still dark and horrid mysteries, and con-
cerning them no particulars are yet available.

Premier Patterson has at last decided upon
Importing an American expert to manage the Victorian
railways. “ And yet,” says a Melbourne paper, “ there
is hardly a single railway in the United States which has
been administered with sufficient ability to keep it from
insolvency.” East year the railway experts of the U.S.
produced an average profit of a little over per cent.
If anything save experience could burst up the_ Austra-
lian’s grovelling reverence for the American fetich, that
should do it.

• * *
The report and balance-sheet of the National
Mutual Life Association shows that the total annual
revenue in now close on £BOO,OOO, and the funds of the
society are £1,373,272, being an increase during the year
of £113,699. The interest on investments for the past
year covered ail death claims and left £BOOO over, and the
institution which can show such a result as this is in a
decidedly enviable situation. The expenses of manage-
ment are a trifle under 19 per cent, of the total income—a
very low' proportion considering the intense competition
existing nowadays. The National Mutual has been 24
years in existence, and it has a good record of steady pro-
gress. It ranks a good second among purely Ansi ralian
offices—the gigantic A.M.P., of course, holding first place
—and seems likely to keep up its position.
• * #
The Land Corporation of West Australia has
declared a dividend, in London, of 27f per cent. It is a
little thing like this which shows the blessings of foreign
capital at tiieir best. The Land Corporation of VV.A.
has employed no labour W'orth speaking of, it hasn't
irrigated any of the Westralian deserts, it has grown
nothing, it has neither ploughed nor fenced nor dene
anything at all except make land dear for the genuine
settler. In four years, at the present rate of progress,
VV.A. will have more than paid back, in dividends, all the
foreign capital it ever got from the corporation —for a
great deal of its money never readied Australia at all—
and will still owe the whole amount all the same. In 20
years it will have paid it hack five times over, and will
still owe it and have to keep on paying it. And it will
keep on paying it over and over again and still owing it
to all eternity, unless it buys out the corporation some
day by paying it, in a lump, about eight times the money
it first invested, and thus gets rid of the inculms. There
is no Old Man of the Sea who sticks so close as the curse of
foreign capital.
* • *
The sudden prorogation of N.S.W. Parliament
left the Bank Notes Bill unfinished, and its future
prospects are uncertain. It was lost by one of those
small and foolish blunders characteristic of the men who
throw Acts of Parliament together in N.S.W. The Bill
passed the Assembly, but in the Council it was discovered
that the Bank of N.Q. had been omitted from the list of
note-issuing institutions. The missing name was in-
serted, but before the verbal amendment could be agreed
to by the Assembly Parliament closed up. It is now alleged
that confidence hasn’t been restored so much as was
commonly supposed, and that for want of fhe expected
legislative assistance it may be found advisable to renew
the expired legal tender proclamation. Also, there are
ugly rumours to the effect that one reconstructed bank
is'past saving, by any legislative process which can be
invented, and when it goes the smash will be something
unprecedented in its completeness.

The Equitable Building Society of Brisbane
held its lottery on Monday lust, and the big prize (£30,000
worth of city propety) was won by the holder of ticket
No. 87,203. The said individual, it is alleged, only bought
his ticket on Friday last, and three days later affluence
fell upon him with a thud. Tims it is again demonstrated
that the last shall be first, and the first last. The Equit-
able leaves a good reputation behind it as a square and
honest gamble, and the Australian Building Society,
which is run to some extent by the same people, has an
honourable ambition to go and do likewise.
* # #
The old, old project for the conversion of the
Vic. debt into 3 per cent, interminable stock, is again to
the fore, and ttie Govt., it is seriously alleged, is inclined
to make the experiment. The theory of the project is this:
Victoria, like the rest of the Australias, lias a big debt
(£48,000,000), made up of a vast number of fragmentary
loans and debentures and stray issues of Treasury bills, at
various rates of interest. They keep failing due, and
they always fall due at awkward times. They are per-
petually being renewed under difficulties, and when the
province has to renew an old Joan every year it can’t
gel a decent chance to float a new loan. Therefore,
it is suggested that Victoria should convert its debt
into permanent 3 per cent, stock issued at 75—in
other words, that tin debt of £48,000,000 at 4 per cent.,
which is constantly falling due, should be exchanged f >r
a debt of £64,000,000 at 3 par cent., which will give no
trouble beyond payment of interest.
It is interesting to know that some British
financial authorities approve of this unspeakable project.
The great advantage claimed for it is that it will make
the interest no heavier—in fact, the interest will remain
exactly where it was before. But, seeing that it won’t
make it any lighter, it is difficult to understand where
the advantage comes in. If Victoria is never going to
pay off its debt, then the amount of it doesn’t matter, and
it is quite as easy for the province to owe £200,000,000 at
1 per cent, as £50,000,000 at 4 per cent. But if Victoria
does mean to pay off its debt some day or other, then it
will lose exactly £16,000,000. And the only equivalent
for this awful loss is the fact that the interest is to remain
just as it was before. Seeing that the interest is remain-
ing just as before, in any case, the benefit isn’t especially
conspicuous.
• • • * *
The bait is the opportunity for bragging forever
afterwards about the credit of the “kawliny.” Britain
borrowed for many years at 3 per cent., and was fearfully
and wonderfully bumptious over the fact that she could
get money at a lower rate than any other nation on earth.
The boast sounded well and cost just about £220,000,000.
Many of the British loans were floated at about 50, so that
if the country had paid 6 per cent, and borrowed at par,
the annual burden would have been just the same, and
the principal, which is now being repaid, would have been
£220,000,000 less. That great statesman, the bottomless
Pitt,’fooled away this stupendous amount for the mere
joy of being able to assert that England could get money
at so low a rate, and it is now suggested that Victoria
should fool away £16.000,000 for practically the same
reason. The fact that some eminent financial authorities
in Bull-land approve of the scheme is fully accounted for,
by the fact that Bull would get the £10,000,090. That
tenth-rate mediocrity, Treasurer Carter, is aiso believed
to favour the awful' project, which, considering the in-
herent dullness of the man, is not surprising.
The one absolute certainty of the case is that
the loan will have to be redeemed practically at par. No
country, when it begins to redeem its bonds, gets a chance
to buy’ them in the open market at any serious discount.
When a nation borrows, the fact that it wants money
makes its credit bad, and it borrows on unfavourable
terms; when it begins to pay off its debts, the fact t hat
it doesn’t want money makes its credit good, and its
bonds go up, and it buys them hack on unfavourable
terms also. The terms are always more or less unfavour-
able either way.
In the present state of general bankruptcy it is
as easy to start a gold-rush as to start a tote. Thus the
“It is reported that there are already 1000
diggers on the Burrangong goldfield, N.S.W.; the country
is very rough, and there is nothing to warrant a rush.'
Next week, probably, we shall read that all but a small
man and a big dog have deserted Burrangong, wherever
that may be.
• * *
Pluck has been rewarded afc Hillgrove (N.S.W.)
Some working miners extended the Baker’s Creek tunnel
many hundreds of feet; and have at last struck a gold
shoot of stone on the line of the reef misnamed “ big.’’
It never was a big reef, not even in comparison with
Smith’s reef. The stone shows coarse gold, and £2OO
has, it is said, been refused for a 16th share. What has
become of the little reef? Does it pinch right out under
the hill, or is it too poor to work?
# » •
Coolgardie stocks sickened a little in Adelaide
and Melbourne this and last w'eek, and no wonder. Cool-
gardie syndicate was just a modest little thing of 20 £25
shares, formed six months ago to take up ground. Then
shares were split up again and again till there were 1000
at £26. These ran up to £l5O, hence we have a company
which started with a capital of £5OO reaching a market-
value of ..150,000. Even at the present rates the market-
value is over £BO,OOO, and the original shares have a pre-
mium of thousands each.
* * *
Capitalists are wanted to tender for the Iron-
clad and Golden-clad mines and plant, at Cargo, N.S.W.
The former was worked to death by an English oo.— so
was the co. The latter was the idol of “ Yorkshireman,
the turf Attorney-General,’’ who, bedridden for years
before his death, could not see the mine, but imagined
himself a millionaire by its possession. Under the
influence of Yorkshireman’s enthusiasm the writer visited
the Golden-clad, and described it as a rank dulfer.
Then came along a man who put up a 20-head battery.
One crushing sufficed.
* # #
Apparently the bottom has nearly fallen out of
the famous Star of the East mine, Ballarat. The reef in
tile lower levels has practically vanished, and the im-
pression among many shareholders is that only a few
more dividends remain in the grand old mine which made
so many fortunes.
* * *
The comingr solution of the sulphide problem,
as a means of buoying up the hopes of silver shareholders,
is getting pretty sick. If the problem were solved to-
morrow the greater problem would be, what to do with
the silver. It is almost beginning to be an open question
whether there is any sulphide problem at all—or ever was
one.
* * *
There have been no extensive forgeries of Bank
of France notes for many years. The last great foiger
who troubled that institution was exiled to Cayenne in
the latter days of the Empire, and in attempting to
escape got lost in a swamp, where lie was eaten alive by
crabs. Ever since that awful crab-feed not more than a
dozen bogus notes have been presented at the Bank of
Prance altogether.

Tenders are called for the erection of a cyanide
plant, Georgetown. The returns for-the field this year are
23,291 tons, 23,000 ozs., but the warden’s report recently
issued says there is too much reason to believe that it is
the custom of the district to considerably reduce the ton-
age for the purpose of making the yields look better, a
practice not altogether unknown in N.S.W.

Manduratna Gold Trust Mine, better known as
the Junotion Iteefs, N.S.W. is now working on tribute.
It is a low-grade, fine-gold mine, worked by water-power.
It has one big thing—a nominal capital of £BOO,OOO. It
was born in the boom and died from the usual want of
gold.

to be debarred from having an interest in the new £1

into inaction by Bonanza Consols, Mt. Pooles, Marvels,

Dorados. Like the mining tipster, Sydney is played
out.

# # #
Tributes are the order of the day at Croydon
(Q.). This has been brought about by two causes—t'he
financial crash makes call paying an impossibility for the
majority of shareholders, and somehow the mines, as a
rule, only begin to pay when worked under the tribute
system. Many reasons, most of them libellous, are
advanced to account for this curious circumstance.
* # *
After draining shareholders of between .£2OOO
and £3009, most of which was put into surface improve-
ment, the No. 2 and 3 South Lady Isabelle (Croydon, Q.)
was recently let on tribute. Then, with the usual fatality
which attends Croydon shareholders, in less than a week
from the time the tribute began, payable stone was
struck. The tributors expect to make a respectable
fortune out of their bargain.
# * *
Lansell’s 180 mine, at Bendigo, is now down
8000 feet, and still sinking. Lansell is evidently in a fail-
way to settle a theological problem which has troubled
the world for 19 centuries.
# * #
The Iguana Extended Block (Croydon) started
work after its six months’ exemption on Nov. 24. A
crosscut is to be put in for the Ophir Block reef. The
latter claim, last year, paid £15,000 in dividends and then
lost the reef in the bottom. One crushing returned nearly
one pound of gold to the ton. Should the Extended he
successful iu its search it will give a tremendous impetus
to deep mining on Croydon.
# * *
The Russell River (Q.) settlers have 2500 coffee
trees now bearing the first crop. About half a pound to
the tree is the yield expected.

Galbraith, the Mfc. Morgan detective, passed
through Sydney a tew days since on the road to another
big mine in another big province. He is bound on the

* * #
Hannan’s Rush is worse than a thousand brick-
fields. Imagine 2000 men desperately earning a dear
living by dry-blowing, the dirt as red as flower-pots, the
temperature 130 in the sun ; and, in the absence of water,
hundreds of tons of dirt being dissipated by wind, some-
times supplied by the atmosphere and oftener by the
bellows. A recent escapee said to the writer this week:
“If hell is worse than Hannan’s Kush I’ll join the Salva-
tion Army.”

Mount Morgan November yield: 4300 tons,
80620z5. gold.
# # #
A Bendigo public meeting protests against the
proposal to throw the Whipstiok part of that district open
to agricultural selection. Several well-known lines of
reef are going in that direction, and the possibilities of
the Whipstiok are still far from exhausted.
* * *
Melbourne is in a bad way—worse, it is ad-
mitted, than Sydney— yet there is more Stock Exchange
business done there in one day than Sydney does in a
month. From eight to ten new mining uo-liability co.’s
are floated in Victoria every week. That number has not
been floated in N.S. W. this year. In Sydney sixty brokers
have sometimes not scored a single sale among them in
24 hours, and the share-dealers are getting thin and their
clothes greasy 1 Melbourne brokers are gamblers in a
gambling business, and, in the absence of clients, will
gamble with themselves; and, in the absence of money,
will gamble with bills. Sydney brokers are in the same
gambling business, but have a horror of risking their own
money, and, in the absence of clients, simply sit down
and gaze at each other. They should be called upon to
pull themselves together or go to gaol, as men having no
visible lawful means of support.

What sort of a dam have they at Mt. Allen ?
A few weeks back it was reported in Sydney that.it was
full ; now a wire is published, “ Water might last another
week.” Surely a mine where CSXoz. of amalgam is lifted
from the plates after two or three weeks crushing, should
lie made less dep ndent on water. Why not crush dry as
is done in all arid districts; particularly where the gold
is fine and the ore dry as at Mount Allen'?

In cutting a plat in the Mt. Lyell mine last
week, 70 tons of ore were taken out, containing nearly
100,000 oz. of silver, and valued at £14.000. Yet the pros-
pect failed to float in the badly-scared city of London.
* # *
The Mt. Gaban monthly divs. have changed
into a call. Pambula correspondents are discreetly silent.
Only the poor public remember the phrases—“As soon
as the plant is completed divs. will be resumed “There
is an enormous quantity of ore ready to take out.”
Better be without a plant than work one on calls.
* * m
Mini and party, of the Oakey Creek mine,
N.S.W., have had their Xmas crushing—lBo tons, 310 oz.
It is simply wonderful how this boat-shaped, narrow shoot
of gold quartz continues to go down. A little more than
a year ago this mine was sold for a song, under peculiar
circumstances. Since then the buyers, four hard-working
miners, have each netted some thousands. They have
also acquired the adjoining block, in which the shoot is
now being worked.
* * #
Omeo is another Vic. mining field on the mend.
The Good Friday mine recently crushed 100 tons for
2100 z., and several others are looking better. Bright,
too, is looking brighter than for many years. The old
Peabody mine has gone into the hands of an English co.,
and there are in many directions signs of a mining revival.
Victoria, generally, is working her mines for all they are
worth.

AN N.S.W. ELECTORAL NIGHTMARE.
“Jack” Rider, who died lately in Mel-
bourne, where he was a pressman for a quarter of
a century, was Hansard reporter in Fiji Parlia-
ment when J. S. Butters, of Melbourne, was
Speaker, under the pre-British system. Rider
was once found guilty of “breach of privilege’’
and locked-up in the “coal hole” for a period.
He used to tell yarns about the luxurious life he
led in gaol, and how sorry he was when the
session came to an end and he was released. Poor
Rider has been dying of dropsy for years, and at
last finished up in Melb. Hospital.
Admiral de Mello, the would-be Brazilian
king-maker, has a malodorous reputation. Dom
Pedro trusted him, and he joined the conspiracy
against that good old potentate, not from Republi-
can sentiment, but for the sake of hard cash. He
conspired against President Fonseca, and helped
to overturn that aged warrior. He conspired next
against Peixoto, and proclaimed young Pedro as
emperor. Now he announces that he has no
desire to abolish the Republic, so apparently he has
betrayed his own baby monarch also. At latest
advices he had escaped with difficulty from Rio
Janeiro with his fleet, and the Republican
squadron is after him, looking for his head.
That historic politician,
Vincent Pyke, returns to
Maoriland Parliament in his
66th year. He was a store-
keeper on a Victorian gold-
field in the old, gaudy,
riotous, hard-drinking days
of ’53. Then he was a mem-
ber of the nominee Council
in the pre-Government-by-
Contradiction days. He sat
in the first Victorian Assem-
bly as member for Castle-
mame, and later on was sent
to England as immigration
agent. Next he returned
to Victoria and became Com-
missioner of Customs in the
Nicholson Cabinet. In 1862,
he drifted to Maoriland
along with the rest of the
“ young iniquities ” that
race who staggered all the
old traditions of the pro-
foundly serious Scotchmen of
Otago, and painted the
solemn province a lurid crim-
son. He was a P.M. in the
Tuapeka district for ten years
or so, and after that, for 17
years, he was the stormiest
politician ever known in the
ante-Fish days of Maoriland.
Now he goes back to Parlia-
ment as an independent
member, so that he can bang
the furniture into ruins with-
out being hindered by party
obligations, and affably heave
bricks at everybody. People
who know Pyke are divided
into two classes—those who
would gladly lend him every
cent they have in the world,
and those who would go a
long way to see him hanged.
Barney Allen, the Mel-
bourne bookie, who, when
confronted by burglars in the
stilly night, lost no time in
handing over his keys and
valuables, says the fraternity
complimented him on his
coolness. Barney is evi-
dently imbued with that
fierce spirit of dare-devilry
which would lead its pos-
sessor to save his life even if he died for it.
Author J. M. Barrie once won a school-
prize offered by the girls to the boy with the
sweetest smile. Barrie is, so far, an exception
to a generally reliable rule. The boy with the
sweet smile and the famous turn-down collar —
that collar which, if it were turned down another
inch, would turn his shirt inside out —is, as a rule,
hanged very young.
It is rumoured in London that the Prince
of Wales has been attacked with facial paralysis,
that grotesque complaint which draws the features
round in the direction of the ear. At all events,
he has remained of late in unwonted seclusion, and
at his age the fate which hangs, in some shape or
other, over every short, stout person who has
lived hard all his life can’t be very far off.
The Duke of Westminster, income several
hundred thousands per year, dresses so shabbily
that his valet turns up his nose at his cast-off
garments- Bedford, Devonshire, Argyle (all
dukes), Isaac Holden, the richest man in the
Commons, Mackay, the “ Silver King ” of
America, each try to ape old, stout John Lucas,
of the N.S.W. Legislative Council, the original
colour of whose clothes can now hardly be told.
Mr. 0. G. Heydon, the recently-appointed
N.S.W. Attorney - General, ambitioned poli-
tics for some years, but failed to secure elec-
tion at Argyle, and subsequently at East Mait-
land. He has the reputation of being an
energetic prosecutor and laborious defender in
criminal cases, and a very persistent and careful
advocate in civil cases. He achieved some repute
in journalism during his early years at the Bar.
Cyril Haviland, the secretary of Sydney
School of Arts, who was arrested the other day,
on his own confession, for a serious abbreviation
in his accounts, was a spiritualist and a poet, in
addition to many other things. Also, he habitually
wore the far-away look of a man who communed
with beings of another world and was unfitted for
reliable arithmetic. A poetic spiritualist is pro-
bably about the worst combination for book-
keeping purposes that could be imagined.
George Ash, M.P. (S.A.),who is an articled
clerk in Premier Kingston’s office, in November
passed the third year examination fortheLL.B.
degree, taking first-class honors and the Stow
prize. He is credited with being the most in-
dustrious member of the Assembly, and he con-
scientiously reads every Bill and paper placed
before Parliament. He is a journalist whose
political career was forced upon him because a
squatter ruined him in a libel-action.
The recently-deceased Hon. Roger Moly-
neux was, a few years ago, well-known in Bohemian
circles in Sydney, where he eked out a precarious
subsistence by scribbling for Punch, &c. As a
linguist he had few superiors, and it was a treat to
hear him recite Hans Breitmann’a ballads. He
was consumptive and for years suffered greatly;
he died a few weeks ago in the comparative odour
of sanctity. His will has been proved by his
brother, Lord Sefton, at the large sum of 60s.
An old Sydney identity has just died at
Jersey, Channel Islands—Mrs. Thomas Goodall-
Gore, aged 76, daughter of John Kinchela, old-
time Attorney-General and a Supreme Court
Judge in the Thirties. Judge Kinchela is buried
at Liverpool (N.S.W.). When on the bench he
was deaf and nearly blind, infirmities which
induced the notorious Major Mudie, of “Castle
Forbes,’’ to jibe at him. Consequently the Major
was horsewhipped in George-street by the Judge’s
son.
Carter Harrison, the deceased Mayor of
Chicago, had been five times chief magistrate of
the Dibbs-cursed city. Prendergast, the oftice-

OLD MAN (whose daughter Mr. Jones is courting): “See here!

that the people of Chicago must be a poor-spirited

George Charles Hawker, the oldest legisla-
tor in S.A., so far as the years of his life are
concerned, has had a remarkable political record.
He was first elected to the Assembly for the
district of Victoria in January, 1858, and was
Speaker from April, 1860, to January, 1865, when
he dropped out of the House. He was re-elected
for Victoria in February, 1875, and resigned in
May, 1883, having, in the meanwhile, held
Ministerial office for nearly four years. He was
back again as member for North Adelaide in
April, 1884, and has filled the seat ever since,
despite many attempts to dislodge him. He is
almost a millionaire, but judiciously votes the
Democratic ticket on occasions.
The history of the Peel family is a melan-
choly illustration of the effect of hereditary dis-
tinction upon human character. Clever old
weaver Peel begat the bright political Robert
whose services were “ rewarded” with a baronetcy.
Robert’s heir inherited the brains of his ancestors,
but soon deteriorated into a raffish man of
pleasure. His title killed his ambition and made
of him a brilliant trifler, with just sufficient
enemy to pump out an eloquent Jingo speech at
widening intervals. Now that this second Robert
is a played-out buck, taking pride in little else
than his moustache, his son, young Bobby, figures
as a bankrupt who has already pawned his
reversionary interest in all the prospects remain-
ing to him as the heir of his fallen dad.
Almost every week the expectations of
smokers are raised by wildly-written advertise-
ments lauding some new brand of cigars, the
which upon sampling are found to be provocative
of nothing but bad words and disappointment.
There is, however, a silver lining to every cloud,
and the man who has invested his money in a box
of unsmokable atrocities is sure to learn—if he
doesn’t know it already—-that for delightful flavour
and excellence of manufacture Frossard’s Swiss
Cigars (25 in the bundle) are a permanent institu-
tion the world over.
When grim old Carlyle wrote his “Sartor
Resartus,” the walking-stick as an accessory to a
man’s garments did not strike him with sufficient
force to write the matter up properly. If poor
old Thomas were alive now and were to visit the
premises of Messrs. G. H. Smith & Co., at 302
George-street—the largest hat manufacturers and
importers in Sydney—his rugged old soul would
receive a shock, and he would realise how
important an item he had omitted. Endless
varieties of the article are displayed, such as stout
cherry, vine in many sorts and designs, malaccas,
black perricle root, black ivory tusks mounted on
partridge wood, etc., etc. All sorts of handsome
designs are shown in chamois hooka, ivory crutch,
and ebony. Umbrellas are also present in infinite
variety.
Judging from its enormous and unprece-
dented sale, Walker’s Whisky, and how to get a
bottle of it, seems to be the one subject that in-
terests the Australian public to the exclusion of
all other topics. And, after all, despite the hoary
old proverb—written by a man who, no doubt, only
drank home-made wine—about liquor stinging
like a serpent, and other things to that effect,
there can be no possible doubt that the man who
sticks to a good brand like Walker’s Whisky has
in him the elements of sound sense, kindliness,
and a healthful life; and cannot be anything else
but a good citizen and an ornament to his
country.

POLITICAL
POINTS
S.A. Government thinks of introducing
compulsory military service.
Premier Kingston (S.A.) has abandoned
his State Life Assurance Bill.
Parkes refers to the N.S.W. Treasury
bench as the Dibbs Government's “ besmeared and
dishonoured seats.”
“ Mines and Taters ” Slattery has agreed
to be N.S.W. Minister for Justice, for awhile, in
addition to his other duties.
Oscar Wilde : “To recommend thrift to
the poor is both grotesque and insulting.” Some-
thing for the National Ass to ponder over.
The Earl of Glasgow denies that he has
recommended Premier Seddon for a knighthood.
Seddon denies that he has recommended Glasgow
for anything
Mashonaland, the territory which the
British South Africa Co. has embezzled, is now
called Rhodesia, after Cecil Rhodes, the Capetown
Premier-brigand.
The farewell demonstration in honour of
Robt. Reid at Melb. Town Hall, was indeed a
ghastly joke—a jerry-built festival, founded on
soft goods and gas.
Microbes, it has been discovered by a
German scientist, can be fired from a rifle along
with the bullet. Every day the hoary romance of
glorious war is being sliced off in big junks.
Two hundred women have been put in
charge of as many small railway stations in Vic-
toria, whereby the department saves £30,000 a
year. When will we get a cheap female Ministry?
G. H. Reid: “Premier Dibbs is the
greatest political tramp and sundowner in the
country, and in all the 125 electorates there is not
one in which he can put the sole of his political
foot.”
The N.S.W, Aborigines Protection Board
receipts, for last quarter, amounted to £24 os.
10d., and the costs of collection were £lB 7s.
This left £5 13s. lOd. for Kaween Wikitoria’s
niggers.
Treasurer Playford, in S.A. Assembly:
“The former gardener at Government House got
£lOO, but the present man is willing to do the
work for £8 less.” Economy is the god of the
stupid man in No. 18 hoof-tanks.
Premier Dibbs (being interviewed by a
newspaper man who wanted to know what the
Government intended doing after its defeat) :
“ I’ve got nothing to tell you. You can just go
to a place lined with hot bricks ! ”
The Argus opposes the introduction of an
American railway expert to boss the traffic depart-
mentin the Vic. service: “ We advocate the policy
of Victorian experts for Victorian railways. ” How
would this policy apply to Victorian governors ?
A bill to nationally purchase the minerals
of Great Britain has been tabled by the British
Labour-members ; and Fegan, M.P., is to move
for the nationalisation of N.S.W. coal-mines, if
ever the Assembly gets into working order again.
That long practically-insolvent institution,
the N.S.W. Civil Service Superannuation Fund,
is now in the happy position of receiving £6OOO
and paying out £BOOO per month. The difference
comes out of the fast-fading capital, which is only
£480,000.
Since the Marquis of Salisbury has pro-
posed “an international agreement for the sup-
pression of Anarchism,” it behoves Mr. Andrews,
of Sydney, and the enterprising Tommy Dodd,
either to arm and entrench themselves, or submit
at once.
The S.A. Government is considering the
advisability of bringing in a bill to prevent law
appeals beyond the local Supreme Court. This is
one of the worst-needed reforms in Australia, and
it is to be hoped the other provinces will follow
Kingston’s lead.
At the last Q. elections, on polling day, a
naked Kanaka —at least he was so nearly naked
that it made no serious difference—with “Vote
for ” chalked on his back, was sent around a
Northern town. An obvious hint herein for
travelling showmen.
The cable states that Herr Somebody-or-
other has expressed his thanks to God for the
escape of the German Emperor and Chancellor
Caprivi. This is either an “ad,” for the Herr’s
piety, or it is meant to show that the Almighty
is having a good time.
During the recent Maoriland elections a
candidate was asked by a female voter if he was
in favour of a Bill to reduce the hours of labour.
To which he replied that if medical men were in
favour of the idea he certainly would support it.
Then a great silence ensued.
Victorian Speaker Tom Bent is reckoned
up by the Scottish Leader as an “ illiterate
market gardener,” who occasional!} 7 hangs his wig
and gown upon the knob of the mace whilst he
delivers “extraordinary harangues on things in
general.” This “ disgustin’ forrin’ parrygrarf,” as
Bent would call it, is really one for Thomas and
two for the House that elected him to the billet.
Any man has a right to be Speaker if he can get
there, and the burly old barnacle from Brighton
did get there.
In his recent Melbourne lecture entitled
“Twenty Years in Pentridge,” Mr. Hosking, a
retired warder of large experience, gave his em-
phatic testimony in favour of the abolition of the
lash. It brutalises both the giver (on the bench)
and the receiver (on the back).
Cabled that the U.S. Democratic party
demands the imposition of a duty on imported
coal and iron ore. This looks so like more Pro-
tection that that famous cabled message of con-
gratulation to President Cleveland seems more
than ever wasted and misplaced.
THE NATIONAL ASS.
It has no Head, and yet some say
They’ve heard the shapeless object bray !
It has no Legs and never rears.
But Flops and Brays—All Ears, All Ears !
Dipso.
Edmund Barton, Q.C., makes five ex-
Attorneys-General now in Parliament, the others
being Manning and Simpson, in the Council, and
Wise and Want, in the Assembly. In the Coun-
cil are three ex-Solicitors-General Fauoett,
O’Connor, and Salomons. Manning has held
both offices.
It is a curious sign of the times that the
Sydney Presbytery, the other day, rejected, by a
considerable majority, a motion calling on the
people of N.S. Wales to return none but strictly
pious candidates at next election. Even the
Presbytery seems to have lost its old faith in the
pious candidate.
Another foreign sailor, who fatally stabbed
a messmate, has been sentenced, at Sydney, by
Chief Justice Darley, to three years’ penal servi-
tude. At the same sittings a man charged with
housebreaking got ten years, chiefly, his Honor
said, because he “drew a knife” to prevent
arrest. Proputty again.
During the late Behring Sea trouble,
Herbert, U.S. Secretary for War, accepted the
services of King, the aeronaut, whose duty would
have been, in case of war, to hover over the
British fleet and drop bombs of dynamite on to
the decks. King professes to have mastered some
of the difficulties of balloon steering.

The Vic. Railway Commissioners announce
that for the future “ladies not engaged in busi-
ness ” may obtain periodical tickets over the
suburban lines at half-rates. Females, however,
who are working for a living do not share in this
concession. The Fat Woman is as much a political
institution as the Fat Man.
When Parkes, who turns with every politi-
cal sign, bids frantically for the votes of the
Active Service Brigade—the democracy or chaos
is coming right enough. People now young may
yet live to see Parkes the Anarchist carrying red
flags at the ripe age of 110, There is no end to
the old man’s possibilities.
The warm-blooded Kingston refused to
allow Symon, Q. 0., to appear nominally for the
Crown, but really on behalf of a private prose-
cutor, in S.A. Criminal Court. Symon appeared
nevertheless, and proved that Kingston had no
power to interfere. In this affair Kingston seems
to have made an ass of himself.
S.A. Legislative Council rejected the
Kingston land-taxation proposals. The same old
National Ass crowd voted against the measure :
Angas, Baker, Bos worth, Stirling, Warren, and
Duncan (squatters), Copley (farmer), Darling and
Simpson (capitalists), and Ward (pig vote). The
Government was beaten by one vote.
The rumour that John Hoolan, M.P., will
resign his Parliamentary seat in favour of Tom
Glassey, the Queensland Labour-leader, wears an
air of probability when considered in connection
with the recent reports concerning meteoric
showers of libel-writs against the Mundic Miner
(Georgetown), which vigorous publication is
owned by Hoolan.
Parkes (describing Dibbs and Co.) :
“Burglars . . . sharpers . . . heathen
Chinese . . . traitors . . . coveting des-
picably the mean patronage of the Crown of this
prostituted country . . . sticking in degraded
office and drawing salary . . . waiting to be
kicked out . . . the vulgar dramatic longings
of George Dibbs,” &c.
A strenuous attempt was made, when the
military vote was before the S.A. Assembly, to
wipe the forces out of existence altogether, but
the War party commanded a majority of one in
the test division. The Ministry is pledged, how-
ever, to put the knife in deeper, although already
the vote has been cut down from £46,000 in
1892-3 to .£23,000 for 1893-4.
The new Yankee Brown-Segmental gum
with a charge of 301 b. of smokeless powder, send B
a 601 b. shot at an initial velocity of 3130 ft. pe r
second. With armour-piercing projectiles the
gun will penetrate 19in. of iron and 13in. of steel
armour. Two of these persuasive weapons are on
board of Marshal Peixoto’s New York auxiliary
fleet, which hoped to corner De Mello’s squadron
in the narrow waters of Rio harbour.
Melb. stockbroker G. D. Meudell, who is,
or was recently, in London, booming private Fat
Man interests, has more than the usual gall of his
class. In a recent press interview, he attributed
all the disasters that followed the Melb. land-
boom to the “so-called Labour-party, whose
power, through want of funds, is broken for the
time being.” “The representatives of this party,”
he adds, “ have not been business men, under-
standing £ s. d.”
One Allen, an American senator, thought
he had got away with the jaw championship,
recently. He spoke without intermission from a
quarter-past five in the afternoon till eight o’clock
on the following morning, his only nourishment
being a cup of tea. A London paper, however,
brings the American gent, up with a round turn
and tells him that there is a man in N.S.W.
Assembly, named Neild, whose heels he couldn’t
see for dust.
The sudden prorogation of N.S.W. Parlia-
ment blocks two little bills which Neild, M.L.A.,
was just about to introduce. One was a short
measum providing that sheep should only be
slaughtered on Monday, so that they might enjoy
the advantages of the Sabbath before execution.
The’other enacted that, if a letter was exactly full
weight before the stamp was put on, and the
stamp made it overweight, the overweight should
not be charged for.

MINISTERIAL ITEM.
Robt. Reid, of Smellbourne, embarks on his “important mission.”
The cable-man had a bad attack of horror*
one day last week, and sent along an assortmen
of gory sensations. They included a dynamite
outrage in the French Chamber of Deputies; the
confession of the dynamiter; rumours of antici-
pated blasting operations at London and Wash-
ington ; a riot at Palermo, 14 rioters killed, and
the town-clerk and his wife seized and beheaded
by infuriated “socialists;” and the capture of a
Customs officer at Bari (Italy), and the burning of
him alive with kerosene accompaniment, on
account of his interference with a religious fes-
tival.
In the S.A. Electoral Bill, which has just
passed the Assembly for the third time since 1890,
are provisions fixing the maximum expenditure on
a contest at .£lOO, directing that all nominations
shall take place on one day, and preventing a
candidate from standing for more than one seat
at the same time. It also allows candidates to
talk up to midnight before polling day. At
present their mouths are shut when the nomina-
tions have been received. The measure also
enfranchises a number of Crown tenants who have
lost their votes, so far as the Upper House is
concerned, through a technicality under the exist-
ing Act.
Apropos of N.S.W. ex-Attorney-General
Barton’s disastrous brief in the Proudfoot case: J.
H. Symon, when Attorney-General of S.A. in
1881, accepted a brief in a big case against the
Ministry of which he was a member. No one
seemed to be especially shocked, and the matter
passed almost unnoticed. The proceeding, like
most objectionable things, was in accordance with
legal etiquette. Symon is a potent authority on
legal etiquette.
At the division which led to the practical
downfall of the Dibbs (N.S.W.) Government,
Minister Slattery voted in defence of the practice
of members of the Cabinet holding briefs against
the State. As Mr. Slattery, at that same moment,
was . acting as solicitor in the case of Seymour
against his colleague, the Minister for Works,
and consequently against the State, he, at least,
had the courage of his opinions.
almost every lawyer holding office in an Austra-
lian Cabinet has made it a practice, from time
immemorial, to take briefs against the Cabinet of
which he forms a part. The Bulletin would
now like to know if any legal Minister ever took
a brief in his private capacity, against himself in
his capacity. There doesn’t seem to be
anything in the etiquette of the profession to
prevent him doing so.
The principal feature of the great Foreign -
trade meeting, last week, at Sydney Protestant
Mall, was the presence of a crowd of bruisers,
bullies, _ pugilists, chuckers-out, and assorted
plug-uglies, whose business it was to bash out of
human shape anybody who didn’t seem to agree
with the principal speakers, and then throw him
in a maimed condition on the pavement. They
were an enthusiastic crowd, and one or two dis-
sentients had most of their clothes torn off and
were battered almost beyond recognition. It is
about time the police took cognisance not only of
the hired chucker-out, but also of his employer.
If they don’t, the beetle-browed brigade will pre-
sently run N.S.W. politics, and all public meet-
ings will be unanimous because the opposition has
been previously removed on a shutter to the
hospital.
N.S.W. Minister Copeland’s political
illustrations from the game of euchre would go
down badly with Gerald Anderson, Queensland
Under-Secretary for Education. A bush-teacher,
writing to the Education Department on one
occasion, used a figure of speech about “playing
the bower,” and great was the shock the epistle
gave to the office, Anderson desired to dismiss
the offender at once, but the Minister wrote a
brief and profane minute saying that the teacher’s
language “ was d impudent, but as otherwise
he was a of a good fellow, a formal rebuke
would be sufficient.” Accordingly, Anderson
committed his indignation to paper, and informed
the sinner that it was highly unbecoming in a man
of liberal education to express himself in the
language of a low, vulgar card-game, and that if
the offence were repeated the public service would
know him no more.
Present superannuation system in N.S.W.
public service is grossly unfair, and it is no
wonder that the younger barnacles object to it.
Unless a man is retrenched on the score of
incurable disease tie must patiently corrode in the
service until he is 60 years old before he can
claim anything; if he retires voluntarily before
that age he gets nothing at all, and the payments
that he has made into the fund are simply for-
feited. It was hoped that this forfeiture system
would keep the Superannuation Fund alive and
solvent; but it hasn’t. So many large, healthy-
looking men have been smitten in the prime of
life by immortal complaints—that is to say, by
hopeless diseases which cause the patient to live
( and draw his pension for ever afterwards—and
have thereupon retired to become an eternal
burden on the Fund, that it has never had a
chance from the verv homnnini'.
The Sydney Active Service Brigade is now
“laid for ” at public meetings by a rival organisa-
tion, the Mosquito Club, whose short and simple
political creed consists in throwing the Active
Service Brigade men down the steps whenever
opportunity offers. Naturally, this will result in
the formation of another organisation whose busi-
ness it will be to jump on the Mosquito Club if it
doesn’t leave the Active Service men alone.
Then the Foreign-trade hired bruiser will climb
on top of the other organisation, and the Protec-
tionist bruiser, who will presently develope him-
self, will attack the last-named from behind, and
the policeman, as the general representative of law
and order, will get on top of the heap. The only
certain consequence of all this is the flattening-out
of the Active Service gentleman who, in the
general scrum, will have $ll his friends and
enemies sitting impartially on his breathing
apparatus.

Gold discoveries. Very rich fields have
been opened at Southern Cross, Coolgardie, Mur-
chison, &c., in West Australia, which will be one
of the greatest gold-producing countries of the
world. Land in Perth and Albany, the principal
cities, is ridiculously low in price, and must cer-
tainly increase very much in value in the next
few years. One of the best investments at present
time is to secure a few lots. The Intercolonial
Investment Co., Ltd., 309 Pitt-street, Sydney,
offers exceptionally well-positioned blocks from
.£lO each on very easy terms, or for cash,
guaranteed title. The company has been estab-
lished in Sydney and Perth for many years, and
is well known throughout the colonies for straight
dealing. The manager is Mr. W. J. Douglas,
vho will be pleased to forward plans to any
address, and, having a personal knowledge of the
properties, will select faithfully for intending
purchasers where required.

The last letter written by Mrs. Knorr, the
Melbourne baby-assassin, before her trial, was
addressed to an old friend of her better days. In
it she implored him, if the trial went against her,
to take care of her helpless children ; also to call
at a certain shop where she had left two ostrich
feathers to be curled, and get them for her, so
that she might wear them in the dock. It was a
long letter full of agitated entreaty, but the
orphaned babes and the two ostrich feathers ran
all through it side by side.
* # #
The Hopetouna are ever troubled by
thoughts of diphtheria at their huge Government
House, and when the youthful heir developed sore
throat, the other week, his parents flew back to
town —they had been staying at Salmon Wilson’s
place—on the sombre wings of their old alarm.
The drainage of the “ ’Ouse ” is under suspicion.
Moreover, the odour of the adjacent Yarra, on a
calm, warm evening, is apt to make a young man’s
fancy lightly turn to thoughts of tomb.

Maoriland paper :—“ The introduction of
women into politics entails the possibility of
tremendous evil to the community in the near
future.” The anti-land-grab crank, the teetotal
fanatic, and the anarchist prophet will probably
be succeeded by a more horrible infliction as
follows :
The ‘ New Australia ” movement is bound
to boom merrily now. A lady in Balmain has
received a letter from a young female friend who
sailed in the Royal Tar, telling of moonlight
dances on the hurricane-deck of the steamer while
steaming up the Rio de la Plata, and the music of
sweet guitars, played, no doubt, by handsome
young Spanish-Americans with dark eyes and
long, curling black moustaches. And there was
also a very polite steamer officer who would
always say : “With your permission, senorita
and then the soldiers that formed the guard of
honour at the Theatre Nacional at Asuncion were
also very polite, and told the young lady from
Balmain that the Australian girls—and she in
particular—were very pretty. From this day
forth nine-tenths of the unmarried women of
Australia will want to go to Paraguay, and riot
among moonlight nights and guitars and hand-
some officers and such things, and the common-
place Australian man-emigrant that has dusty
boots and a grimy face and a bad hat will be
rejected and despised until the girl gets hungry
and vho black-moustached cavalier fades away
into dim distance.
SANTA CLAUS RECONSTRUCTED.
“I’m afraid this is about all I’ve got to give you this year!”
A few iteus from the gushful epistle
We all sleep on the floor, and have our meals gipsy-
fashion, in tlie open air. It is great fun. We eat oranges
all day and sit in the shade. . . Towards evening we
go for a swim in the creek. . . I ate twenty oranges
yesterday. [This young lady enjoys good health.] We
get a t icusand for a shilling and as many as we want for
nothing. . . We transhipped at Monte Video to a
beautiful steamer, the Rio Parana. We used to have dances
every night. The Spaniards would often play the guitar,
and when they didn’t our own fellows would play the
violin or concertina. At Asuncion crowds of people came
to welcome us, both Spaniards and Europeans. .
We camped two days in the theatre, an enormous brick
building built by King Lopez [“ King ” is good], and the
Spaniards used to bring us eggs, oranges, meat,
vegetables, and water, and would not hear of pay
ment. Some of the upper classes are remarkably
handsome. [No doubt some of the guitar push—
hence “ upper classes.”] . . Some of the boys used
to hand us in oranges and cigarettes through the
gates, and the soldiers would peel them for us with their
swords [shades of Lopez! and these swords imy have once
got polished by hewing human livers]. . . They would
tell us we were very pretty, made us very polite speeches.
. . One boy, about 19, told me he would be charmed
to visit me in my new home, and pushed a bundle of
cigars into my hand. . . All the women smoke, and I
suppose they thought we did.
Further on this young lady, who writes charm-
ingly throughout, says :
The Indians are dreadful thieves, but are not bad-
natured—they will bring you half-a-dozen eggs and steal
something twenty times the value. . . We are dread-
fully short of reading matter, unless we choose to wade
through books on Socialism and political economy and
such-like. We have organised a literary club and lam
one of the members. T . We have the funniest tents
you ever saw, some made of calico, but more of blankets,
bed-ticking and cretonne—mother’s is like Joseph’s coat
of many colours.

Miss Grace Norman, daughter of the
Queensland Governor, is to marry the Hon. G.
Wallop, private secretary to her father. Wallop
comes of the Portsmouth family, and is burdened
with a vast number of valuable initials.
The minute books of the first Australian
Masonic Lodge in N.S.W., recording proceedings
from 1820 to 1841, were recently discovered in a
lumber-room in the Lands Office and handed over
to the Lodge, now No. 1 Australian Constitution.
In the first years of this century, Henry Hayes,
transported for abduction, established a Masonic
Lodge, for which offence he was lagged to Van
Diemen’s Land.
Sir Edwin Arnold,
lecturing on the beauty of
cheerfulness, the other day,
couldn’t refrain from drag-
ging in a reference to Princess
Alice who “died so divinely
from the kisses of her sick
child.” We have been
nauseated with that “touch-
ing ” royal incident. Queen
Victoria’s daughter did noth-
ing that any other mother
wouldn’t do. She nursed
her child, and kissed the
patient, and perhaps inhaled
fever-germs during the em-
brace. She, the mother,
died from the fever because
she was a princess who “lived
high.” The writer has seen
a poor woman hugging to
her breast a baby covered
with small-pox sores. Pro-
bably she didn’t die of that
disease. She was too starved
to catch it.

A definition of a naughty
story : “A story which one
woman would enjoy, though
two women would be dread-
fully shocked.”
Sir Christopher Teesdale, Y.C., who died
the other day, had been a Court flunkey for 30
years or more, though he ranked as one of the
youngest of ‘‘Crimean veterans.” Before drop-
ping into the narnby billet of Master of the
Ceremonies to his Gracious Queen, he had a long
term at “Equerrying” for Albert Edward, who
never went anywhere without “ Colonel Teesdale
in attendance.” An imposing person was Tees-
dale. The writer remembers him sending for the
head waiter at a Scarborough hotel, to ask that
overawed menial what the devil he meant by
allowing flies in the dining-room. There was only
one fly to be seen, but it had happened to buzz
around the kernel’s soup, and the kernel wouldn’t
have it, by Heaven ! Subsequently, the manager
arrived with the main body of the hotel staff, and
ran the demnition fly to earth. This incident
shows the magnitude of the things that go to
make up a Royal equerry’s daily life.
The first woman ever thrown out of a
political meeting in Maoriland was landed out
on the crown of her bonnet, the other day, at
Wellington. Being inflated with her new dignity
as a female elector the henfranchised one per-
sisted in interrupting the candidate on the plat-
form, and shaking a somewhat withered gamp at
him with violent objurgations: The meeting
stood it patiently for a while, but at last it could
stand it no longer, and the air was rent with a
cry of “ heave her out.” Then, as she flatly
refused to be quiet, she was taken up as gently as
circumstances would permit, and heaved. There
was a vision of a bonnet with two broken feathers
in it, an agitated brown skirt, two darned stock-
ings of uncertain hue, a faded umbrella rising and
falling in the melee, a hysterical voice telling the
assailants that they were no gentlemen, and the
first ejected she-voter in Australasia was urged
down the steps and left weeping on the pavement.

yers frantically argu-

and the lady who had
been- prodded with the umbrella in tears and great
indignation.

The Gaulois bewails the demoralisation
of French kitchen poetry by the savage influence
of British taste. “ A French 090 k who goes to
London generally loses half his ability in a
twelvemonth,” it seems. The gourmands of INew
Babylon are addicted to great masses of plain
food, the preparation of which is bound to blunt
the fine edge of culinary art. And, then, their
brutal hankering after horrible sauces, called the
Worcestershire or the Anchovies ! Mong Doo .
The gros bifsteak and the sauce of Worcestershire
are fatal to all that is beautiful and sublime in the
teekling of the palate.
# • •
Very Rev. and very ancient Dean Macart-
ney introduced a touch of religious melodrama
into his performance at Melbourne pro-Cathedral,
the other Sunday morning. Staggering under
the tropical heat, the old gentleman of 95 next
birthday had to be assisted all the way up to the
pulpit. He got through his sermon without a
hitch, and then added a solemn postscript, “Dear
brethren, I have come here to-day in much
weakness to endeavour to warn you. I feel that
my days for witnessing the truth are very nearly
ended.” It goes without saying that the congre-
gation was visibly impressed, but there was
nothing strictly startling in the Dean’s statement.
Intelligent people don’t need to be warned that
clergymen are not immortal, and as regards the
Macartney idea of “the truth,” it is far too
brimstoney to convince unemotional persons.
Moreover, no preacher is entitled to support weak
arguments with illogical references to his extreme
age, and the Dean’s obstinate old faith in a red-
hot Sheol is not a bit more worthy of childlike
acceptation even if his years were 600 in number.
* # #
The great success of the Victorian Sunday
excursion trains is probably the beginning of the
end of the desolate Melbourne Sabbath. Nothing
outside the great desert of Sahara was ever
sadder or more depressing than that awful Day
of Rest. It was an utter desolation—a long
vision of deserted streets with no rational amuse-
ment in it, no libraries, reading-rooms, picture
galleries, concerts, no hope or joy or brightness
or recreation—nothing but occasional discordant
church-bells and bricks and mortar. The horrible
silence and iron-greyness of everything ate into
the soul. Everything was suspended except the
Yarra smells, and the interest on one’s over-
draft, and the desire to commit suicide and get
away from it all. The generation of bank-
wreckers which grew up under the influence of
Melbourne Sabbatarianism were in keeping with
the rest of the system. Like it they were grim
and serious and of a dull-brown cast of soul, and
their morals were of the kind that grow up in a
community which is always sneaking silently
round to the back-door of the public-house, and
breathing mysterious pass-words through the key-
hole that it may get in surreptitiously and
whisperingly drink muddy beer in a room where
all the blinds are down. Probably the Sabbath
as it is by the Yarra has done more than anything
else to undermine the morals of Victoria.

In Australia, says the Colonies and India,
skipping diversions are all the fashion among the
haut ton. They are—among the officials con-
nected with Australian banking and financial
institutions. Hardly a day passes without some
new banker taking skipping exercise.
• # #
Mrs. Lynn Linton goes for the cigarette
woman :
It is an uncleanly habit and unappetising. It tinges
the teeth and it fouls the fingers ; while lips heated and a
breath heavy with the scent of stale tobacco are surely
less delightful than the fragant freshness which belongs
to youth and is associated with beauty in a woman. And,
do what she will, the scent clings, and more persistently
about her than about a man. In her long hair alone the
odour of tobacco is predominant an odour which all her
specifics are powerless to banish in favour of something
more subtle and enticing. Severe smokers among men
are offensive enough, Heaven knows! Add to them
severe smokers among women, and we shall have a com-
bination of disgust, a reduplication of distastefulness.
Think of a woman nursing her baby while smoking a
cigar; your cook dropping the ashes of her pipe into the
stew; your lady’s-maid burning little round pinholes in
your brocade ; all the thousand and one household duties
which fall to the share of women, interrupted or damaged
by the universal pipe.

The godly men who run the parish church
at King-Edward in Aberdeenshire, Scotland,
being in want of anew shepherd, advertised, and
the following printed circular was sent to the men
who were named as references by the applicants :
King-Edward, July 8, 1893.
Sir, —The King-Edward Congregational Committee re-
quest that you will confidentially favour them with in-
formation on the following matters regarding Rev. Mr.
, who has applied for the Church of King-Edward :
1. Age.
2. Height.
3. Physical strength and health.
4. Personal appearance and manners.
6. Father’s occupation.
6. Freedom from defect or deformity.
7. Voice—whether loud, low, harsh, monotonous, whin-
ing, drawling, squeaky, provincial in accent, dis
tinctly heard in a large church.
8. Popularity in his present situation—causes.
9. Sunday School work.
10. Music—does he sing or play on any instrument.
11. Visitation—does he visit in the parish where he lives,
and do you believe that if appointed he would visit
the whole parish twice a year ?
12. Temperance—is he an abstainer from spirituous
liquors,; or, if not an abstainer, does he abstain
from spirituous liquors in his ministerial visitations:
and is he strictly temperate always at other timea
and has he always been so?
—I am, dear sir, yours faithfully,
(Signed) George Barclay.
Session Clerk
This is pretty comprehensive, anyhow.

One of the features of the Maoriland elec-
tion was the house-father who marched his wife
and daughters to the poll, and insisted on going
along with each of them into the little box, that
looks like the place where one secretly negotiates
a loan from Mosenstein on the family spoons, to
supervise the filling of the ballot paper. One old
gentleman, who evidently bad bis household in a
good state of discipline, had twice to be removed
out of the box by violence, and then he indignantly
accused the returning officer of breaking up the
peace of families and generally disturbing hi*
happy home.
Not empire-lust, nor greed for spoil,
By Mammon torn from those who toil,
Nor curse bacchantio;
Not the sharp rush of blood-stained feet
Of Anarchy through city’s street;
No tale romantic,
To show the future how we fell
The historian of our race shall tell,
In tome pedantic.
He’ll point, when on us he descants,
To woman reaching for man’s pants
With straggles frantic.
C.A.C.
THE CONSPIRACY.
We sing a sombre song
Of plots to murder Dibbs.
A Hindoo came along
Enquiring for “ His Nibs.”
He winked his heathen eye
And muttered, “Caitiff knave I
I’ll kill him presently
In fact, I’ve dug his grave.”
A hero stood on guard
At good Sir George’s door:
He viewed the stranger hard
(O, lynx-eyed janitor !)
And saw that he was armed—
Observed a pistol large.
“Police 1” he cried, alarmed,
“ Here, take this wretch in charge.”
They seized the weapon grim,
Which gave them quite a shock
In each official limb.
The hammer wouldn’t cook.
No little ounce of lead
The barrel did contain:
“ Now, praise the Lord !” they said,
“We all can breathe again.”
Sir Dibbs he heard as how
The miscreant was caught;
He wiped his clammy brow,
But did not go distraught.
Though warnings he had had
Of his impending doom.
He cried in accents sad—
“ Bring Guy Fawkes to my room."
There, there the villain stood,
Cut off from hope of flight.
“ What want you with my blood?”
Exclaimed the hardy knight.
But Guy Fawkes held his tongue,
Made no reply at all.
No doubt he will get hung,
And like a soldier fall.
Sir Dibits is still alive,
Too brave to show dismay ;
How long he will survive
The poet cannot say.
The plot to shoot him down
Is being plotted still;
He’li die to save the Crown-
Poor man 1 we know he will.

And at Newcastle, Brisbane and Toowoomba,
In a Melb. city police-court, t’other day
the Law’s assininity broke out in a quite unex-
pected place. An accused person, under remand
couldn’t be admitted to bail for the reason that
his bondsman was unable to provide any better
security than hard cash. The Law, it seems has
no power to regard cash “on the nail ” as a satis-
factory equivalent for a paper claim against house-
hold property or stock-in-trade. And since the
would-be bondsman in this case possessed neither
houses with a mortgage on them, nor goods of
doubtful value, and was merely prepared to plank
down the substance of .£5OO instead of the shadow
of a like amount, the Law declined his bag of
specie, and the prisoner was removed in custody
according to the chuckle-headed statoots.

* * #
The woman-vote at the recent Maoriland
election was gall and wormwood to the wife of
many an old member. As a rule, the wife of a
politician who had a reasonably sure seat was
wont to give herself airs of importance, and snort
contemptuously at the lady on the other side of
the fence. But the female vote changed all that,
and she had to put on her best bonnet and go
round and abase herself to the grocer’s wife.and
the wash-lady and other persons of inferior status.
As a rule, she had a hard time of it. One old
politician s wife began by calling on the woman
next door and asking for her vote. “ You know,”
she said, with a winning smile, “we have been
near neighbours for many years.” “Yes, we
have, certainly, but we have never spoken to each
other till to-day. <7ood-day.” Then the door
banged.
* # *
A Melbourne policeman lately lost his
false teeth in a swimming-bath—-he had been
piddling about in the shallow end, and after he
came out he went and looked with an awe-stricken
expression at the deep end, and while he was
yawning in a somewhat ample fashion, the eating
apparatus dropped into the water. He gave the
bath attendant 30s. to dive and recover them.
This suggests a whole lot of things. An aged
constable with artificial shop-teeth isn’t likely to
be a very active man in pursuit of a criminal. A
constable who won’t dive for his own teeth, looks
like a man who is hardly fitted for a risky calling.
And a constable who has 30s. to give away in that
reckless fashion must have been on night-duty a
good deal, and have made the best use of his
opportunities.

The London Daily Chronicle, in review-
ing Grace Langford’s “Werona: an Australian
Romance,” implies that its defects are atoned for
by one thing—“ the snobbishness of the Austra-
lian elite is mercilessly whipped.”

Latest suicide : Walter Thorn, an 11-year
old youngster, at St. Lawrence (Q.). “Tired of
life/’he left word, was the reason for his depar-
ture.
# * »
Some enemy has forwarded to The Bulle-
tin a copy of a neatly got-up little book called
“The Cornstalk : His Habits and Habitats,” by
Whaks Li Kell, which is intended to be a wildly
humoristic satire on all phases of Australian life.
This, however, it just misses, and the author gives,
instead, just 165 pages of Dead Sea drivelling, with
here and there hoary old English jokes uprearing
their horrid fronts like the weed-covered and
barnaely piles of an abandoned wharf. The most
notable atrocity of the lot is a kind of poetic
treadmill of 117 verses. The book is published by
Troedel, Cooper & Co. with “all rights reserved.”
This, for the sake of our common humanity, is
good news.

Last week, in Townsville (Q.), a gentle-
man just discharged from the local gaol (where he
had been in retreat in re larceny of a cheque) im-
mediately partook of many drinks, engaged a cab,
and drove up to the Court-house, with a friend.
He saw the B.M. and asked for a free pass to the
Towers, “ where he owned a valuable mine.” He
also introduced his friend, and wanted him to be
franked along by rail as a wages-rnan. The P.M.
didn’t see it, however, and the scheme fell
through.
* # #
One day last week Magistrate Smithers,
at Redfern Police-court, sentenced a man to one
month’s imprisonment for giving his wife an ugly
gasli on the side of the face with an axe ! Truly
wives are cheap in N.S.W.
A man who was lately arrested in London
for stealing a carpet-bag asked the mercy of the
court, “as he was just on the point of sailing for
Sydney, where he had a relative high up in a
certain banking institution.” “What is his
name?” asked the magistrate, with a doubtful
voice. “Mr. ,” was the reply. “Ha!”
said his Worship, taking up a newspaper, “I have
his name here ” (a proud smile lit up the prisoner’s
face); “he’s just got five years. I couldn’t let
you go to him. Three months.”

The N.S.W. artillery-men had an exciting
time on Saturday afternoon with gun practice at
Sydney Heads. They fired at fixed targets and
at moving targets, and they fired promiscuously
at things in general. One of The Bulletin staff
was sunning himself in the street at Watson’s
Bay, and they fired between his legs with the
Nordenfeldt gun. But for the providential cir-
cumstance that he is slightly bowed in the
extremities, there would have been a vacancy in
this office.

A police-court conviction bars a person out
of the Melbourne Benevolent Asylum. A woman
found in one of the local parks exhausted from
privation and exposure, the other day, was arrested
on the good old charge of “insulting behaviour.”
bhe had to be sent to gaol. The Asylum would
not receive her because she had been arrested once
before under exactly similar circumstances, and
convicted of “insulting behaviour.” Charity by
machinery is a delightful thing.

The Jin de siecle society woman is thus
caressingly alluded to by Sarah Grand, the
authoress of “The Heavenly Twins” :
For vulgarity, for boldness, for folly, ignorance, want
of principle, petty weakness, intrigue, and positive vice
you must go to the average society woman. Her one
motive is self-seeking. She is a bad wife, a bad mother,
and a false friend. For intellect, she has a fair supply of
shrewdness and cunning ; for religion, a rotten con-
glomerate of emotional superstitions that do not improve
her conduct; for virtue, the hope of not being found out;
while for charity, good feeling, modesty, and every
womanly attribute she substitutes tact—the tact to
respond outwardly to what she sees is required of her by
different people.

An important debate was in progress in a
certain Australasian Parliament, and the wife of
a Minister was in the gallery, anxiously waiting
to know the fate of the Cabinet. By-and-bye, a
red-nosed and aggressive-looking Oppositionist
rose to speak in a thick voice, like a man with his
mouth full of roast crab. *' He’s drunk,” said the
Minister’s wife in a whisper to a lady companion.
“Oh, no,” said the other, in a shocked tone,
“ he’s only excited.” “He’s drunk, I tell you;
•do you think I kept a public-house for over twenty
years and don’t know a drunk man when I see
■one.”

Property makes us acquainted with strange
bedfellows.
Purves, in the Speight v. Syme case, cross-
examining witness Archer who had been lolling in
the box with an exasperating abandon : “ Would
you like a bed ?” “Eh !” “ Would you like to
lie down?” “Oh, no.” “Then straighten your-
self up, sir,” returned J.L.P. sternly. Collapse of
Archer, mentally but not physically.

That alleged syndicate for the capturing
of Jabez Spencer Balfour, “alive or dead,” has
frightened the Gladstone Government into an
extradition treaty with the Argentine Republic.
From the first it was declared that scandalous
“ reasons ” accounted for Balfour’s safety in a
country which would willingly arrange the afore-
said treaty if it were warmly desired at the
British end. No such warm desire was shown
until the private kidnapping of Jabez became a
matter of public discussion, whereupon Lord
Rosebery moved in the matter. Now it is to be
presumed that Jabez Balfour will also move in the
matter and find another place of refuge.

Dr. Teague, insolvent, states that he pur-
chased certain East Melbourne property at public
auction for >£9500, “a far less price than the
probate-duty assessed upon it six months before,”
yet the present mortgagees value their security at
.£3960 only, showing a “ depreciation in value ”
of £5540 since the date of purchase. When will
these good people cease to use the word “ value ”
in connection with land-boom prices ?

to be suffering from measles, and had at once to
be removed to her home and isolated.” Can any-

men were fined .£16,870 apiece, with the alterna-

Editor Bulletin, — A correspondent asks
in your issue of 16th Sept., “How those letter
titles are conferred ?” The answer is simple. By
paying two guineas to the Royal Geographical
Society one can be made in 24 hours a F.R.G.S.
Isn’t every advertising Anglo-Australian one?
The raison d'etre of all these pretentious societies
is £ s. d., which must be got anyhow. Is not
almost every advertising Anglo-Australian also
‘F.L.S.,” which means “Fellow of the Society
of Literature”? Any ambitious Australian can
have a whole alphabet tacked to his name if he
will but come down with the dust. For the
modest sum of one sov. he may even become a
non-resident Fellow of the Imperial Institute
and claim the proud title of “F.I. Inst.” The
Royal Society is the only one which there is any
difficulty in getting admittance to, and yet even
amongst that learned body there are scores of
humbugs.—E. J. Jerrems, London, Nov. 14.

“ A man never looks to see if there is a
woman under the bed.’*— American paper.
An unpublished incident of the visit of
the Russian sailors to Paris. Two days after they
had quitted touring the Parisian shops and kissing
the shop-girls in rows, a dead woman was brought
from the Seine to the morgue. Her underclothing
was made entirely out of a Russian flag.

A gentleman with a thirst on him turned
up in Melbourne police-court, the other day, under
peculiar circumstances. He was passing a big
plate-glass window, when he saw what he thought
was a very old friend, and he rushed up to shake
hands with him. The friend rushed at the same
moment. Then there was a smash, and he found
himself wallowing among the wreckage of a
window. He never saw the old friend afterwards ;
presumably that deceptive person had gone away
while he was busy picking large sheets erf plate-
glass out of his system. Fined ss.

The New Zealand Elections.
The elections now progressing in New Zealand
mark an epoch in the advance of the world’s
civilisation. From what vve hear there will be no
cause to regret the innovation in politics now
inaugurated; and we shall hail with delight the
realisation of another proof of the assertion that
woman is equally competent with man to conduct
and control the multifarious items that make up
our existence. But there is a subject of greater
importance even than those two momentous
questions, and that is the enfranchisement of the
body, for what is the power to vote compared with
the exquisite felicity of experiencing the pulsations
of healthy blood percolating through a system
untainted by disease, and uncontaminated by in-
fection of any kind ? And yet there are thousands
who, through careless indifference, or indolent
perversity, fail to avail themselves of the great
medicinal remedy and natural restorative, “Mar-
shall’s Tonic Nervine.” That vitalizing agent
subjugates disease, overcomes weakness, replen-
ishes healthy vigour, and emancipates the patient
from the seeds of death, so that after a short
course of the remedy he becomes enfranchised,
both in mind and body. Another grateful witness
to its value, Mr. Thomas Green, bookbinder,
Shephard-street, Marrickville, writes: “Having
been suffering from general weakness for several
years, I got no relief from any medicine I took
till I tried Marshall’s Tonic Nervine. Then, from
the first, I felt the greatest benefit, felt strong and
healthy again. You may publish this for others’
benefit, if you choose, as I think they ought to
know.’
"Vesuvius in Flames.”
One day an Englishman who had become a resid-
ent in Naples was astounded and charmed at the
sight of the burning mountain. The appalling
grandeur of the thing intoxicated him with delight.
The terrific explosions, accompanied as they were
by the projection of huge boulders and burning
lava, presented a scene of weird and awful propor-
tions. From that moment he knew of nothing
half so grand as Vesuvius inflames. In thecours,
of years he left Italy and took up his abode in the
States. One day he met an American who boasted
very exultantly of his country. In a little while
each found himself trying to outpace the other in
the relation of the most wonderful thing he had
witnessed. The sublimest thing the Englishman
could remember was the mountain conflagration.
The Yankee gave a chilling smile, shrugged his
shoulders, and waited half impatiently for the end
of the story. With a cynical and contemptuous
air he said, “ Look here, stranger, did vou ever
see the Ealls?” “Falls; no.” “ Ah,, I thought
so,” replied the man of the stars and stripes.
‘ Now, my friend, you go back to Naples, and
bring Vesuvius over here, bag and baggage, rocks,
thunder, lava, and all the paraphernalia of the old
spitfire, and place it under the falls, and Niagara
will pour such a volume of water into its belching
mouth, that in less than a week not a scrap of fire
will be left.” “Impossible,” the Britisher ex-
claimed. “Its fires would send your billowing
cascades into huge clouds of steam and make
another crater in its place.” After all, Vesuvius
with its fires is as nothing when compared to the
hidden flames that burn in man. Internal shock
rends his frame. Destroying forces continue their
work until met by Warner’s “Safe” Cure,
which paralyses their strength and finally delivers
the sufferer from their grip. No matter how
emaciated or depleted the system, Warner’s
“Safe” Cure rebuilds the broken channels, re-
pairs the shattered system and rejuvenates the
whole body.
