My Dear Moorabinda—
Miss Stewart sported one good-looking dress in
“Princess Ida” at Sydney Lyceum— a cream-
coloured brocade, cut a la Essie Jenyns as J uliet,
with a cream-colour court train, lined with dull-
red shawl-pattern, the dress lightened with regular
Golcondas of stage “ jules.” It was the most eye-
arresting turn-out on the zoological-looking stage,
where the recumbent damsels in spots and stripes
appeared like so many leopards and zebras. Lady
Blanche —in the plentiful person of Elsie Cameron
—appeared to be dressed in mackintosh stuff,
and was as shiny as a tree lizard.
The blue-and-white pattern of Miss Young’s
gaberdine suggested cheap crockery-wa.re. By
the way, I really think a damsel who is, so to
speak, well-developed—to put it meekly—ought
never to wear a straight, pinaforish dress, but if
she must, something ought to be let out at the
hem to allow for the development. May Pollard,
in violet velvet and rich brocade, produced a very
paintable effect; and when the glittering Amazons
took the stage in the last screech, it almost looked
as if the pantomime was upon us.
The men in “ Ida ” were smudgily dressed, I
thought —worth eight-and-six per 3'ard and all
that, but seedy colours. The trio of adventurous
lordlings were as dingy as so many mopokes. The
assumption of girls’ robes on the stage gave rise to
a little horse-play—but it was interesting to see
how the phenomenally-neat Bracy put his_ robe
and cravat on, as though he had a dozen mirrors
to go by, while the hastily put-on gowns of the
other two looked as tho’ somebody had dressed
them with a stable-fork. “Princess Ida” was
soulfully staged, and the only contretemps oc-
curred when Melissa hugged Fiorian on the prompt
side, and her plumed helmet tumbled off with a
sound of falling fire-irons.
That perspiring adage, “ If at first you don’t
succeed, try again,” has beeff once more verified
by the election of Dr. Grace Robinson to the
onerous position of resident medical officer at the
new place d'accouchees (these things sound ever so
much better in French—at least, to the uneman-
cipated ear) at “Floodgate,” Botany - street,
Moore Park, Sydney. Dr. Robinson, it will be
remembered, is the lady over whose non-election
to a similar position at the women-managed
Children’s Hospital, at the Glebe, such a nice
little battle waged, some months ago.
Dr. Robinson is a slight, pale, fair and very
juvenile-looking resident medico in petticoats, and
will go down to posterity as the very first lady to
have medical charge of a public institution in
Australia. Besides which Dr. Robinson has a
lovely chance to cover herself with grisly glory
and become the ladies’ doctor par excellence on
the strength of the opportunities for practising
she’ll have. It is a great deal to know for certain
that your particular Death-dodger isn’t getting
rusty from disuse in the interim when you don’t
happen to want a blue pill.
I confess I am not astonished to hear that the
Sydney branch of the Girls’ Friendly Society is at
ebb tide pecuniarily, and that the attendance has
dwindled down several hundreds since ’92. Con-
sequently great efforts are being made in social
circles to raise the necessary needful, and for a
time it is probable that the G.F.S. will take the
hat from the Women’s College or the Thirlmere
Hospitals and other pet enthusiasms.
Expressed in a verbal nutshell, the G.F.S. is
home for itinerant or stationary virtue —the
of institution which flourishes best in a more
rigorous climate than this, and under more oppres-
sive social conditions. The Australian girl, is very
seldom frost-bitten, and hardly ever helpless, and
she goes with a calm front from Cape Yorke to
Wilson’s Promontory if necessary, playing off her
own bat all the way. She doesn’t need a Blue
Ribbon lady in a poke bonnet to meet her at the
railway station, and take her in charge as if she
had to be fed by hand and didn’t know enough to
wash her face without getting soap in her eyes.
Therefore, in a land where maidenhood is more
rampant than retiring, the coddling club fades
away to a fad, and the news that a cold frost is
settling over the Girls’ Friendly Society doesn’t
even provoke the mildest sort of surprise.
Are any big guns threatening Sydney during
the next civic year? Because, if they aren’t, why
did Alderman Burdekin suddenly want the
Mayoral stipend raised five centuries ? On Mayor
Mannings re-re-re-re-election (he is four times
Mayor), the other day, there was quite a pow-
wow, I b dieve, as to whether a man could enter-
tain society on £lOOO a year, and, as the motion
was lost, I suppose the concensus of opinion was
that he can.
But, of course, it all depends. If you (my
remarks are now addressed exclusively to Mayors)
are “at home” with champagne to all the world
and his wife at frequent periods, and give many
small, medium, and large hops in the season, then
a “cool thou.” wouldn’t cover the gas-bill; but
entertaining like that is a matter of taste. There
are no by-laws that I know of ordering “ His
Washup ” to give so many capers per season, and
the entertainment of distinguished deadbeats
doesn’t cost very much. A Mayor can always
turn ’em loose in the Town Hall and sool the
organ on to them, and if he includes corkscrews in
the programme it’s quite his own affair.
But, after all, I really think that a City
Father who wanted to save_ enough out of his
official screw to buy a breast-pin with would have
to pose as a rabid teetotaller, and force flat
lemonade on his tormentors for the good of their
souls—and his pocket. As a matter of fact, the
mayoral salary should be elastic. For instance, a
bleeding municipality would be only too pleased
to pay a “Jules Mumm ” mayor on the higher
scale—while a Blue Ribbon potentate, of whom
the papers might report, “ Mayor Dash then
ordered ginger-beer to be opened,” ought certainly
to get through at a lower rate.
The mere fact that half the medicine-men in
Sydney recommend people to dress in the new
cellular cloth (the central dep6t is opposite
Hunter-street, in George-street) shows that the
“ faculty ” consider it hygienic—but even if they
didn’t, I think the cloth would tempt people—it
is so nice-looking. The fabric itself is a soft-
looking mesh, canvas pattern, and keeps the
wearer at an even temperature in a l l seasons; and,
unlike most “sanitary” clothing, which is a
bilious drab calculated to make even Venus lose
confidence in herself at the mirror, the new
stuff can be had in all the most becoming colours
in the prism. Every sub-rosa garment in man’s
or woman’s wardrobe —including hose and venti-
lated corsets of Parisian cut—is displayed at the
cellular depdt at most moderate figures, besides
which “ industrious ants ” who cut out and cobble
their own and their male relatives’ under-wear can
buy the cloth by the yard if they like.
Some people put things very plainly—even
hideously. A recent marriage advt. in the
S.M.H. ended with the mystic advice, “See
Thes. XIV., xx.,” or figures to that effect —I have
forgotten the exact numerals. When somebody
looked up the text it was discovered to be “ Pray
for us.” But I think he made a mistake, after all.
I think he really meant “Pray for ME.”

T ABIES’ Superfluous Hair effectually removed off

superfluous hair removed off the face strikes me as
being decidedly inadequate. I once knew a
woman who actually couldn’t wear thin hose of
any kind because of the—but the rest of the story
is unavoidably postponed.
I have received from the V.R.C. a most deli-
cately got-up booklet, in die-away tints of grey
and gold and sunset-pink, containing (I mean the
book does) a series of rather smudgy photos, of
the beautiful wild-flower panels done by Mrs.
F. C. Rowan, in the Governor’s pot-plant-haunted
lunch-room at Flemington. It is a classic pam-
uhlet with stiff, old Roman names (supplied from
the inexhaustible well of Von Meuller’s botanical
lore) set forth on the leaf facing the exhibit—
with something of the depressing of the
name-pegs in the Gardens. To my mind the
Latin elaborations spoil the book.
The air has been metaphorically black with
pupils’ concerts lately, and exhausted juniors, in
a fearful state ol frizz and sash-end, have monopo-
lized Sydney platforms, while parents and friends
surged on the ground floor, and the proud mamma
whose darling had a star part brought her tea in a
bag to be sure of a front seat.
Madame Kellerman’s audit took four days to
fag through at the Y.M.C.A., and was the usual
long-drawn-out success, ending in the presentation
of an escritoire to the gifted teacheress. But the
cleverest part about it was the absence of those
flowers which are usually rushed over the foot-
lights to the pupils. There are few things more
absurd than to see a nervous girl who has just
managed to get through the “ Battle of Prague ”
alive, being bouquetted at the finish. It almost
looks as if the crowd was beflowering her for
kindly leaving off !
Mons. Deslouis had a pupil’s concert on the
13th, when some of the singing was very good and
all the items were classic—a presentation of a gold
watch-chain and pendant to Mons. Deslouis
diversifying the programme. But the thing I
enjoyed was the recitation by Marcel Deslouis of
his own poem, “ Que chantent les Cloches.”
Mons Marcel is fresh caught from Paris, and his
recitation was a revelation.
The real troubles of Don Lane, pioneer of the
brand-New Australians, are about to begin, for it
is reported that the Monte Video papers find the
Australian women “exceptionally beautiful and
comely.” Evidently the man in the sombrero
(whom I prophesied about) has already fixed his
bold, black eye on the visitors, and each male
relative is hereby advised to hobble and tether his
own particular bit of muslin until she gets over
the fascinations of the pale-coffee-coloured stranger
in the slouch hat, who calls her “senorita” with
the melodious accent of old Castile, and offers her
pomegranates over the back fence when the old
man’s away.
But we mustn’t overlook the fact that the she-
journalist is rare in Uruguay, so we have no
means of knowing how the male New Australian
is catching on as a professional beauty. Because,
if Monte Videan belledom has been bowled over
by the attractions of the men as badly as the
senors have been by those of the girls, the situa-
tion, in one sense, may not be so bad as it looks.
In another sense, it is probably a good deal worse
than it looks. Monte Video is a big, cosmopolitan
kind of city, where the residents see all manner
uf strange people coming and going, and become
acquainted with all kinds of beauty, so that the
local senor gets the fine edge worn off his suscep-
tibilities. But away up the river, in lonesome
Paraguay, he sees nothing, from the beginning to
the end of the century, save the saddle-coloured
sisterhood of the land, and when the new variety
of girl heaves in sight, the blasB don will probably
cut his way through the back fence with an axe in
his anxiety to get in and propose to her on the
spot.
Do you know what I am waiting for to make
me alter my dark suspicion that women are
responsible for all the titular attractions of Aus-
tralia ? lam waiting for a bachelor or a widower
to rise up, Sir Gingerbread ! Every titled man
who has been basted with the flat side of a sabre
has had a woman edging him on. The two latest
recommendations for “ennobblement ” —Mr. R. J.
Seddon, the Maoriland politician, and Judge
Harding, of Queensland (and a very pretty Judge,
too—l used to think it would be rather nice to be
tried by Judge Harding for some gilt-edged mis-
demeanor and sent to a cell, weeping gracefully
into a lace handkerchief)—are both married, it
almost goes without saying. Mr. Seddon, by the
way, hasn’t been recommended for a knighthood,
after all, but I am,not'going to let that interfere
with my argument. He ought to have been,
rather than leave a lone female out in a great
waste of theory waiting for corroboration to rise
in the east. But for woman’s yearning after
titles and decorations, man’s alleged desire to sell
his soul for the privilege of wearing a ribbon like
a horse’s girth at moments of extreme official
pressure would be a very much abbreviated
emotion. Woman is undoubtedly the gaudiest
sex— but let us be charitable —it the fault
of her training. Women are brought up to admire
ribbons.
The story goes of one titled lady, at present in
Australia, that, during the voyage thitherward,
her trick was to chew (women don’t smoke, they
think they do, but just watch how they gobble a
cigarette) little paper dissipations by the bundle,
and one evening she sallied on deck to enjoy a
whiff on the poop. She hadn’t been puffing five
minutes before the' quarter-master, or some such
brass-buttoned tin-god, came briskly up : “You
must not smoke here, madam. If you want to
smoke, you must go into the smoking-room or
abaft there !” The lady smoked in her own state-
room after that —from which such clouds of dust
and ashes billowed during her occasional quiet
whiff, that the passengers christened her the
“ Kippered Herring ” for the rest of the voyage.
Talking of smoking at sea. lam told that two
Sydney society ladies, who went Bull-wards not
so long ago, chewed up one hundred boxes of
cigarettes between them on the voyage ! But the
most Christian construction to put upon this is
that the girl, who always has a cigarette in her
lips, doesn’t do much k flirting, I mean.
The statement—in a “ ’Ome ” journal—that in
thirty years’ of hard praying and cocoanut tithe-
snatching there have been only three converts to
the European notion of Christianity among the
muoh-talked-of Matabeles, reminds me —though I
really can’t trace why —of a certain Australian
young lady, daughter of a once-famous N.S. W.
sculler, who got engaged not long ago to a banker
who was not of her own religious persuasion—he
was an Anglican and she was of the Vatican.
Thereupon the young lady set diligently to
work to save her Gussie’s immortal soul from the
parsonical stockyard and lure him into the sheep-
fold of Father O’Rourke. Instead of tickling
Gussie under the chin and curling his moustache,
as the average engaged girl does, she spent every
opportunity in arguing about transubstantiation
and doxology, and trying to encourage her best
young man into committing scissors—or schism,
or whatever it is.
At last, her indefatigability won the day.
Gussie flouted the bishop and adopted the Pope,
and his fiancee went about with the exalted ex-
pression of one who has fought a good fight and
got the scalps thrown in. But, behold !it soon
appeared that Gussie was converted very badly
indeed, so badly that at last he broke it to his
promised bride that he really must give up all
earthly things and get him to a friary ! And he
suited the action to the word by making enquiries
how to enter the priesthood the very next day.
The moral of this strictly true story seems to me
to be —never convert a lover to any faith that lias
celibacy in the recipe.
“Ouida’s” mother is dead, and Queen Victoria
has sent a message of condolence to the orphan
By the death of her ma Omda is left without a
chaperone, for Madame de la Ramee always lived
with her gifted daughter.
Which reminds me that a Melbourne girl, just
returned from a Continental tour, told me a few
reminiscences of a certain gifted novelist, who is
supposed to have simply coined money with her
pen. She —I mean the Melbourne girl happened
to be at the same hotel where the novelist and her
mother were putting up. Hearing the sound of a
“regular father of a scolding” emanating from an
adjoining room, she interviewed the landlord, who
simply said : “It is Mdlle remonstrating
with Madame her mother!” Becoming more
garrulous he confessed that the talented authoress
wasn’t as regular as clock-work with her hotel-
bills, but squandered money for everything else
first, and often relied on the generosity of a pass-
ing Duke or Duchess to get her out of pawn. At
one hotel they had to take the doors of her apart-
ments off to induce her to quit the premises.
“ No one is a hero to his—landlord.’’
A fashionable Sydney camera-man has hit upon
a clever way of attracting business. He has
rented a shop-window on the Block and filled it
with cartes of society women, leavened by pic-
tures of Charles Ryley and one or two other male
beauties. In the hope that her smirking counten-
ance may be plac 'd in the front row, so that
Augustus may admire it on Saturday mornings,
the suburban girl is almost sure to rush the—l
forget the name—studios. By the way, a Melb.
photographer exhibits a portrait of Nellie Stewart
which you can’t see for the flies that have yielded
up the ghost between the picture and the pane.
As there are no flies on Nellie, the artist is hereby
advised to pass a feather duster round exhibit A,
if only for the sake of eternal fitness.
Yours faithfully,
SAPPHO SMITH.
The attempted dynamite outrage in the
French Assembly is still another proof that there
are a great number of very bad shots in this
world. The anarchist had a whole Legislature
for a target when he threw his little pot of com-
bustibles. He was overhead, and only needed to
drop it and let things take their course. It was
all just about as easy as hitting a mountain with
a brick. But, while waving his bomb needlessly
about his head before letting fly, he struck it
against something and it exploded in mid-air, and
wrecked the gallery instead of the premise#
below, and another great sensation went
wrong. The Barcelona anarchist who aimed
his package of dynamite at the _ head _of
Marshal Campos was another case in point.
The gaudy Marshal’s cocked hat made a
splendid target, and the assailant took a pot-shot
at it from a range of a few feet —and missed.
Anarchists always miss, somehow. A school-boy
aiming his dictionary at a hen is ten times more
destructive to the object aimed at. It is the man
standing by that the dynamiter always blows into
six or more pieces.
Bread and Violets.
The bread called life r-so cold, so very cold,
With robes of desolation fold on fold,^
So bare and dry and barren ! O, my God .
Why was I born a singer—not a clod ?
Better it were in the rush and the whir,
Better it were in the crest of the wrong,
To find a brief joy in the crush and the stir
Than to ache with despair and to pour out s
song,
So hopelessly longing for—what?
Violets called love. Sweet, purple, and warm,
Innocent, too, as the world well says,
Coyer, perchance, than a woman’s form,
Breasting the thorns of a deep, dread maze,
Which maze call “ love ” —of another kind
From the violet love of a violet year ;
But which shall we choose? The body or mind,
Violets —or roses? Safety—or fear?
• • • •
Roses —and wine in a bright golden cup —
Roses, all crimson, but dry-leaved and hot;
Wine, when the sediment stirs and comes up
When sparkle of bubble has fled and is not;
Or violets—and bread ! Cool violets, all wet
With the innocent dew of an innocent sky,
Their perfumes forever more pungently yet
Arise to caress in the sound of a sigh,
Peacefully, calmly, no riot of love
To mock with its madness the Heaven above !
Then, say which love shall we cherish or dread?
The roses —and wine ? Or the violets - and bread ?
INA M. W.
The will of the murdered bank-manager,
Phillips, of Carcoar, was proved at =£B3s.
The oldest journalist in Australia is Win.
Holden, of S.A. Register. He is 86 years of age,
and is still a tiger to work. He is a heavy smoker
and a Swedenborgian.
Lee, M.L.A., who nearly wrecked the
Dibbs Government, is brother to ex-Stipendiary
Magistrate “ Benny ” Lee, whom Minister
O’Connor “retired” some time ago. The Lee
family has now got level with its enemy, who has
lost both his billet and his famous brief.
Though baby-farmer Knorr has been sen-
tenced to death in Melbourne, it is not likely
that she will be hanged. No woman has been
hanged in Victoria for about 30 years. This is
one of lovely woman’s wrongs, and points out a
bitterly distinct inequality with the lordly male
whose neck is sufficiently coarse and hairy to be
broken officially.
Mrs, William Ferguson, who came out to
South Australia in the ship which brought the
first Governor, Captain John Hindmarsh, and
witnessed the proclamation of the province under
the old gum-tree at Holdfast Bay, died last week,
an octogenarian.
A grin of derision went round the Court
when Chief Justice Darley, in sentencing solicitor
Broad to five years’ penal servitude, said that he
was thankful there were not many solicitors in
this province who indulged in dishonest practices.
His Honor probably meant that few solicitors
were found out indulging in dishonest practices.
In Cochin-China the imperial treasures
are deposited in hollow tree trunks, and anchored
out in a huge tank in the centre of the royal
palace with 20 crocodiles to keep a dull, fishlike
eye on the property. No one can embezzle the
treasure without first killing the crocodiles, conse-
quently there has never been a shortage within
the memory of any living man. Some day Aus-
tralian bank shareholders will drop to the advan-
tages of this method, and insist on the reserve-
fund being kept in a tank likewise. A crocodile
is a lot more reliable than an auditor any day.
The long-forgotten Dora Dura blacks have
been heard from again, in the vicinity of Bunda-
berg (Q.), where one of them has been run down,
fallen upon, badly flattened, and captured by a
local policeman. The other got away, but after
this unlooked-for success he will probably be pur-
sued simultaneously in eighty-four different
localities scattered all over this continent, and be
arrested in large quantities throughout Australia.
The squashed and fallen-upon aboriginal now in
custody is being taken to Victoria for trial. The
Dora Dora blacks have had an interesting history.
Nobody saw them commit their famous homicide,
except the victim himself, and he died leaving be-
hind him a general description which might fit
almost any two niggers in this continent. The
only circumstance by which they could possibly
be identified was the fact that there were two of
them together, therefore the police and the general
public have ever since consistently chased every
pair of niggers that were heard of. Probably 40
separate pairs of niggers have been pursued and
have got away in the course of the hunt. The
forty-first pair, however, were short-winded, and
Nemesis overtook them as it always does overtake
the corpulent aborigine with brief and transitory
legs. Now that one has been captured the prin-
cipal means by which the other can be identi-
fied is tlie fact that he is alone, therefore there is
going to be a bad time for solitary natives during
the next few months.
The point raised as to the validity of the
N.S.W. Electoral Act of ’93 is one that does much
credit to the ingenuity of its discoverer. The
whole question is whether the Assembly has dele-
gated, or can delegate, to the electoral district
commissioners its power of “establishing new and
other” electoral divisions of the province. It
is contended that the commissioners exercised a
delegated power in altering the boundaries of the
constituencies, and that as these alterations were
not subsequently enacted by Parliament the alter-
ations are invalid. And the awkwardness of the
whole matter is that if the point is a good
one, the Act is still valid as to the repeal of the
former Act, so that neither Act is available for re-
election of members. But the A.G. will probably
decide that there is nothing in the point. The
Assembly did “establish new and other” elec-
toral divisions, the boundaries of those electorates
being such as the commissioners should fix. The
commissioners did not exercise a delegated power
to establish electorates any more than a policeman
who collects the roll does. If the A.G., however,
should think otherwise a one-section bill run
through the House, when Parliament meets, will
fix the thing up, and failing that, Premier Repub-
lican Dibbs will have a lovely chance of trying
Government on strictly damn-the-Constitution-
Act lines.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
t Blackham says Turner “ gob the needle ” because
he was often sent in last at the commencement of the
tour, when he was completely off owing to ill-health.
He is also of opinion that the English fast bowlers com-
pletely demoralised the Australians, who are too much
used to tricky slow bowlers.
* * »
Ooningham, who was generally a bystander,
who had no provincial prejudices in favour of particular
men, no brother to “bear up” for, and who “never
drank a drop during the whole of the tour,” seems to be
the most reliable authority on the conduct of the bicker-
ing “ Hoys.” Coningham’s general grievance tends to
make his opinion sacred, and he, by the way, has his best
word for the harassed manager. The manager returns
the compliment. They were comrades in sorrow,
* » *
Taking it all round, however, the saddest feature
about the return of the Australian Eleven is the great
mass of explanation which commenced as soon as the
team landed, and which has raged at intervals ever since.
And, after the explanation, the saddest thing is the want
of explanation. The team came ashore silently and with-
out any semblance of an ovation, and went its lonesome
way, mostly b3 r twos, no one man, apparently, being on
cordial terms with more than one other man and, in some
cases, he didn't appear to he on very cordial terms even
with him. The majority of members, when interviewed,
seem to have been too depressed even to complain
violently. But they insinuated things about each other
in a dreary, bilious, dyspeptic fashion. Some of them
“ weren’t prepared to say ” that one member’s nose
hadn’t been pulled, or that another member hadn’t used
unprintable language, or that somebody did n’t want to fight
somebody else under lamentable circumstances, but
nobody denied anything with much vigour. It was
stated, in a general sort of way, that the team wasn’t
quite so drunk as was commonly supposed,.hut if it didn’t
over-drink itself it probably over-ate itself instead. Its
present attitude seems to suggest great masses of un-
digested food, more than anything else, as the principal
disturbing influence.
# # *
Rising young cricketers are hereby advised to
join the Y.M.C.A. There will be a fearful demand for
sobriety and Christian gentleness when the next Aus-
tralian Eleven is chosen, and the batsman who says ids
prayers every night will assuredly be picked before the
comparative worldling who bustles through them on
Sunday nights only'. Nor will anything in the shape of a
“demon ” bowler stand a chance of a place in the team.
Young men must learn to bowl earnestly and straight,
and with the fear of the Lord before their eyes, instead of
putting “devil" into it.
* # #
The late Spring meeting of the V.R.C., which
was at the time described as a financial success by fatuous
sporting reporters, proves to have been a failure even
when compared with the disastrous Mud Carnival of 1892.
Consequently, a further cutting down of stakes is an-
nounced, and the glory of a £lO,OOO Melt). Cup may be
considered a thing of the past. This year the V.R.C. had
profitable weather for Derby, Cup, Oaks and Steeple, yet
the four days’ receipts fell about £28,000 below the
champion record of 1888. A speedy improvement in busi-
ness is out of the question. The big meeting has declined,
proportionately, to a much greater extent than the more
purely horsey gatherings in March, May and July, and
here we have a convincing proof that the general public,
the raw, amateur race-goers, are becoming “full-up” of
the so-called sport provided for them nowadays. Press
commentators on racing matters ape mostly incapable of
tellifur the truth, so this extraordinary drop in Melh. Cup
attendances is attributed solely to bad times and “ pre-
vailing depression.” As a matter of fact, the temptation
to have a hit upon a horse is one of the last things to be
killed by adversity.
The thousands of men who have lately sworn-off
their old habit of “holiday” betting at Flemington are
not all of them unable to afford the luxury of a wager.
Scarcity of funds is, of course, an important factor in the
change which has come over the V.R.C. dream, but the
awful decay of Turf decency has much more to do with
the matter. For a good while past The Bulletin has
said that the V.R.C., by allowing folks in general to he
swindled beyond all reasonable endurance, would surely
kill the goose that lays the golden eggs. The goose afore-
said is a hacker, and not a lajer, but the figure of speech
should he intelligible, nevertheless. The oft-plucked
bird seems to have the death-mark on its brow, and if
racing “ form ” much longer remains as impudently dis-
honest as at present, the goose will die withouta struggle.
V.R.C. stewards may spare themselves the trouble of con-
sidering ways and means of luring their lost patrons
back to Flemington, unless they can officially guarantee
a fair percentage of fair runs for the public money. Those
careless guardians of sporting morality may abandon the
idea that their gate-money will improve wnen “things
begin to come round.” Things must become a deal more
square in the racing world ere the disgusted punters will
want to visit it again. James White was the V.R.C.’s
best friend. He kept the public well supplied with
favourites that tried to win, and frequently succeeded in
the attempt. Now that White is dead, the burden of
roguery is more than racing can bear.
* * *
One of the incidents of the last Melb. Cup :
When the wife of a well-known owner of race-horses saw
hubby’s animal beaten even for a place, she straight-
way tied herself up in a hysterical attack and cleared
about space around her in 10 sec. This is about
the only instance on record where it could be safely
assumed a race was run absolutely on the square.

The Bulletin has received the following
printed slip, numbers the like of which have been sent
abroad throughout the Australian provinces; “Notice to
Subscribers of Tattersall’s Monster Consultations. In
consequence of the difficulties which have recently arisen
in connection with the transmission of letters, I have
been compelled to alter my address. Until further notice
please address -J. H. Chisholm, General Post Office,
Sydney, N.S.W.” The generally accepted idea is that all
letters with reference to Tatbersall’s consultations should
be addressed (o Telegraph Chambers, Brisbane. Intending
subscribers should bear this in mind when reading the
above, which is evidently another of the mysteries of the
Australian turf.
# * *
In the J. J. Miller prosecution, dismissed in
Melb. recently, the Bench called W. Miles, clerk of the
V.R.C., to swear that the Melb. Cup was really raced for.
Which reminds us that Sydney Banco Court does not
officially know what the Melh. Cup is, and hesitates to
infer without evidence that it is a kind of horse-race. It
was a Sydney judge, too, who interrupted counsel by
saving, “ Well, we have heard a great deal about big totes
and little totes, but I should like to know how big a big
tote is. Would it go into a room as big as this court?”
And not a barrister in the case was quite certain whether
it would.

Playing for Prince Alfred (Wesleyan) College
in a cricket-match against >St. Peters on the Adelaide
Oval early this month, Clem. Hill, son of one of the S A.
Railway Commissioners, made 360 not out, which is be-
lieved to be the record of Australia. The total score of
his side was 621. Hill’s father is a cricketer, and a good
eleven, including a daughter who bats vigorously, could
be put into the field by the family.

• * *
The latest boating fatality on Sydney Harbour
—the sinking of the Ripple and the loss of seven persons
out of the thirteen on board—arose, so far as appears
from the evidence up to date, piincipally through the
ghastly architecture of the foundered vessel. According
to regulations, every sailing-boat which is kept for hire
must be provided with corks or air tight chambers or
some other means of flotation, sufficient, in case of
accident, to counterbalance the weight of the centre-
board and ballast, and keep her afloat even when full of
water. Therefore, the ordinary sailing craft will float
upright and full of water, upside-down, sideways, or
practically anyhow. The regulation, however, as per the
pig-headed statoots of New South Wales, does not apply
to private boats. The Ripple, with her ton, or ton-and-a-
nalf, of lead, on her keel, had just as much chance,
when once she filled with water, as an iron stove
or a cartload of loose bricks. For all practical purposes,
she might as well have been a grindstone turned loose on
the surface of the Pacific. The sinking of the Ripple
isn’t a commentary on the consequences of “Sabbath
desecration,” though many a profound and nasal moral
will doubtless be drawn from the disaster. Nor is it a
confirmation of the old-fashioned “thirteen superstition.”
It is merely a solid proof that 20cwt. of unsupported lead
is an eminently sinkable material at all times. Vessels of
the Ripple kind are mere death-traps when anything goes
wrong. Ernest Vider, the well-known Sydney cyclist, is
among the drowned. His body and that of another of the
crew are unaccounted for. Considering the shark-infested
slate of these waters, it is quite possible that they' may
both remain unaccounted for till the Day of Resurrection.

The finish between Woodward and Bucknall for
the M.B.C.’s Austral Wheel Race was very fine, the limit
man, although “collared” some distance from home,
being beaten by half a length only. Tub Bulletin's
“ tip” of Bucknall, Jorgenson and Elliott was fairly near
the mark for the whole day’s racing, inasmuch as the
N.S.W. man pulled off the One-and a-Half Mile in bis
(second) class, and Elliott scored a heat in the One Mile
Open.

Bookmakers, as usual of late, were more or less
excluded from the M. 8.0. meeting. One determined
individual was turned off the ground eleven times, but he
paid his twelfth entrance fee, found a means of making
Ids quiet little book, and cleared £BO on the day. The
reward of perseverance.

The Vic. Railway Department has offered to
consider the propriety of reducing fares to suburban
race-meetings as soon as the racing authorities are ready
to push trade by reducing their charges for admission. It
is thus that the State works to suppress betting.
A GREAT SPECIALIST.
A MAN FOR THE PEOPLE.
An important discovery, which is exciting con-
siderable attention among the general public
throughout the country, has been made by Dr.
P. Stanich, Aural Surgeon, the well-known
specialist of Sydney. It is the cure of the nasal
complaint, technically called Ozcena, which
generally results from catarrh or cold in the
head, an affection hitherto considered, to a
great extent, incurable. Ozcena is a stinking
ulceration of the membrane, destroys the nose,
and renders a person’s features most unsightly.
Dr. Stanich’s cures have been so effectual that
particulars of discovery have been spread all
over the Australian continent. This discovery
justifies the reputation of Dr. Stanich as a
scientist of front rank. As a specialist in diseases
of the ear, he has long enjoyed the highest
position in the country, receiving high written
acknowledgments from the Right Honorable
Sir Alfred Stephen, Lord Loftus, and other
prominent citizens, whom he has benefited.
Like all able men, Dr. Stanich is a philanthropist,
as he administers to hundreds of poor patients of
all grades and ages, without charge, at his consult-
ing rooms at 145 Elizabeth-street, Sydney, which
is a busy spot at almost all hours. Visitors from
all parts of N. S. Wales and other colonies are
found there, as testimony to his great reput-
ation, while inquiries by post on almost all
kinds of complaints connected with the head and
throat pour in on him from all quarters.

Henry Copeland, N.S.W. Minister for
Lands, has explicitly explained to the Nat. Ass.
what he would have done with his Land Bill
hadn’t he kept on doing something else with other
things. Copeland would have extended time to
squatters and given them vested rights in water
and fencing improvements ; would have facilitated
homestead purchases ; and established a scheme of
28 years’ leases. Echoes of distant chorus, com-
mingled with fumes of brimstone :
Not, oh ! not, my son,
This our present stew ;
Had we only done
What we meant to do.
Same’in every sphere are
Things that history mentions ;
All the tracks to here are
Paved with good intentions.
Would it be senility, or a smart electioneer-
ing trick, if old Parkes were to throw up his
knighth/jod? He could probably easily assume it
again. Dibbs threw up his republicanism.
Yarney Parkes, M.P. (N.S. W.), says that
it is impossible to “sink the fiscal issue.” His
good old man has managed to sink his own fiscal
issue pretty successfully all his life.
The latest political trouble in N.S.W. is
the professed discovery, by somebody or other,
that the one-man-one-vote principle is contrary to
the “ constitooshun,” and that, in consequence,
something will have to be done by the British
Parliament before the new system can come into
force. This should be a grand opportunity for
Dibbs, who loves to meet an apparently insuper-
able obstacle, so that he can rush at it and put his
head through it. The spectacle of Dibbs cheer-
fully damning the Constitution and going straight
ahead would be inspiriting.
Last week a demented Hindoo, armed
with a huge piece of artillery, called at the
N.S.W. Premier’s office, and was arrested in time
to prevent a tragedy. G. R. Dibbs afterwards
remarked to an interviewer : “It is consoling at
all events to know that a man can die only once.”
Which, considering that the weapon wasn’t
loaded, and was so rusty and so m-uch out of
date that it probably couldn’t have been fired,
and that the Hindoo, apparently, didn’t know
how to load it in any case, and that he was seized
before he got within several corridors of his victim,
and that he was a poor, weak little man whom
Dibbs could have bowled over with a book while
he was still fumbling with his antiquated bombard,
sounds like an altogether superfluous piece of
philosophy.
A wasteful expenditure of powder and
ball, under the pretence of teaching the N.S.W.
artillery how to repel an attack upon Sydney by
two hostile cruisers and their torpedo-boats, prac-
tically took the form of a bombardment of
Watson’s Bay. One officer followed the moving
target so zealously that his shot ricochetted over
yachts and launches, splashed among rocks, and,
in some instances, ploughed through land in the
immediate vicinity of houses, to the terror of the
inhabitants. Major-General Hutton, greatly
enraged at the spectacle, gave the order to cease
firing. An officer ran to what appeared to be a
telephone, and went through the dumb show
business of conveying the General’s orders to the
zealous gunner ; but whether the telephone circuit
had been broken, or whether (as was merrily
stated after all danger was over) the wire had
forgetfully never been connected at all are
matters that require explanation. Strenuous
efforts were made to hoodwink the Sydney press,
and these appear to have been partially successful.
The long-haired Caldwell, M.P., wants
S.A. Legislature to enact that Ministers of the
Crown shall in future be personally liable for any
direct loss to revenue occasioned through neglect,
mismanagement or “ misinterpretation of legisla-
tion.” In the event of an action against the
Government being successful, the Minister, whose
department is involved, is to be liable for the
amount involved and costs. Finally, no Minister
who has been once convicted under the proposed
system shall ever hold office again. Considering
that the Ministers who came to grief under this
clause would only do it through want of brains,
and brains are hereditary, Caldwell might con-
sider the advisability of taking action against the
politician’s parents unless they can prove an alibi.
Dufchie, the Tory member for Wellington
(M.L.), is a man with a temper which won’t bear
close inspection, and during the late campaign it
was a favourite amusement to rouse up the slum-
bering ironmonger. At one of his meetings he
was obviously making a great effort to preserve
his calmness. One fatuous elector asked if the
candidate would dance a hornpipe if returned ;
another inquired if he had a good tip for the next
races; and a third wanted to know if he was in
favour of young men taking their girls into the
Botanic Gardens after 4 p.m? Duthie swelled up
visibly with indignation, but still ho preserved a
semblance of composure. Then two electors got
up together, one inquiring if the candidate
would take the income-tax off empty packing-
eases, and the other wanting to know if he was
in favour of making every teetotaller drink two
quarts of cold water every morning and making
Robert Stout drink three quarts? And then
something burst audibly in Duthie’s head, and he
let go his hold on himself, and blew up with a loud
report, and said: “ !! !
!!! !! - !!!!!!”

Ballade of a Dying Race.
[FOR THE BULLETIN].
Strange land beneath the Southern skies;
Strange race whose sun seems on the wane,
That from the white race shrinks and flies,
As flies the night at morning’s reign ;
Alike ye treat with dull disdain,
The white man’s God, or Chinese Joss,
Sleep, ease, and food your highest gain,
Doomed Children of the Southern Cross.
Ah, sad and soft Australian eyes,
Eyes dark with depth of wistful pain ;
What mournful memory in them lies
Of days that come not back again ?
Ere yet the white man crossed your main,
Ere yet his gain had proved your loss,
Were there brave days of love and Fame,
Doomed Children of the Southern Cross?
Harsh Fate to you so much denies,
Your lives such little joys contain,
Your aims above the brute scarce rise ;
Poor darkened souls, poor feeble brain,
Seek out some wild, free bush domain,
A kingdom sweet of fern and moss,
There free a little while remain
Doomed Children of the Southern Cross.
Envoi :
Yet shall ye hear in drip of rain,
In moan of winds that dead leaves toss
A sad and ceaseless low refrain,
“ Doomed Children of the Southern Cross.”
CRAIG ELLACHIE.
Dear Bulletin, Your Melb. corres
pondent, “Nem.,” who boasts of living on Bd. a
day is an extravagant epicure and will come to
want some day. Let him take to “ burgoo ”
and sheep’s liver, and put 2s. a week in some
consumptive bank against his old age. A man
camped under a potato sack “ fly ” in Albert
Park told me that he had lived on this sumptuous
fare for five weeks at a cost of half-a-sov. Some-
times he begged the liver “for the oat.”—Yours,
Snell.
Dear Bulletin, —Some years ago, “Jimmy
the Malay,” now residing at Port Moresby
(N.Q.), was taken out of his canoe by a huge
alligator, which got him down on the river
bottom and sat on his chest. Jimmy, however,
with great presence of mind, “gouged” the
reptile’s eyes and it scooted, and James rose to
the surface in great haste, and regained his canoe
and composure. Beyond a slight shook to the
system and a few bruises he was little the worse
for his adventure. This statement isn’t the reck-
less lie it appears. The alligator, although it’s
teeth are made something after the cross-cut saw
principle—only more so—does not seize its victim
and chew him up on the spot, as is generally sup-
posed. Instead, it drags him gently to the
bottom, stands on him till he drowns, and then
digs a hole in a mud-bank and buries him till he gets
gamey. Then it calls around in a week or so and
“ scoffs ” him without mastication.—Yours, Bak-
TRAX.
Editor Bulletin, — I beg you tell the
manager to discontinue sending your paper, as it
is utterly impossible to get it delivered at Cool-
gardie. I have received six Bulletins while here,
and may consider myself lucky. At Coolgardie a
tent serves for the post-office, and the mail, when
it arrives, is dumped down on the ground outside
and everyone helps himself. Last mail I secured
two of my papers just as another man was in the
act of walking off with them. I saw other men
taking papers which did not belong to them.
Reading the W.A. papers, you would think it
was all gold here, but nothing could be more mis-
leading. Nowhere have I had a harder job to get
a few dwts. than here. For every one who gets
gold here there are hundreds who get nothing.
All the glowing accounts come from the lease-
holders, who naturally want to boom the place.
The Government, too, seem to be bent on making
money out of the field, seeing that they charge 3d.
a gallon for water as salt as the sea. Altogether
there are about 800 fossickers here, some getting a
few dwts. a week, more getting nothing. I, my-
self, have got 2|dwts. in a fortnight, and if rain
does not soon come I’ll have to move on to I know
not where. Hundreds of others are in exactly
the same predicament. So you will see that this
hell-hole is not exactly what the boomers paint it.
—O. Sodergkken, Coolgardie, Nov, 3, 1893.

The case of the forger lately sentenced to
ten years at Newcastle, N.S. W., was a ghastly
exhibition of the detective incapacity of the
police. Twelve years ago the prisoner (a coal-
trimmer by trade who dabbled in photography)
became a suspect, and though, as was afterwards
learnt, he periodically disposed of numbers of
forged notes, the police could never obtain the
slightest scintilla of evidence against him. When
the Narrabri utterer was arrested a P.O. order was
found on him addressed to the forger’s wife, and,
accordingly, the Newcastle police swooped on the
latter’s residence, where, after a scrupulous
search, they failed to find any article of an in-
criminating nature. A week later they again
searched, with the same annoying result. On a
third visit, however, they found, stowed away
among a number of dust«or »-d, discarded
negatives, one which faintly »h >wed on one
corner part of the impress or a B.N.S.W.
i>s note. Conviction ensued. The trial was
chiefly remarkable for the manner in which the
prisoner broke up the photographic expert im-
ported by the prosecution. Several hundred pieces
of paper, cut exactly to the size of a bank-note,
were found on the premises of prisoner, and the
expert deposed that such a size was never under
any circumstances used in legitimate art. Prisoner
thereupon handed in a printed trade-list in which
were several quotations for that size of sheet.
Some of the paper was then handed to the expert,
and he was asked it it were possible that it could
have been so treated photographically that by
merely damping it a picture would immediately
appear upon the blank paper. The expert dog-
matically deposed that no such process had been
discovered. Accused then damped two of the
sheets, on one of which immediately appeared a
photo, of Lily Langtry, and on the other a photo,
of the courthouse in which the trial was being
conducted.
The other day the Australian dailies
announced that Gueen Victoria had sent 140
bottles of wine to the London hospitals. It is now
asserted in the English press that the wine was
sour and had to be thrown away.
A correspondent writes from one of the
Russian Asiatic provinces to the Victorian Post-
master-General ; “ That wonderful projectile, the
boomerang, interests me so much that I would
like you to send me one. I will forward the
money with pleasure.” The next thing will be a
cablegram: “ Boomerang hurled at the Czar.”
A N.S.W. police-station has been struck
by lightning. Well, Ananias was also struck by
lightning. Which recalls that the Synnott Bros.,
who used to run an abusive evening paper in
Barataria, were fond of heading articles about
their loathsome contemporary, “ Annanias
Again.” “Why do you spell Ananias with a
double ‘n’?” asked a stranger one day of the
chief reporter. “ Well,’’replied the reporter who
cordially detested his employers, “because there’s
a double ‘ n ’ in Synnott. ”
Dear Bulletin, —Melbourne Age reports
an alleged “marvellous” case of a girl in an up
country town, who daily sheds splinters concern-
ing which she can give no explanation. It is
strange nobody seems to diagnose so simple an
affair. Many times has the writer seen men, some
of whose ancestors must have been in the timber
trade, shedding sawdust through their pores.
Lovy diet and excessive exposure to the air appear
specially favourable to this phenomenon. Take a
dozen Domain dossers at random, strip them and
then brush their skins with a hard clothes brush,
and see if in several instances sawdust doesn’t
come off in packets. The girl’s father has pro-
bably a near relation who was a sawyer, and the
inhaled wood-fibres which he transmitted have
concentrated in her system through some unusual
mental and physical combinations, and produced
the above result. Nice girl to hug in a hurry, by
the way.—Yours, C.J.

The Boodlers’ Requiem.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They grew in beauty side by side,
They shore one land with glee ;
Their gaols are scattered far and wide,
From Perth to Coral Sea.
They took the same old public in,
. They stole the widow’s cow;
The took the same poor orphan’s tin—
Where are those dollars now ?
The first defaulter wandered West,
A godly man and true ;
lie tied his colours round his breast,
He tied our assets, too.
The sea—the blue, lone sea—hatn one,
He lies—he lieth low ;
His vessel made a famous run
To far Fernando Po.
One bears a chain with cheerless clank
Upon his lily hand;
And one reposeth on a plank—
The last of that bright band.
And, parted thus, they rest who wore
The same old greasy smile,
Whose eyes with pious tears ran o’er,
Whose cheeks were fat with guile.
They’re gone who stole the children’s bread
And pawned the pauper’s gear ;
And o’er each dear departed head
The vulture drops a tear.
Q.
P. LUFTIG.

Bill Cody, the Wild West man, has bought
a fine house in North Platte, Neb., “near the
spot where he killed his first Indian.” Which
recalls the good Australian ecclesiastic (he who
“buried his heart” with his wife and then married
another lady half her age) asking a Q. squatter
how it was that while the surrounding country
was so sterile the station-garden was so fruitful—
“ Niggers, Bishop, niggers. Eleven niggers in
that quarter-acre. We buried all we could find
there. The rest of the ’s died in the ranges.”
Morals: Habits which change every 50
years or so.
“The Pilgrim Mothers are quite over-
looked.” — American.
“In Memoriam of Our Beloved Em-
ployer.” Odd sort of a testimonial, exhibited in
a Melb. window, signed by a number of men.
The pigeon-English Bible, issued in 1830,
for the Chinese, is outrageously funny. Hardly
one can be got now, for it was bought up like a
Punch.
The Earl of Pembroke, well-known as
joint-author of that charming book “South Sea
Bubbles,” has consented to write a preface for
Louis Becke’s forthcoming book of Polynesian
tales and sketches from The Bulletin. It will
be published in London.
Dear Bulletin, —I am out of a billet and
want to go out and find a chunk of ambergris. I
was told that, some time this year, a party of shore
whalemen in Tasmania got 18501bs. from the
carcase of a whale that drifted ashore. Can you
give me any particulars? The matter was alluded
to in the daily papers. N.B.—Please tell me in
what part of the whale the ambergris is located. —
Yours, &c., Clericus Bal^ena.
The latest “ bomb ” found in London, just
in time to prevent a hideous explosion, now proves
to be an ancient shell, dug up on the Italian
battlefield of Genestrello, and preserved as a
curiosity. The owner lost the run of it while
shifting his furniture to new quarters; then a
policeman found it, and the conspiracy was
promptly cabled all over the earth.
In England, according to last census, there
are 60,000 more married women than married
men. At first sight it seems difficult to under-
stand how any woman can be married without a
corresponding man to assist her, but the assump-
tion is that the 60,000 are really old maids, who
call themselves “Mrs.” to avoid the obloquy
which attaches to the bony spinster when she gets
to be over 43.
A toilet luxury in every respect, Ayer’s Hair
Vigor never fails to restore the youthful freshness
and color to faded and gray hair. It also
eradicates dandruff and prevents the hair from
falling.

The present political situation in N.S. W-
is just this ; Premier Dibbs will probably have to
go out of office because he approved of Attorney-
General Barton holding private briefs against the
State. Then Eyeglass Reid, who tacitly approved
of Burton’s proceeding until he found that there
was political capital to be made out of it, will
come in. Barton himself has quitted the Cabinet
because he asserted his right to hold briefs
practically against the Treasury; and if a
Foreign-trade Cabinet is formed it will probably
include Want, Q. 0., who, when Attorney-General,
did almost exactly the same thing. Yet, under
the beautiful system of Government by Contra-
diction, the country’s choice practically lies
between Dibbs and Reid, and if it doesn’t like
the one it has simply got to exchange him for the
other.
The latest Victorian Bible-in-schools depu-
tation claimed to represent 200,000 persons who
were solid in demanding the abolition of “godless
education.” This is unwonted moderation on the
part of the sectarians, who generally claim to
speak for 75 per cent, of the population at the
lowest estimate. And though one election passes
after another, and their most frantic efforts seldom
result in the return of half-a-dozen members to the
Assembly,they always turn up again on the door-
mat of the Education Department with the same
old assertion that they represent the great mass of
the nation, and “ godless education ” is doomed.
The front of a 14-storey coffee-palace is a small
thing compared to the front of a sectarian depu-
tation.
Ex-Governor Jersey, now that he has gone
“Home,” has begun to fire oft the usual heavy
platitudes about N.S. Wales. The following
choice samples lately arrived by cable :
Lord Jersey advocates the carrying out of a thorough
system of irrigation in N.S. Wales.
Lord Jersey predicts a great destiny for Australia.
Lord Jersey states that under sagacious leaders trades-
unionism is not dangerous, but he advises a more prudent
recognition of the rights of capital.
Lord Jersey referred to the general condition of the
colony, and said that, with a proper system of irrigation,
the colony would eventually become a"great p ople.
The latter statement is about as brilliant as if
Jersey had said that, with a proper system of
bullock-driving, a three-rail fence would eventually
become a cathedral. And the rest only amounts to
a solemn assurapee that there are milestones on
the Dover road. Also, deah friends, rain is wet
and generally falls downward in the shape of
damp moisture descending in a liquid form
vertically with a tendency to arrive at a lower
position than it previously occupied. It is getting
to be very dreadful—that weary statement that
somebody has stood himself up on his two soles
and said that Australia has a great destiny before
it. What is a destiny, anyhow ? And why is it
that, though every country is always going to
have one, no country ever seems to have its
destiny visibly on hand? Also, is there any
destiny at all, and if there is should it be picked
green or allowed to ripen on the tree ? *
While Providence has been good to the
tobacco growers of New South Wales by giving
them climate and soil admirably adapted to the
cultivation of the leaf, it has left them to contend
with an unprofitable market. And a 1 though
much enthusiasm has been shown by politicians of
the kind who want to settle the people on the land,
nothing appears to have been really done, except
by Mcßurney, Wallis, and Co., who lately sent a
trial shipment of N.S.W. leaf to Canada by the
Arawa. The Hon. Mackenzie Bowell will, it is
stated, obtain an expert’s opinion on the leaf. If
the experiment proves successful, tobacco growers
will have reason to be grateful to the enterprising
firm that makes the venture.
M'Burney, Wallis & Co.’s Report.
Wool.—At our usual weekly sale ((he seventh of the
season) held yesterday at the Wool Exchange the
attendance of buyers was again very large and competi-
tion for all descriptions extremely vigorous; indeed,
biddings were so spirited that it was difficult for the
auctioneer to pick out the highest bidder. Bellies, pieces
and locks continue to sell well, as also “star lots." Our
catalogue comprised wool from the northern, southern,
and western districts, and as there were few high reserves
to hamper the sale, we were enabled to sell nearly every
line, only four lots being passed in at figures which will
no doubt result in business being done. Amongst the
most important lines of greasy sold were the following:
WA/Green Wood, from Murrumbateman, at 7id ; D from

from Yass, AA/West Blowering from Tumut, DE/R from

Sydney, 15th December, 1893.
The "Antipodean.”
The second issue of the Antipodean is a lot better
than the first, and the illustrations by Frank
Mahony, Percy Spence, and B. E. Minns are
far above the average. R. L. Stevenson’s
blank verse, “To My Old Familiars,” atones for
eight cruel pages of “gossip" about himself and
his partner, Lloyd Osbourne, by J. Tighe Ryan,
which, written about any two other mortals, would
not be so wearisome. Mr. Ryan is ■ a soulless
gentleman who ignores the fact that Robert Louis
Stevenson has been sitting, with his wild hair and
his naked feet, on the chest of the Australian
public for some years past, and that this country
would celebrate with champagne and all kinds of
drink the news that Samoa was cut off from com-
munication with the outside world for 10 years to
come. A cleverly-written sketch, “An Aus-
tralian Pantomime,” is marred by an unquot-
able verse of a high-kicking dance-song.
“The Banjo’s" “ Geebung Polo Club" has its
humour intensified by Mahony’s splendidly-drawn
horsey pictures. Rolf Boldrewood’s story, “Five
Men’s Lives For a Horse ’’ has but one thing to
commend it—the author’s portrait. The tale
itself is by no means worthy of the author of
“Robbery Under Arms”—being merely the
judicial warning of a police-magistrate that the
wages of duffing a mare are death or Darlinghursb
Gaol. Charles Lilley, in a dozen leaden pages,
talks about Maoriland—and, of course, drags in
that high-flavoured Maori maiden, Hinemoa, by
the hair through the biggest part of a weary page
(The Bulletin wants to know if Hinemoa isn’t
entitled to a rest). The article also gives the
portrait of a Moderator of the Presbyterian
Assembly, and alleges it to be a “Maori Chief
in Full Dress.” Lilian Blair’s “ Dick’s Letter”
is an atonement for her “Solita” of last
year’s Antipodean, and Nat Gould’s “Racing
in Australia’’ is readable enough. All too
short is Pierce Fletcher’s “ Struggle for
Supremacy," depicting the tree life of a North
Queensland jungle. “ Types of Australian
Beauty,” shewn on the page opposite Francis
Kenna’s sombre poem of “ A Wayfarer ” and fill-
ing the two succeeding pages, must have been so
placed designedly to prepare the reader for the
depressing “ Gossip About Robert Louis Steven-
son” before-mentioned, winch follows. Edward
Bevan contributes an instructive article on “ Art
at the Antipodes.” In his “ Tale of the Western
Desert,” Ernest Favenc is at his best, and the ex-
citing story is worth reading, if only to make one
feel how good it is to be alive and able to take a drink
while other people are buried alive in the burning
Saharas which lie out on the sunset track. An
attempted humorous story about pigs is worthy
of a place in London Punch— let it pass in
silence. “Ivy,” a bigamous story by W. O.
Hodgkinson, is also humorous and much better —
by reason of its brevity. Winifred Scott’s
“Taming of Kitty Brent,” a bush tragedy told in
forceful language, has a murderer who hides the
bones of his victim “in a hole under the old bark
roof.” Generally writers of bush murders drag
in a hollow log, where the remains are discovered
by a bushman named Jim, who sees a dingo
emerging, chawing a thigh-bone, so the old bark
roof is distinctly refreshing. “Widderin,” a
bushranging episode from “Geoffrey Hamlyn,”
told in verse by John Farrell, carries one with it
to the finish :
Just a leaf from the large-hearted tale of magnifloent
years that are done
Warm with manhood and splendid with hope, and ful-
filled of the trees and the sun—
Just a shadow of terror and crime, black as ever was
written or told.
Thrown across the broad light of true friendships, and
loves that were purer than gold.
Tho intelligent comp, was more than
usually to-the-poinb when he asserted (as he did
recently in an English newspaper) that “ the ship-
owners of Hell have refused the demands of the
strikers.”

The great carnival and sale now being
held by Messrs, J. F. Collins and Co., at their
extensive premises, 235, 237, and 23'.) Bitt-street,
Sydney, is the sight of this festive season. To
attempt to enumerate even a few of the extra-
ordinary bargains in ladies’ and men’s and chil-
dren’s clothing would be a hopeless task for any
sane human being. Still more hopeless would it
be to endeavour to describe the endless variety of
style, pattern, and finish of the wealth of goods so
profusely displayed in this great emporium. One
line of the Messrs. Collins’ business is, however,
especially worthy of mention, inasmuch as it is
the specialty which, more than any other, has
made them famous—suits to order from 555., and
trousers from 12s, (sd.
In these days of scepticism and medical
fraud the advent <>f new remedies for alleviating
or curing tho bodily ills of humanity is received
by most people with perfectly justifiable doubt,
and only those remedies that stand the crucial
test of their alleged virtues by suffering people
survive the ordeal. Tike, for instance, rheumat-
ism. How many thousands of wildly-craeked-np
specifics have been palmed off upon those who
writhe and groan in its excruciating tortures—
and how few have proved their efficacy. The
remedy known as “Thulinius,” however, seems
to stand higher than the average. Some of the
cures reported are sufficiently remarkable, and the
testimonials from Sydney people forwarded to the
proprietor are worth reading.
Within the last two months something
like 23 books have been published in London about
Matabeleland or Mashonaland, written by all
sorts and conditions of men and women on all
sorts of subjects, from Lobengula’s policy to the
pattern of Cecil Rhodes’ socks. Unless an earth-
quake or the cholera or some such Providential
interposition arises and wipes the place out of
existence, the name of Matabeleland will prove as
great a burden upon humanity as that of Robert L.
Stevenson.
Said Julian Salomons, in arguing a case in
Sydney Banco last term :—“ A fire-proof safe, y’r
Honors, see, is a safe of the kind sold as fire-
proof.” “But a fire-proof safe must be a safe
that is proof against fire,” interrupted one of their
Honors. “Oh no, no, your Honor,” replied the
Q. 0., “there is no kind of safe that is proof
against fire—as men will find out when the end
of the world comes.”
The cataleptic town of Cardwell (N.Q.)
was actually on the drunk once in its history, A
surveyor’s party had come down from - the
Tally River in a cutter; and owing to this unex-
pected break in the monotony the whole town was
violently inebriated. The surveyor’s men had to
start back —some 30 miles—on the midnight tide.
They got aboard per dingy, roaring drunk; and
clapped on every rag the cutter had, though it
was blowing fresh. One man took the tiller; the
rest went to sleep, until they were suddenly
awakened by hearing the helmsman yelling out a
song. He had been at the rum-keg. By this time
it was blowing hard. “Where are we, Jack?”
“ Oh, close up. Ain’t she just travelling 1 ” Then
they went to sleep again. When they next woke,
the first object that arrested their vision was the
Cardwell publican, standing on his verandah and
rubbing his eyes, because the cutter was high and
dry on the mud in exactly the same spot where it
had been at high tide. They had been sailing all
night, but had forgotten to take up the kellick.
Dear Bulletin, —My word, it just was hot,
and the ex-Samoan and I didn’t know what to do
to cool ourselves. “Ah ! I’ve an idea,” said he;
“I’ll show you how we do it where I last came
from. Let’s go and buy two watermelons.”
“Two?” “Yes, two; and the two largest we
can get.” We went, we bought, and we struggled
onward, sweating and praying. “Come to my
cellar.” We kummed. “Strip.” “Stark?”
“ Matter of taste; I’ll keep my flannel on
myself.” I did the same. Then the Samoan
genius produced a huge piratical cutlass, with
which he halved each water-melon. “litre's a
dish for you,”said he; “here’s one for me. Now,
scoop out the meat, and do as I do.” He scooped
out all the fruit of both his halves, and then—
sat down in one half of the shell, and put the
other half on his head.” I did the same. We
ate all the “meat ” between smokes. Then “the
sun went down and all the land was dark.”— The
Dipsomaniac.

The Ophir of Charles du Breil.
Fourteen years ago Charles dußreil, Marquis
de Ray, whose death was reported last week, was in
the zenith of success and popularity as the organ-
iser of a colonising movement that was to create a
New France in Oceania, and Frenchmen went as
mad over him as they did 10 years later over
Boulanger. And as the military charlatan’s
schemes grew, inflated, and burst disastrously,
so did those of the enthusiastic Charles. Yet,
paradoxical as it sounds, he was an honest
swindler. He believed in his impossible scheme,
and when it fell flat with the dull and sickening
thud of a gone-bung Australian financial institu-
tion, no one lost more money over it than he did.
John Law, with his Mississippi venture, and
Blount, with his youth Sea Bubble, were mere
ordinary swindlers. Du Breil honestly believed
in his great fraud, and, like prancing Boulanger,
saw a dictatorship looming ahead for him —in the
palm-groves of Oceania.
• • •
The prospectus of Nouvelle France came
out in 1879. Land had been bought (honestly
enough) in New Ireland, and elaborate plans were
prepared of the new city with its extensive wharf
frontages, and public buildings, and parks and
promenades, together with supplementary plans
of the suburbs, where every settler was to live in
a gorgeous bungalow, with green blinds and tes-
sellated floors, while native servants in abundance
hung around to clean his boots and mix iced
drinks. The plan of the city was dotted over
with big cathedrals. This pleased the priests and
the pious old maids, who subscribed largely for
shares. And gold and other valuable commodities
were indicated in the prospectus.
* * •
The climate was described as “delight-
fully cool and somewhat moist, and specially suit-
able for the cultivation of grapes and figs.” The
natives were “amiable and hospitable,’’and further-
more were yearning to be made into good
Catholics. Seventeen priests immediately volun-
teered to go and show them how to do it, and the
clergy took up more shares. In a few months 2000
persons had enrolled themselves as colonists of
Nouvelle France and were ready to start. From
Barcelona and other Spanish ports came hundreds
of the better class of peasantry, all intent on
settling on each family’s purchase of 20 hectares
(at so many hundred francs per hectare), and
Italian emigrants tumbled over one another in
their eagerness to buy shares and get away in the
first ship, to a country where they could have
four square meals a day, and where “ wine-
making would be a staple industry.” (Gin, at a
dollar a bottle, is the only liquor known to the
native and white nobility in New Ireland). And
then the clergy kept booming up Charles’s fad to
the best of their ability, took more shares,
appointed a bishop in part thus, and looked
eagerly forward to sharing in the profits.
# # *
The French contingent (recruited prin-
cipally from Marseilles and Toulon) consisted of
about as shady a lot of people as ever kept out of
a gaol. The first lot sailed in the barque
Chandernagore, and on their way to their
El Dorado put in at Sydney. The captain of the
ship was M'Laehlan, the commander of the
colonists M. de Villacroix, both men of deter-
mination and nerve, and able to cope with the
ruffianly element on board which sought to raise a
mutiny and make things rough generally. After
a few weeks’ stay in Sydney, during which time
the daily press grew black in the face endeavour-
ing to prove the expedition a French piratical
excursion, bent on annexing Australia and taking
away Parkes, or bombarding Pinchgut or some-
thing, the Ohandernagore, with the flag of Liberia
at her gaff, and 80 colonists filled with claret and
enthusiasm on deck, sailed away to El Dorado.
* * *
M‘Lachlan, the captain of the Chander-
nagore, soon found he had a large contract on
hand. But he and Villacroix were grim cus-
tomers, and when the ruffianly element on
board tried to run the expedition and the Chan-
dernagore on their own ideas they got badly left.
Thirty-nine days after leaving Sydney 12 of the
colonists elected to land at the Lachlan Islands
(lat, 9° S., 153° W. Ion.) instead of going on to
New Ireland. The island looked very nice, the
coloured ladies were very agreeable in their
manners, and they had plenty of claret—why go
farther. This was their El Dorado, so they
stopped. Some of them died there of fever, and
some gob eaten.
* * *
A few weeks more and they reached the
site of Du Breil’s great city at Port Breton, on
the south end of New Ireland—with the exception
of the dismal Falklands, probably the most dank,
sodden, squashy and muddy region on earth. The
place looked very nice, however, in a transient
gleam of yellow sunshine, and the precipitous
sides of the mountains were clothed with tropical
verdure. Some claret was drunk and many cheers
given. Then it commenced to rain furiously for
40 days and 40 nights. Sometimes the deluge would
stop for a quarter of an hour, and then the
colonists of New France would bolt on shore to
select their farms, but before any pegs were
driven in another few thousand tons of water
would fall and send them back to the ship to
drink more claret. So they got tired of it at last
and asked the captain to look out for another El
Dorado where it wasn’t quite so wet. He took them
to Likiliki Bay, near Cape St. George. Here
they commenced to clear “ the wondrously fertile
land ” (about 3900 trees to the acre—leaving out
vines, &c.), and drink more claret. (The history
of the expedition was a wild orgie of claret from
beginning to end.) At this place the rain wasn’t
so bad—only about 6in. per diem—and, as the
Marquis had said the climate was humid, they
didn’t grumble any more.
# • #
The steamer Genii followed with more
people, principally women, and then a large
barque—the Marquis de Ray—came in loaded
with claret and cotton-ginning machinery, and
other material for founding a colony and convert-
ing the heathen. She was made into a storeship
and became the scene of much hilarity and beauty,
the latter imported from the streets of Marseilles
and Toulon. Meantime, every day one or several
died of fever. A few months of this, and the
expedition burst up, and, as everyone had both
legs swelled to a stupendous size through taking
too much quinine, everything came to a standstill.
Then Tom Farrell, an English trader at Mioko,
on the Duke of York Island, appeared in time to
save the colony from making itself into a huge
cemetery, and arranged to give the survivors
passages to Sydney. The whole lot fell on his
neck, and kissed him, and wept profusely down
his back
Justhere another steamer arrived, with 600
more colonists—principally Spaniards from Barce-
lona —all anxious to get into their bungalows and
other varieties of country palaces. She was the
Nouvelle Bretagne, and hardly had she dropped
anchor before a Spanish cruiser—the La Gaspe—
came, and straightway arrested the captain of the
Nouvelle Bretagne for some mysterious outrage
committed on the voyage out, and took him away
to Manilla. And suddenly it occurred to the
passengers that they would go back to Spain and
abjure their sodden, ghost-haunted El Dorado; so
without further delay the Nouvelle Bretagne
followed in the wake of the La Gaspe; leaving
the Frenchmen and Italians to take free board
and lodging with Tom Farrell, at Mioko.
* * *
Placing Capt. Fred. Boore in charge of
the Genii (Rabardy, the French skipper, had
died), Farrell sent his troublesome boarders away
to Sydney. The ancient steamer arrived safely in
Port Jackson and the Government of New South
Wales made provision for the emigrants; many
of whom are now doing well in the Clarence and
Richmond River districts as farmers. The
Marquis was tried for obtaining money under
false pretences, sentenced to four years’ imprison-
ment, and fined 30,000 francs. Certainly he was
an adventurer—because the enterprise burst up.
Had it succeeded, he would have been called
a philanthropist. And but for the claret and the
dancing and the hilariousness of everybody con-
cerned it should have succeeded.
MIOKO.
The recent Salvation Army prosecutions
in Maoriland furnished a glorious “ad. ” for the
big-drum brigade. Two officers, a colonel and a
majah, having maddened the people of Milton by
blowing their vociferous wind instrument all over
the town for many days and nights, were at last
fined in a small amount, and as they declined to
pay and told the Law that it was a lost soul, or
words to that effect, they finished upin gaol. When
they came out a huge demonstration was worked
up at the Army’s “fortress” in Dunedin. Two
prison cells were erected on the platform, each
with an iron-grated window, a shelf with a bible,
a bench and other fittings. In the middle of the
service an alleged warder appeared dragging in
the colonel and the majah, in prison dress, and
locked one in each cell. When they endeavoured
to speak to him through the grating he sternly
ordered them to “shut up.” After a while he
brought the majah out by the collar, and allowed
him to address the audience, but in the midst of
his pathetic account of his sufferings the warder
re-arrested him and ran him in with violence. Then
he brought out the colonel with needless ferocity,
but presently, just as his oration was making good
progress the persecutor fell upon him also and ran
him in again, and ordered him to keep silence.
Next he brought out the majah a second time, but
in the middle of a hymn that sufferer was re-cap-
tured, and the colonel had another show. After
they had both been let out five or six times the
warder finally locked up the pair of them, and
presently he brought each of them a pannikin of
tea and a hunk of forbidding-looking bread, which
they ate with great difficulty in their cells. Fin-
ally a collection was taken up in one of the panni-
kins, and the proceedings closed, the colonel and
the majah, when last seen, being asleep, each on
the cold floor of his dungeon, while the warder
maintained a relentless attitude outside the door.
Vows.
[ FOR THE BULLETIN ].
Not to be bound by chains,
However golden;
Not to be vowed, or thralled,
In no way holden.
Not to be kept forever bound
To a word, that, meant when spoken,
May lose its meaning with the years,
Yet must live on unbroken.
Not to receive a vow
No man may pay thee,
Vowing to love till death,
Through death it may be.
Not to shut eyes and turn away
From the powers that loves dissever,
Close eyes to Time, be blind, and say,
This love shall last for ever.
Only the now we know,
Or dream we know it;
Others may reap the grain,
Though our hands sow it.
Future and past alike are not,
We may joy to-day, or sorrow,
May love as we two love, beloved,
And love no more to-morrow.
Let us go into love
With eyes unblinded,
Seeing life’s long beyond,
And death behind it;
Feel we our fierce immortal faith
But in silence sweet, not say it
In words, for what are we, beloved?
A day, an hour, may slay it.
Yet, oh my one beloved,
What vow could bind us
Closer than this one kiss?
The world behind us.
Starlight and moonlight in the east,
In the west a dull, red river,
And somewhere, God, to read our hearts,
And write on us “For Ever.”
Sydney.
M.L.M.
A recent visitor to La Grande Chartreuse
says that the youngest monk in residence is inor
than 50 years of age. In less than a-quarter of a
century the last holy father may be expected to
die out along with his Order, and the monastic
cordial manufactory will be a romance of the past.
They have been, generally speaking, a queer lot,
these concoctors of the famous green and golden
liquors. The stories told of their continence, the
alleged result of virtuous properties in chartreuse,
were simply moonshine. Guides point out a cer-
tain window of a holy cell from which a rope used
to be lowered in the gloaming, the said rope being
subsequently hauled up with a damsel attached.
At last the rope, worn out by exposure to weather
and the weight of the fair sex, broke. A girl’*
neck was also broken by the fall, but the guilty
father —who doubtless pulled pro bono monkico —
was smuggled away. Nobody got into trouble,
with the exception of the lady deceased. This
scandal spread abroad, however, and the old
fiction that a steady consumption of chartreuse
kept the tender passion under complete control
was thereupon abandoned. The Pope who decided
to wind up the Order evidently knew all about it.
