Even now it would appear that the story of the
moral Matthias Larkin (he used to go to early chapel every
morning) is not quite finished, and the utter bottomless-
m si of that saint hasn’t been fully appreciated even yet.
The latest allegation is that still another £40,000 dis-
crepancy has turned up in the accounts of his Building
Society. The principal item in this terrific shortage is
said to have disappeared in 1886 seven long
years ago. Yet the accounts of the society were
overhauled every year and duly certified as correct.
Then there was a stupendous special overhaul at the time
when Matthias fell from grace, and all manner of things
which the auditors had never noticed came to light.
Later on, when Matthias was safely in gaol, there was
some more overhaul, and a lot more robberies—on which
it was proposed to base a second prosecution—turned up.
And now, when it might reasonably be supposed that
every separate item in the accounts and every security
had been inspected unto seventy times seven, it ia
seriously alleged that a deficiency of £40,000 has alto-
gether escaped notice, and that the holy Matthias has
been at it again quite unobserved.
If this latest story is true, then it would seem
as if it were quite possible for any big financial institution
in Australia to have been robbed of all its capital and
reserve fund somewhere about the year 1860, and the
matter to have been accidentally overlooked until now.
And it would also seem that there is no faith in auditing
or in auditors, and that financial experts are a simple-
minded race and easily taken in. Also, the incident is
still another warning against the pious banker. When
anybody in a position of trust shows premonitory symp-
toms of piety, then it is time to sack him at an instant's
notice. Even a suspicion of hidden piety ought to be
sufficient re. son for a special investigation. Further,
shareholders should keep an eye of distrust on fat and
benevolent-looking officials of all kinds. The manager
who embezzles the cash, and the auditor who fails to
discover him doing it, are almost invariably Jovial
men with corporations. Auditors especially should be
gaunt, hungry-looking, morose men of a discontented and
quarrelsome turn of mind.
• * »
Greece, the historical land to which the
fatuous J. Bull has lent something like £20,000,000,
is now offering 10s. in the £ with no security for the pay-
ment of the composition. Australasia is pretty nearly the
only country which borrowed largely from Bull or any-
body else, and didn’t let the lender in at some time or
Other. Even some of the big states of Europe have com-
pounded with their creditors in the past, Austria, for
example, paying a humble Is 2d. in the £, said Is. 2d.
oeing in paper, while Russia panned out 6s. in the £, also
in paper. A good many of these states are now about
ready for another burst, and Bull, as the most enterpris-
ing lender, will be “ left ” again. And evefi this will be
only preparatory to the great day of uni\ ersal burst, when
Bull will be left altogether, and for the very last time.

8., in a letter, describes the horrors undergone by a party

“Samuel Heckscher, senr., Banker, Hamburg,"
continues to bombard Australia with his “ State lottery "
circulars. Wherefore it is obvious that the paternal
postal authorities, who refuse to deliver letters to George
Adams or J. J. Miller, are not refusing to despatch letters
(in printed envelopes) to the precious Hamburg “banker.”
An extract from lleckscher’s latest circular should serve
as a solemn warning to the unwary—“ The present offer
concerns the 305th lottery, in which from the 3rd to the
7th class of 103,000 numbers successively 48,400 must
win for certain. The winning chance is therefore a very
large one, as about one half of all the numbers will be
drawn with prizes.” The privilege of one whole ticket
can be obtained for 365. As far as one can understand,
then, the “sweep” amounts to £185,000, whilst the grand
total of the prize-money is £406,850. But there are so
many classes, and such a fearful complication of figures
upon the circular that the above estimate may be far
below the truth, Samuel Heckscher is probably the only
man on earth who understands all the gorgeous possi-
bilities of a 365. ticket.

The N.Q. way, Jones owes Smith an account
which ije can’t pay out of any present prospects. Smith
puts Jones into a hotel. Jones pays his supplying trades-
men hard cash for a time until he lias established a
“ three-months’ credit.” This enables him to pay off
Smith, and both feel happy. Then, when Bones, Dough,
Treacle and Co. begin to throw writs about—smash-oil!
in due legal form for Jones.

A Queensland medico, suspecting his gas-meter,
burned kerosene for a month. Bather larger bill than
usual. Then he turned on every gas-jet in his establish-
ment for a month. Smaller bill than thepreiious one.
He has now christened his meter “The Towzer.”

Dear Bulletin, — A Tasmanian sluicing co.
lately let tenders for five half-mile sections of race ai from
235. to 295. per chain. Contractor sublet at 9s. per chain
each, the “clearing” and “forming.” Three men
worked 10 hours a day, and did three chains in a week,
and that on the easiest “ forming ’’ on the race.—Tambo.
» * *
Editor Bulletin, —One of the most frequent
causes of contention in a shearing-shed is a difference of
opinion as to whether sheep are wet or dry. Frequently,
some shearers vote against their own opinions, in order to
further what they imagine to be the boss’s interests,
namely, to get the shearing over quickly. Since the
average shareholder in a pastoral company ordinarily
fails to sympathise with the grievances of any working
men, suppose that somebody Just noted down the numbers,
etc., of the bales in which damp wool was pressed, and
sent a list of these down to the insurance-companies
interested ?—Anolbdoul (N.S. W.).
* * *
Experts say that if the Australian meat-trade
with England is to succeed, chilled, and not frozen, meat
must be sent. There is a difference in the value of the
two of nearly 2d. per lb.
# * •
Any quantity of “gems” are being excavated
from Retreat Creek (Q.) “Sapphire Mines,” but where
they go to is a conundrum. The stones are paid for at
the rate of about 3d. per carat —uncut. After cutting they
are worth from 30s. to £3; and yet the owners aver that
they don’t pay.
* * *
Opals have been found at Kyabra, Cooper’s
Creek, a dozen miles distant from any previous workings.
One piece of stone brought down the scale at 401bs. It is
about 18in. long, 12in. wide, and 4in. thick. The opal
runs right through it, being at one side quite 3in. in
diameter, and at the other about lin.

It is estimated that 70 per cent, of the fires
which destroy or damage dwellings and shops in N.S.W.
are the work of the criminally-intentioned. There is no
class of offence, however, which Juries are so reluctant to
fasten upon an accused as the crime of arson.

During the recent American banking panic a
western cashier nailed to the door: “This bank has not
busted. It owes the people $36,000; the people owe it
$55,000 : it is the people who are busted. When they pay
we pay.”

Overend, Gurney and Co. has j'usfc been finally
wound up after a liquidation of 27 years. This was the
famous London bill-disoounting firm which smashed in
1866 for something like ten millions, and created a panio
and an aggregate bankruptcy of a hundred millions. The
present generation has forgotten the very name of
Overend, Gurney and Co., but the liquidator has been
drawing his salary ever since.
• # »
An official assignee of a back-blocks estate,
having unavailingly sued a debtor to the estate, lately
applied to make the debtor bankrupt. The debtor was,
in due course, adjudged bankrupt, and thereupon that
assignee was appointed official assignee in his estate
as well. This opens up great possibilities to an
energetic assignee. If, for instance, the official assignee
of a large banking or other corporation procured the
sequestration of ail debtors’ estates and became official
assignee in respect to them, he might secure profitable
occupation for the balance of his life.
Now that whaling from the port of Sydney
Is to be revived, the following (taken from the report of
the select committee on the Costa Rica Packet case) will
be of interest : On the first voyage of the Packet in 1887
(Malayan Archipelago), when she only carried three boats
and an inefficient crew, she took 76 whales during the
season (Ist Nov. to 10th Jan.); value of sperm oil, between
£14,000 and £15,000. For the voyage of 1891 she carried
six boats and a picked crew and had taken but two whales
at the commencement of the season, when the captain was
arrested hy the Dutchmen at Ternate. With the most
ordinary luck, she would have returned to Sydney with
oil to the value of over £20,000.
The select committee seems to have had amber-
gris on the brain, and the witnesses in the case told them
all that could be told on the matter. The captain, how-
ever, mentioned that he never counted on obtaining
ambergris, although a careful search was always made.
Grey ambergris is worth sometimes 1 10 an ounce. Capt.
Carpenter himself once got 501 b. He heard of one case
where 20001 b. was taken out of one whale (at £lO per 07.
this would be worth £320,0001),
Particulars of the right-whaling cruise of the
Costa Rica in 1839 elicited the information that the price
of baleen (whale-bone) varies from £2BOO to £3200 per
ton. The smallest quantity that Capt. Carpenter ever
took from a whale was 7001 b., the largest 32001 b. The oil
of the right-whale, however, is worth but half that of the
sperm whale, which is now quoted at £5O to £54 a ton.
At the Otis ore crushing works, at South Mel-
bourne (Vic.), the mills and concentrators have been
working full time during the past two months. There
has been a rush of orushings from Westralian claims,
among these being several tons of rich quartz from the
Golden Gate and Aushu Island claims, Murchison, which
averaged 4oz. 19dwt. per ton. The searching character
of the crushing as proved hy the infinitesimal difference
between the metal won and the assay value of the stone
has been such a recommendation of the Otis crusher that
the owners of the Golden Gate have ordered a complete
treating plant, consisting of a No. 3 crusher fitted with
copper-plates and a pair of Otis amalgamators. These
are now being gotten ready for shipment to the new field
where they will treat 250 tons a week. A similar plant
is in course of shipment to the Frances Reward Co., Mur-
chison, having been worked continuously at South Mel-
bourne for some time on Murchison stone to the satisfac-
tion of the purchasers. The Mount Magnet (VV.A.), which
recently purchased an Otis crushing and amalgamating
plant, has just completed its first crushing, and wires
that the return was 4300 z. from 120 tons.
Pnrnamoota, Barrier district (N.S.W.) once had
a flutter as a gold country, but although the Black Prince
was a fairly good thing, difficulties in the way of treat-
ment stopped general investment. Now a big area has
been taken up by Uriah Dudley, and a lot of the big Hill
men, and is to be worked for all it is worth. Mr. Dudley
conducted tests of 10 tons of stone from this properly—
known as the King—the machine used being the Otis
crusher and amalgamator. The finest screens were used
and excellent results obtained, almost all of the assay-
value being saved. Mr. W. B. Wilson has forwarded to
the Otis Co. a big quantity of very rich gold stone mined
from the Day Dawn Co., Cue (W.A.). Westralia coming
as the last of the reefers gains the experience of the
older provinces which have burned their fingers over in-
efficient machinery. The Otis crusher and the Otis
amalgamators came into favour at the right time-
cautious managers in Maoriland and Westralia purchased
baby plants to start with. The baby plants have done
more than their work, with the result that numerous are
the “ repeat ” orders received by the Co. from the people
who Justify their first Judgment by their most recent
action. Gippsland, by the way, has got hold of something
big. The Otis Co. crushed 6 tons of Gippsland ore,
assaying over lOoz. gold and 350 z. silver per ton.

The Subversion of
Discipline.
( FOR THE BULLETIN.)
I considered my appointment to the Public
School at Smiley’s Lagoon the first step in a
career that would at least end in an inspectorship,
for I was a born teacher. I would have taken a
class in Inferno with the certain conviction of
becoming head-master.
Teach? I could teach anything. I had trained
a dog and other pet animals to a perfection almost
supernatural. I had studied every “Art of
Teaching” and “School of Management” from
cover to cover.
I had formed theories of ray own. I had planned
a system which, if carried out, would make a
school a model of discipline.
The whereabouts of Smiley’s Lagoon was a
matter of indifference—at most, I would be there
only 12 months. After the first examination, I
would be allowed to sit for exam.—l would be
promoted—next year, my skill-mark would be
good enough for another grade—l would pass—
obtain a first-class school, and become, by sheer
force of merit, an inspector. It was so simple, I
laughed at the thought of being daunted by
Smiley’s Lagoon.
The weekly Royal Mail dropped me, my dog,
and my carpet-bag, on a hot, dusty road, from
which branched a bridle-track. Taking advantage
of the halt, the driver tied up a broken trace with
hair pulled from his poor horse’s tail, and said :
“The last teacher boarded with Regan’s, he
did—down the track, a matter of five mile away,
maybe. I s’pose they’ll take you in, too. S’long.’’
I reached a bark humpy, and, assuming it to be
Regan’s, “ Good evening,” I said.
‘ ‘ Day ! ”
“ I’m the new teacher—my name’s Reed.”
“ Hum ! ”
“I understand that my predecessor boarded
here, and I’ll be obliged if you will accommodate
me for a few days, till I have a look round.”
“ If you stops here, you boards here for always—
seventeen bob a week and no second table, under-
stand. Sling your swag in there. Tommy sleeps
there, but he won’t mind you.”
The accommodation was simply swinish. I
sickened in anticipation. But, after all, it was
only a probation. I would work—work—work. I
would wrap myself up in my ambition. I would
cloak myself in the armour of indomitable will,
and, in the certainty of success, become con-
temptuous of trivialities.
As I walked to school, I overtook three girls—
apparently about 18.
“Surely,” I thought, “they don’t go to
school ? ”
But, yes; each carried the inevitable lunch-bag.
My theory as regards discipline prevented any-
thing more than a “ Good morning ” as I passed.
But, Heavens! what a face!—an orchid among
cabbages ! The sweetest and prettiest and most
innocent-looking face I had ever seen, even in
pictures. After all, my lot was not so bad.
“ Ain’t he a goat!”
Surely they didn’t mean me ?
“ Hush, Ju, he’ll hear you.”
“ I don’t care. Look at his legs—not bandy, I
know.”
Shade of Socrates ! They were speaking of me.
Me —for my legs are a trifle—only a trifle—bowed.
I determined to wait until I had ascertained how
the school had been conducted by my predecessor,
before attempting any innovation.
Discipline ? They tumbled over one another —
they stamped—laughed—talked; and foremost in
everything was Julia O'Neill —the innocent-faced
girl I had passed on the road.
A radical change was necessary before any good
results could follow. I had occasion to reprimand
her some days later. She pouted and sulked, and
at the end of the lesson—letter-writing—her slate
was blank.
I ordered her to stay in and write it. Three
minutes after she handed it up :
Dear Nora, —I am kept in to write a letter. I don’t like
the new teacher a bit. He ain’t half as nice as Mr.
Kennedy. I don’t think he can say “ 800 1” No more,
from your loving Julia.
I was thunderstricken. I her to write out
“ Impertinence !” five thousand times.
She folded her- arms demurely, and sat looking
at me with a twinkle in her eyes.
I kept her in each day for a week. She only
laughed. I made her stand on a chair; and, on
referring to the shame, she said, “ Ain’t I
ashamed ? No. Look ! I only take threes.”
I put a dunce’s cap on her. She wore it with a
rakish air; and, winking at the class, asked per-
mission to make it more becoming 1 I wrote some
scathing remarks in her exercise book, and found
the leaf pinned on the gate I had to pass through.
Where were my theories about discipline ? How
could I expect obedience when I could not enforce
it? The whole school was demoralised. Had I
had the power, I would have rescinded the regula-
tions relating to corporal and, betray-
ing my manhood, flogged her.
One day, I glanced at her slate. She had
drawn a man standing on his head, his legs form-
ing a circle through which a dog was jumping.
The caricature struck home, and I struck her
across the knuckles with the cane in my hand. A
red weal rose, but she did not wince. She just
said, quietly, “I’ll report you for that 1”
I was beaten. There was nothing but expulsion
—an admission of defeat.
I told her she would not be admitted next day;
and, after school, sat down, humiliated and sad-
dened, to report the matter to the Inspector.
I was startled by a knock and she walked in.
“Well?”
“ Please, don’t expel me.”
I looked at her in astonishment. After all, she
was not icholly bad.
“I can’t help it, Julia, You have ruined the
whole school. It is my last resource.”
I felt glad that she had been touched by the
disgrace, and made some remarks about it.
/‘Disgrace be bothered ! I don’t care—only I
like coming to school.”
“ I don’t understand you a bit, Julia,” I said ;
“ You like it; you like it; still, you make all
this trouble! Were you the same with Mr.
.Kennedy?” "" :
“ML Kennedy! Poof! I’d rather be caned
by you than —than, oh ! expd me, if you like. I
don’t care.”
I hesitated. A victory would do so much
towards regaining my lost authority. Could I
trust her ? It was worth while trying, anyway.
“ Well, Julia,” I said ; “ I’ll give you another
chance. You have caused me a lot of anxiety.”
I laid my hand on her shoulder.
“Let us try if we cannot like one another a
little better.”
She dashed my hand away, and her eyes flashed.
*‘Li I< you? Why, I hate you! Don’t touch
me. 1 hate you with every breath in my body.
Don’t touch me, or I’ll kill you 1”
She laughed hysterically; and, thinking she
was about to fall, I put out my arm. She sprang
aside, and, seizing a bottle of red ink from the
table, threw it at me with all her force. It struck
me on the chest; and the red ink streamed down
my shirt-front and splashed my face.
“ What does this mean ?”
We had been too excited to notice the approach
of Mrs. Regan, chief scandal-monger, until she
stood at the door, and saw—Julia, with dis-
hevelled hair, flushed face, and heaving bosom ; I,
apparently covered with blood, holding her wrist !
“ What does this mean ? ”
“ Oh, he wanted to -to — Oh, take me home,
take me home ! ”
A week later, I received an intimation suspend-
ing me from duty, pending an investigation. Two
weeks later, the inspector tui’ned up. I stated the
case, and he asked for a private interview with
Julia.
After it was over, he said to me—“ You have
had a narrow escape. I congratulate you. The
girl corroborates your story; but it is fortunate
for you.”
“ But why did she throw the bottle at me? ”
“ Because she loves you.”
PONTE.
MY WATCH AND OTHER WATCHES.
One evening last summer I had some writing to
do at my house. The weather was very hot, and
I took off my coat and waistcoat. My new gold
watch (that I had paid .£3O for) I laid before me
on the table, where I could keep an eye on it. So
far so good. I wrote on, and papers accumulated on
the table, one of them covering my watch. Reach-
ing out for something I wanted, my arm
accidentally swept it to the floor. Oh, heavens,
what luck ! I picked it up —tenderly as one picks
up a child who has had a bad tumble. Was it
still running ? Yes, faintly ; but as I put it to my
ear it ticked a few times, feebly and slowly, and
then stopped—a dead watch. A minute ago it
was a vital thing—now merely a lot of motionless
wheels in a case. What was broken ? I couldn’t
say. The watchmaker must repair it and return
it, with his bill. So much for my stupid care-
lessness.
Yet nobody is so poor as not to carry a more
valuable watch than that; one that will run many
years without winding. But when it stops, ah !
then, who is able to set it going again ?
Speaking about the one he owns, Mr. Geo. W.
Burton, of Kirton Holme, Boston, says : “My
heart fluttered in a way to alarm me. Sometimes
it was so bad I fancied I could hear it stop heat-
ing.”
What ailed Mr. Burton’s heart ? Perhaps his
letter will help us to find out. He says : —“ln
October, 1887, I began to feel very weary and
languid. I had a bad taste in the mouth, and in
the morning my teeth and gums were covered with
a thick bloody slime. My appetite failed, and
after eating I had great pain in the chest and
stomach. All the time I had a craving for food,
but dare not take solids. It seemed sometimes
that my head would burst with pain, and I was
so dizzy I could hardly see. After a while a cough
set in, and I spat up great quantities of phlegm.
Later on my breathing became very bad, and I
would break out into a cold sweat. I kept on
growing weaker until it was all I could do to get
about, and in this condition I continued for four
years. During this time I consulted doctors and
used all the different medicines I heard of, but
none of them did any good.”
Now, let’s think a minute. Mr. Burton says
his heart fluttered and palpitated, he had a hack-
ing cough, and difficulty in breathing—three
frightful things. A man might die of any one of
them, as we all know. Yet he recovered from all
of them— and all at the same time. He says
“In February, 1891, I heard of what Mother
Seigel’s Curative Syrup had done in similar cases,
and I determined tojry it, and got a bottle from
Messrs. Grimble and Kent, chemists, Boston.
The first few doses gave relief, and by continuing
to use it in a short time I was perfectly cured. I
make this statement in order that others may
know where to look for a remedy in an illness like
mine” (Signed) “ Geo. W. Burton.”
We rejoice at his restoration to health, but
what, after all, ailed him ? Did he have three
diseases—viz., hearl complaint, consumption, and
asthma? And, if so, how on earth could Mother
Seigel’s Curative Syrup have cured them—each
affecting different organs ? The answer is, he had
but one disease, indigestion and dyspepsia, of
which the feeble heart, the irritated throat, and
the burdened lungs were tell-tales and symptoms.
The poisoned blood —filled with deadly acids from
the stomach —half paralysed the nerves and thus
disordered the heart’s action ; it also infected the
delicate membrane lining of the lungs and air
passages, producing asthma and the cough that
seemed to threaten consumption. One disease,
many misleading symptoms—that is the truth ;
deluding physicians, and frightening patients into
thinking there is no hope.
When life’s timepiece runs down no power on
earth can wind it up again, but Mr. Burton’s case,
and thousands more, prove that it is often good
for many a year after you thought the works
would soon be motionless in the case.

The Fallacies of Hilliard.
( FOR THE BULLETIN.)
With clenched hand grasping the two
letters —the one that sank his last hope of saving
his plantation and the other that blasted his trust
in human nature—Hilliard, the planter of Nairai
Viwa, walked with quick, firm step to his house,
and sat down to think awhile. The great cotton
“burst-up” had ruined most men in Fiji, and
although long delayed in his case the blow had
crushed him utterly.
An angry flush tinged his set features for a few
seconds as he re-read the curt, almost savage
denial, by his father of the “couple of thousand ”
asked for. “A fool to resign his commission in
the Service and go into a thing he knew nothing
about, merely to humour the fantastic whim of a
woman of fashion who will, no doubt, now sheer
very clear of your wrecked fortunes.”
Ten minutes previously when Hilliard, who had
thought his father would never see him go under
for the sake of a couple of thou., had read these
lines he had smiled, even with the despair of
broken fortune at his heart, as he looked at the
other letter yet unopened.
Kitty, at least, would stick to him. He was not
a maudlin sentimentalist, but the memory of her
farewell kisses was yet strong with him ; and his
past experiences of woman’s weaknesses and his
own strength justified him in thinking that in this
one woman he had found his pearl of great price.
Then he read the letter; and as he read the
tappa mallets at work in the Fijian houses hard
by seemed to thump in unison with the dull beats
of his heart as he stared at the correctly-
worded and conventionally-expressed lines that
mocked at his fond imaginings of but a few
breaths back.
• # *
Jimmy, the curly-headed half-caste who
had brought him his letters from Levuka, had
followed in his steps and was sitting, hat in hand,
on the sofa before him when Hilliard raised his
face. The fixed pallor had left his bronzed cheeks.
For an instant the face of another man had passed
before him —Lamington, his one-time fellow-
officer, whom everyone but Hilliard himselt
looked upon as being first “ in the running ” with
the woman who had pledged herself to him. But,
then, Lamington himself had told him that she
had refused him, heir to a big fortune as he was,
and they had shaken hands, and Lamington had
wished him luck in his honest, good-natured
fashion. “Perhaps," and here the dark flush
mantled his forehead, “ he’s tried again and she’s
slung me. Audi . . . * What a damnably
unpleasant and quick intuition of women’s ways
my old dad has ! I always wondered why such a
fiery devil as he was married such a milk-and-
water creature as my good mother. By , I
begin to think he went on safe lines, and I on a
fallacy 1”
• # •
The stolid face of Jimmy recalled him to
the present. He must give up the plantation and
take a berth of some sort. From the sideboard he
took a flask of liquor and poured out two big
drinks.
“ Here, Jimmy, my boy. This is the last drink
you’ll get on Nairai Yiwa. I’m burst up, cleaned
out, dead broke, and going to Hell generally.”
Jimmy grunted and held out his brown hand
for the grog. “Yes? I s’pose you’ll go to
Levuka first? I’ll give you a passage in the
cutter.”
Hilliard laughed with mingled bitterness and
sarcasm. “Right, Jimmy. Levuka is much like
the other place, and I’ll get experience there, if I
don’t get a billet.”
“ Here’s luck to you, sir, wherever you go,’’and
Jimmy’s thick lips glued themselves lovingly to
the glass.
Hilliard drank his off quietly, only muttering to
himself, “Here’s goodbye to the fallacies of
hope,” and then, being at bottom a man of sense
and quick resolution, he packed his traps and at
sunset went aboard the cutter. As they rippled
along with the first puffs of the land-breeze, he
glanced back but once at the lights of Nairai
Yiwa village that illumined the cutter’s wake,
and then, like a wise man, the hopes and dreams
of the past drifted astern too.
# # *
The laughing, merry-voiced native children
who, with speedy feet, ran to the house of IliSti,
the trader, to tell him that a visitor wa« ~ >ming
from the man-of-war, had gathered with ting
breath and hushed expectancy, at the door as the
figure of the naval officer turned a bend in the
path, his right hand clasped with a proud air of
proprietorship by that of the ten-year-old son of
Alberti the Chief.
Iliati, with a half-angry, half-pleased look, held
out his hand.
“ Lamington !”
“Hilliard ! old fellow. Why didn’t you come
on board ? Are all your old friends forgotten ?”
* « •
“ Pretty nearly, Lamington. Since I
came a cropper over that accursed cotton swindle
I’ve not had any inclination to meet anyone I
knew —especially anyone in the Service, but ”
and his voice rang honestly, “ I always wondered
whether you and I would ever meet again.”
“Hilliard,” and Lamington placed his hand on
the trader’s shoulder, “ I know all about it. And
look here, old man. I saw her only two months
ago—at her especial request. She sent for me to
talk about you.”
“ Ah ?” and the trader’s voice sounded coldly,
“ I thought, long ago, that she had reconsidered
her foolish decision of other days and had long
since become Mrs. Lamington. But it doesn’t
interest me, old fellow. Can you drink Fiji rum,
Lamington? Haven’t anything better to offer
you.”
“I’ll drink anything you’ve got, old fellow,
even liquid Tophet boiled down to a small half-
pint ; but I want you to listen to me first. I’ve
been a bit of a scoundrel to you, but, by God, old
man, I exchanged into the beastly old Petrel for
this cruise expresslj to find you and make a clean
breast of it. I promised her I would.”
“Confound it all, Lamington, don’t harrow
your feelings needlessly, and let us have the rum
and talk about anything else.”
“No. We won’t. Look here, Hilliard, it
sounds beastly low, but I must get it out. W
met again—at a ball in Sydney more than
two years ago. Some infernal chattering women
were talking a lot of rot about the planters in
Fiji having very pretty and privileged native
servants—and all that, you know. She fired up
and denied it, but came and asked me if it was
true, and I was mean enough not to give it a
straight denial. How the devil it happened I
can’t tell you, but we danced a deuce of a lot and
I lost my senses and asked her again, and she
said ‘Yes.’ Had she been any other woman but
Miss , I would have concluded that the soft
music and all that had dazed her. It does some-
times —lots of ’em ; makes the most virtuous wife
wish she could be a sinner and resume her normal
goodness next day. But Kitty is different.
And it was only that infernal twaddle caused
it and made her write you that letter. A
week hadn’t passed before she wrote to me and
told me how miserable she was. But I knew all
through she didn’t care a d—— about me. And
that’s the way it occurred, old man.”
Hilliard’s hand met his.” “ Say no more about
it, Lamington ; it’s a mea mate, as we say here—a
thing that is past.”
“But, good God, old fellow, you don’t under-
stand. She’s written ever so many times to you. No
one in Levuka knew where you had gone to; there’s
thousands of islands in the South Seas. And
this letter here,” he held it toward him, “ she
gave to me, and I promised her on my honour as
a man to effect an exchange into the Petrel and
find you.”
“Thanks, Lamington. You always were a
good fellow.” He laid the letter on the table
quietly and rose and got the rum.
• # •
A young native girl, with deep lustrous
eyes shining from a face of almost childish inno-
cence, had entered the door and stood with one
bare and softly-rounded arm clasped round the
neck of Alberti’s little son. Her lips parted in a
smile as Lamington, with a gasping cough, set
down his glass after drinking the potent spirit, and
she set her brows in mock ferocity at Hilliard
who drank his down like an old-time beach-
comber.
“ By Jove, Hilliard, what an astonishingly
pretty face ! She could give any New Orleans
creole points. Time you got out of this before
some of the Rotumah beauties make you forget
things; and, oh, by the way, I’m forgetting
things. Remember you are to come aboard and
dine with us to-night, and that you’re in indifferent
health, and that Captain , of Her Majesty’s
ship Petrel, is going to give you a passage to
Sydney.”
At an angry sign from Hilliard the girl dis-
appeared. Then he shook his head. “No,
Lamington. I appreciate your kindness, but
cannot accept it. I’ve been here two years now
and Alberti, the principal local chief, thinks no
end of me ; and he’s a deuced fine fellow and has
been as good as ten fathers to me. And I’ve
business matters to attend to as well.”
• • *
Lamington pressed him no further.
“Lucky devil,” he thought. “I suppose he’ll
clear out in the trading schooner to Sydney, next
week ; be there long before us any way, and I’ll
find them well over the first stage of married
infatuation when I see him next.”
Another hour’s chat of old times and old ship-
mates in the Challenger, and Lamington, with his
honest, clean-shaven face looking into the quiet,
impassive features of the ex-officer, had gripped
his hand and gone, and Hilliard went over to the
house of Alberti, the chief, to drink kava —and
see the old French priest. From there, an hour
afterward, he saw the cruiser with wet, shining
sides dip into the long roll of the ocean swell, as
with the smoke pouring from her yellow funnel
she was lost to sight rounding the point.
* * *
Said the son of Alberti to Lela, the
innocent-faced girl with the dancing, starlike
eyes and red lips, as they stood watching the last
curling rings of the steamer’s smoke —“ And so
that is why I knew much of what the papalagi
from the man-of-war said to your Iliati; Alberti,
my father, has taught me much of your man’s
tongue. And, look thou, Lela the Cunning,
Iliati hath a wife in his own country !”
“Pah !”—and she shook her long, wavy looks
composedly, and then plucked a scarlet hibiscus
flower to stick in front of one of her pretty little
ears—“ what does that matter to me, fathead ? I
am she here; and when Iliati goeth away to her
she will be me there. But he loveth me more
than any other on Rotumah, and hath told me
that where he goeth I shall go also. And who
knowetli but that if I have a son he may marry
me? Then shalt thou see such a wedding-feast as
only rich people give. And listen : ’Tis well to
starve thyself now, for it may be to-morrow, for
look ! fathead, there goeth the priest into thy
father’s house, and Iliati is already there.”
LOUIS B.
The Wagging World.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
“Good business,” murmured the news-
paper-proprietor as he stood at his private tele-
phone, in his pyjamas. “ Better send down Jones
to see if he can pick up anything fresh.”
“What’s the matter?” queried his wife,
sleepily.
“Only some murders, my dear,” answered the
newspaper-proprietor, crawling back into bed.
“ What a bother that they should ring you up,”
remarked his wife.
“Damn it!” said Jones, as he pulled up the
collar of his great-coat preparatory to a ten-mile
drive.
“ This ought to mean a sthripe fur me fur
certain,” pleasantly remarked the policeman (who
had captured the murderer) to the chemist
who was binding up his wounded finger.
“Why did I doit? Why did Ido it? I was
mad, mad !” said the murderer, as he tried to
scrape the dry blood from his hands on to the
wall.
“ Oh, my babies ! my poor babies,” wailed the
wretched mother as she bent over her murdered
little ones.
# « *
“Because the poor little darlings had kept
him awake for five nights, and he had to work
hard all day, as if that was any reason ! The brute
must be hanged,” shrieked the general public.
“I can nearly understand it,” said the man who
suffered from insomnia.
“Evidently insane,” said the doctor.
“ Guilty,” said the foreman of the jury.
“I concur with the verdict. . . . till you
are dead, and may the Lord have mercy on your
soul,” ended the Judge.
“ Remove the prisoner,” said the associate.
“The burthen the Lord has laid on me is a
heavy one,” wept the condemned man’s old
mother, as they led her from the court.
“Blast it! I suppose I’ll have to scrag’im,”
said the executioner (who was in receipt of a
regular salary).

Missing Word Competition--No. 9.
Any purchaser of The Bulletin who can
correctly supply the word missing from the
paragraph on the subjoined coupon—which
coupon is to be cut out and forwarded to
i he Bulletin, accompanied by one shilling,
will be paid the total amount received in
response to this advertisement.
Only one guess is allowed on each Coupon,
but competitors may send as many Coupons
as they please.
Coupons received to Wednesday, 27th
December, 1893.
The missing word is placed in a sealed
envelope in the custody of the manager of
the Bank of New Zealand, Sydney, and the
contents are known only to the manager of
this paper.
If the word is not named, the money will
be added to that of the succeeding com-
petition.
The sum of one shilling may be forwarded
by post-office order or stamps of any pro-
vince ; «hen tire latter are sent, a penny
to be added for exchange.

Your attention, please, to the Fashions which
bloom in the Spring.

the coming season. Ladies will find a refreshing delight
in a daily visit to FOY’S, where the glittering sprays of
fashionable gems from the continental artists of fashion are
being opened up with all speed
Great is the curiosity and joy of young and old, to
see and admire the spick-and-span novelties from Paris,
London, and elsewhere.

extraordinary colourings, are more varied than the wildest
flio-hts of imagination could conceive.
